THE RED PAGE.
JOSIAH-S DREAM
AND Josiah dreamed a dream, Josiah
Symon, of course (or, as he affronts us in
a long, majestic line on the cover of the
pamphlet—surely with some superfluity ,
of “honours” if not of honour
The Hon. Sir Josiah Symon, K.C.M.G., K.C.
Ah, dear knight! and to think that the subject of
his pamphlet should be plain
William Shakspere
in half the space, with no titular heralds and
no cometary train—plain W. S., and riever sure
how he was going to spell it ! — yes, that very
Shakespear who wrote that “ If I would but go to
hell for an eternal moment or so, I could be
knighted ” (this with our author’s own misprision
of quotation).
* .
The pamphlet—booklet; rattier —with the better
covering it deserves one might call it a book,
though brief, is Shakespeare at Home, by
The Hon. Sir Josiah Symon, K.C.M.G., K.C.
(On reflection, one might approve the precedent
and added honour thus cast upon the knightly
“Sir”: seen truly, it gives strength and assur-
ance, faith by justification,) Shakespear you have
heard of : glory of letters, cynosure of critics, end
and aim of Poverty Point, with the rest (remember
passim that noble scene in the noble drama of J.
Ccesar:
Cas. That you have wrong’d me, doth appear in this :
You have condemn’d and noted my associate
For piling up a bill for travelling exes ;
Wherein, my letter; praying on his side, . ~ \
Because I knew the facts, was slighted of.
Bru. You wrong’d to write in such a oftse., 1'
Cos'. In such a court as this, it is not meet
That every set of shelves should bear his comment.
Bru, Let me tell you, Cassius, you yourself
Are much condemn’d to.have an itching palm ;
And seven'telephones are far toO many
For undeservers.
Cas, lan itching palm ? • , y ■
You know that you the Minister speak this,
Or, by the gods, I ’d gaol you for contempt !
Bru. The name of Themis honours this corruption,
And chastisement doth therefore hide his head,
Cas. Chastisement!
Bru. Remember March, the ides of March remember,!
Did not our country bleed for union’s sake? • ■
And so on.
& at Home (Ad., Hussey & Gillingham; 2s.
6 d. ) extends a lecture delivered to Melb. .Shake-
spear Society by
The Hon. Sir Josiah Symon, K.C.M.G., K.C,
(We shall never weary of writing that royal row
of appellatives, sailing along like Cleopatra’s barge,
while “ a strange invisible perfume hits the sense
Of the adjacent” words. Oh, if a simple pieman had
met that Symon going to the fair, would not the
varlet’s knees have shook the pies?) . It is a per-
sonal, enthusiastic, uncritical fantasia on Shakes-
pear’s life and work, very well written : the
writer’s style is bright, strong and clear. Without
novelty, it skims lightly, agreeably over the four
facts-, the forty rumours, and the -forty thousand
- guesses and speculations that “ Shaksesperearare ”
spells nowadays in his casual, undetermined way.
It is the best we have seen among the little disquisi-
tions with which local Shakespear societies from
tifne to 1 time favour us. Three-fourths indeed is
imaginative fiction, quite futile : Josiah dreams
that Shakespear did this or that, and interprets his
dream pleasantly, vaguely. There are occasional
flaws : e.g., he should have quoted Tarquin (p.
109) correctly, and be should have abstained from
such nonsense as dubbing Tarquin’s specific speech
“the sad cry of all humanity” and giving it a
particular reference to Shakespear himself.
*
Nevertheless, one would not say the little book
was not worth while. In the intervals of strife, it
is good to see these politicians turning to the calmer
paths of letters : did not Griffith, C.J., terrify
Aganippe .with aversion of'Dante that seut the
poor nymph flying to the utmost parts of Beotia
(Q-). But Symon has a weak notion that an
Australian shrine should be set up at Stratford to
profit the Bard-mongers. That is bad : so is
N.S.W. S. Society’s idea of a local statue.
Surely, says a Sat, Review correspondent:
Surely the funds collected for a Shakespear memorial
in this country should be spent, not on constructing
some grotesque statue by an* incapable sculptor that
shall remain to insult good taste for all time, but in
founding some institution where mimes can once more
acquire the lost art, the grand method of declamation,
the magnificent power of speaking- blank verse in rythmic
and sonorous measure.
And surely, say we, any funds available in
Australia should be devoted to founding a local
theatre where intellectual drama can be staged, to
the profit of the people and the encouragement of
local writers and actors.
$
The occasional public utterances of two (at
fewest) of Sydney University professors—Messrs. Mac Ca-
l, lum and Anderson, are always worth hearing or
reading-; and they shine in strong contrast to the
frequent remarks of that windy and ungrammatical
personage G. H. Knibbs, who (fbr the , credit of
the Education Dept, that he doubtfully adorns) should
really be persuaded to stop talking in public. (This seems
hard language, hut not if one had to read those all-over-
the-shop reports.) Prof. MaeCallum referred the other day
to the benefit of studying modern history as correc-
tive to “ that tendency' to look on ourselves as
the roof and crown of things.” But ■we are: of
existing things, and in relation to- ourselves. Prof. Mac-
Caltum is the roof and crown -of all preceding
,‘MacCallums that have striven to make him what he is;
I not necessarily, indeed, in relation to the whole Mac-
ICallum edifice, but in relation to the only part of it that
j realiy matters to him, in relation to himself. To say that
j MaeCallum Mohr was a greater man is to miss the point :
lie is not greater at this vital moment that is
t concentrated in’ Prof. MaeCallum. We would agree
' with almost everything that Bolingbroke has said on
the efficacy of historical study; hut after all the study
- there remains the fact that Prof. MaeCallum is
alive now, and in a few years he will be dead
and mutton, and To-day and To-morrow are the
whole universe, for him. The argument applies equally
to a race or nation. Doubtless “greater” races have
lived than the race of N.S.W. land-agents: nevertheless,
the land-agent is quite right in realising that Now is
the fee-accepting time and Then isthe day of retribution (?)
There is difference only of points of view, but while we
see so many people doddering over the dead Past we
are pound to insist On the virtue of the living Present and
th u promise (that with the advice of the Past and the
labour of the Present we may make performance) of the
Future.
BORROWING BOOKS
THERE are three ways of obtaining books—
buying, stealing, and borrowing. The
buyer loses money, the stealer loses repu-
tation and sometimes liberty, the borrower
loses nothing except the lender’s friendship. The
difference between the last two classes is rather
subtle: both take the book, neither gives it back
willingly; but the thief, if pressed, will probably
deny his theft, while the borrower is always ready
to acknowledge his borrowing. It is generally
best, when trouble is brewing, to address your
victim voluntarily with some such phrase as-“It
was so good of you to lend nje that Book.”.
Borrowing books as a profession or as a means
of_ livelihood is justly condemned by the right-
minded ; it should rather be regarded as a sport
or as a hne art, according to its grade. The lowest,
and least intellectual form consists of entering a
friend's hoii'se during his absence, removing a book,
and leaving a message of insolent triuriiph to that
effect; this last act marks the transition- from a
felony to a . gentlemanly proceeding. The second
Stage represents a moral advance upon the first;
ethically it lies somewhere between highway rob-
bery and Rugby football. The;.-distinctive mark
is, that the owner is'present when the book .is
borrowed ;• the borrower announces- his intention
of borrowing the book, and wihs the race heaps
with- it in his pocket. The; variation of this
method, in which a third frierid is introduced to
hold the owner down while the loan is being com-
pleted, is of a lower grade, and can only be de-
fended as a joke.
In the third stage we pass from barbarism to
civilisation ; and moral institutions, which are the
mark of civilisation, are here present in the form
of certain presuppositions of politeness on either
side, which constitute the r.ules of -the game.-
B-ule I is. v that it is impolite to refuse to lend a
pbok. This would seem to put the game into the.
borrower’s hands; but Rule II restores the balance
by allowing-the lender, within limits, to say that
he has already promised the book to another
friend. Matters now seem at a deadlock, but
Rule 111 again modifies the situation: it says that
lies coming under Rule II must not be too glar-
ingly obvious. It is clear, then, that the borrower
must exercise a nice discretion. On the one hand,
the book must be valuable enough to be worth
borrowing; on the other hand, it must not-be so
valuable as to force the owner to invent a really
artistic lie, and so escape Rule 111. Where ncit
much is at stake he will probably refuse the effort
involved, and let the borrower win the game.
In this higher stage the game admits of several
interesting variations, such as the Sick Friend, the
Journey, and the Biter Bit, The Sick Friend., is
used either in attack or defence : thus the bor-
rower may say that his friend is ill and in need
of a certain book, and offer to take it to him ;
or the lender may refuse to lend the book on
the plea that he has already, promised it to
another sick friend ; in such a ease, however, the
lender must observe Rule 111 with care. - The
Journey variation* is purely offensive : the bor-
rower is leaving the lender’s house and borrows
the book to read in the train ; if the lender replies
that he has promised to lend it to another friend
to read in the, train, lie infringes Rule 111, . and
loses. The Biter Bit is one of the most interest-
ing of all the variations.: as the name implies, the
lender becomes a borrower ; lie has lent, let us sav,
a book (a) to a friend. He then goes to the
friend’s house, borrows a . second book (b), and
says, “ As I am here, I may as well take back that
book I lent you,” adding, “ for poor Jones,” if he
is also playing the Sick Friend.
- The above may serve to indicate some of the.
possibilities of this game. o‘n its merits as a form
of sport it is needless to dwell. Like all the higher
forms of sport, it does much to train our faculties,
and is an invaluable element in the education of
an Imperial Raci? ; at least, it teaches thorough! v
the two great-lessons of Enterprise and Diplomacy
(otherwise called lying and stealing), which form
the AB C of Imperial expansion. It is with a
just historical appreciation that the Book-bor-
rowers’ Club have placed in their vestibule the
busts of Ananias and Barabbas, engraved with, the
motto “ Honour among Borrowers.”
Book-borrowers on the whole are a prosperous
and contented race, and entertain a kindly.feeling
for one another, although their interests frequently
conflict. They are often scattered in this life-
some live near [Wahroonga], some in [Darling-
hurst]; but they will all be reunited later on.—
A.H.S., in Westminster Gazette.
—
Holman Hunt, now 78 years of age, is exhibiting
in London 'Hie Lady of Shaloft, a new painting praised
bv an anonymous critic for qualities of design and colour
absent in Hunt’s previous works. “It will rank as one of
the greatest achievements of British art.”
Dr. Head, a fellow of English Royal Society,
has made the interesting experiment of' dividin''- the
sensory nerves in his own arm and watching sensations
disappear. The nerves were then reunited bv stitching
and the process of recovery carefully watched dav bv day.
Head claims to have discovered that there are" two dis-
tinct sets of nerves to the skin ; one set conveying sensa-
tions of pain, iieat, and cold, th 6 second set carrvin»-
sensations of touch.

where possible. Information is wanted regarding the
nelia or nilyah, apparently a species of myall. Will some

Govt, depts. should save time, ink, and paper. The

THE COMPLETE SWIMMER
RALPH THOMAS, of 30 Narbonne Avenue,
Olaphara Common,‘London, who publishes
Swimming through Sampson Low (10a 1 . G d.
net) is a man, one is convinced, after
Izaak Walton’s own heart, and if he could regain
the simplicity and morality of the 16-17th century
he might be a writer after. Izaak’s honourable
manner. But the modern printer is too complex
for him, the modern publisher too knavish : and,
though he labours diligently toward his ideal, the
fair vision never is realised wholly. Still it is
lovable book that he gives us: a table-book,
too heavy for a bedybook, a book of’ 1
brave subaqueous things. One will tiever
read it all, but one will read it always, overhand
over. Called a bibliography of swimming it' is.
rather a swimmer’s bible, with revelation. doV.
trine, and commentary complete. Though one
looks vainly in the index for a reference, to the
“ record ’’ swim of Jbpah or the value bflargefishes
as life-savingappliancbs.,Jonah’s involuntary .sea-
dive took him,right “down to the bottoms of the
mountains,” whpre the breeds ‘were “wrapped'
about;” ;liis head.,; yet (wgth th,e) aid of a large
fish) hecame in . time to thh> surface.of
things." being apparently in po way distressed.
Arid if "Mr. Thomas made 'a.Jittle f excursus to-
prove that the practice-of prbphecy. is,'a fine
thing for the wind, One could add baleful local
illustrations (for what is poesy but prophecy
writ small and largely talked about?)
... J*
To be sure, the original (?) Thomas was a
doubter... and this Thomas, after examina-
tion of all the books about swimming that he
could find, is profoundly sceptical about the value
of legendary authorities. Comparatively few and
positively - evil have been the writers .about
swipiming; and they steal vr O l^hpw,
they steal from ’one another dr- from one, repro-
ducing .blunders and bad drivings from generation
to generation-. Accidentally, then; Swim Ming is a'
handbook of plagiarism. Incidentally, it is. a
text’-book of typography. Mr. Thomas'" would
swim away from disfiguring italics, from obtrusive
capitals, from superfluous marks of punctuation,
and “ I also object much to hyphens, which help
to blacken the page-.” “I desire what?l write to
be printed in the most effective, artistic, and un-
obtrusive manner, so that anyone will be able to
read without ever giving a thought to the mechan-
ism of the print.” And as far as modern
typefounder will permit- him, he presses toward
his mark : with the result that frequently one has
to give a thought to the mechanism of his thought.
Dexterous punctuation is a light to our head and a
lamp to-our path, and (for our taste) Mr. Thomas
doth detest too much. The plain page tends
to be barren : italics may blot; but capitals should
decorate, and do. “ Dr. N. O. B. Ody Smith,
LL.D.” is. a monstrosity that one would join in,
decapitating; yet... one can but hint how Mr.
Thomas’s “fads” provide a new field of .interest
for readers.
Here remark the best of him : he is individual:
he makes his own book ; and so many of us make
other people's, reducing literature to one dreary
common denomination. Thus is justified his
humble boast that every page has interest. It is
i the unhandy form, the • unworthy binding, that
depress us : the work should appear in larger
type and half-a-dozen small octavos, in leather,
that it might gossip like Montaigne : at present it
is cumbrous, and the print does not represent ade-
quately the author s English sidestroke.
Did we say that the book was all 'abortt swim-
ming ? It is all about books about swimming,
excerpted,and annotated. T-uere is theory and
discussion of practice (the trudgen stroke, for
example, often referred to' in these columns, is
fully accounted for). There are references to
several Australian books on swimming : one of
them, Charles Steedman’s Manual (Melb., 1867)
“is the best, and most complete work on swim-
ming written up to this date [1867 ?] and Aus-
tralians may well be proud of it' : though Steed-
man says thousands of them [English Australians]
have n ever been washed !
Several of his suggestions are worth repeating. One is
that coroners [no apostrophe] verdicts should be changed
into.“death caused by a most important feature in the
physical education of the victim having been completely
ignored, namely, “the art of swimming.”’ Perhaps a
better one is that there should be a law “compelling all
persons saved from drowning, who were unable to swim,
to pay a tithe” of their property to their rescuers.
Nevertheless, one does rmt find that Australian
swimmers’ prowess (as advertised here) is suffici-
ently marvelled at. But there are many illustra-
tions to marvel at, from Assyrians to mermaids
(though surely Assyrians came chronologically
after mermaids: and caught them?) then past
mermaids down to the botches of illustrators un-
able to swim : a curious and enthralling collection.
The index is fully readable.
In long, the book is a new wonder of the literary
world : to an intelligent swimmer it seems indis-
pensable. So, you may demur, is 12a. Cxi. : a fallacy
to be exposed by dispensing.

George Moore, with an eye to Shaw’s pose, has
been prefacing discussion of‘Schiller with the avowal that
he never read Schiller’s work. He adds that Schiller’s
power of writing poetry must have been limited by his
Christian name of. “ Frederick,” which recalls to The
Academy the Oxford man of whom it was written that
“ he goeth not to lectures, for he saith, How can a man
lecture in hags cut like’.that?” Of course Schiller’s name,
was not Frederick,‘but Friedrich'; a different- thing alto-
gether. Omitting' that point, Th,e Academy challenges
Moore to account, for -the fact that men with the same
Christian name have-often had quite different kinds of
genius. “ What resemblance exists,” it asks, “between the
gifts of Robert Burns and Robert Hichens?” Well, both
are pre-oceupied with the lasses, O : “Robert” is always
strongly sexed. In Moore and Carlyle, Thomas is a name
of strict morality. Every 7 Thomas is Respectability’s
pillar. Samuel Johnson and Samuel, Clemens do not run
together, because Clemens soon found out that his name
did not suit him, and,changed it to Mark —even in domes-
tic life. The connection between George Moore and
George Herbert-is an easy riddle. Bot h are deeply devo-
tional, and walk through cloisters of prose swinging
odoriferous sensei's.
MEMORANDA
'The,,Athenaeum ssiys G. W. Lambert’s equestrian
portrait of a boy in the Academy shows “a real sense of
design, and a certain scholarly deliberation in the choice
Of colour, the note of dry red telling well upon a general
scheme of warm greys and browns.” The praise is pleasant
to read ; and if Lambert can paint as well as this, it is
time he painted the two pictures which he owes on
scholarship account to the local Gallery. Since N.S.
Wales (with occasional deliberation) put down the money
to start him on a European career, he should at least ful-
fil the conditions.
“ Beau. S.” writes some Adelaide art notes :
This year’s S. A. Society of Arts Exhibition is the best
Recorded, due to the fact that Will Ashton, Hans Heysen,
and. Hayley Lever, all recently returned from Europe, are
almost the whole exhibition. The place of honour is given
to Will Ashton’s large canvas, *• Winter’s Evening on the
Seine."
Ashton exhibits a dozen other pictures, notably “ An
Evening Idyll',*-' “Sand Dunes,;” ’ arid “The Jetty.”
“•Idyll” is a meadow, with a clump of oaks, a wall at the
bottom, two lovers nqar it, a bay in the background with
'a, sailing boat that apparently-moves, slowly along through
the stiff evening, and a pale moon overhead.
(Heysesn’s notable work A a clump,,of mallee, gracefully
grptiped, with a. hright sun. shining orv the leaves—“ Sun-
shine and Shadows,” Tbe subject is similar to his
“ Mystic Morn ” (noW in S.A. Gallery), but is painted in a
different light.
Levler exhibits an excellent seascape, “ Foundering.”
The foreground is'the depth of a huge wave, capped with
foam. In the middle .distance is the suggestion of a vessel
foundering. In the; background are dark, frowning cliffs,
where heads rise up add hide themselves in vapour. It is
ail. impressive thing, arid kthpetbing. new in seascape.
!L 1!. Gilt’s “Fringe o? the Sea,” and Davies’ “Path
Through the Bush,” are both? better than their owners’
exhibits of last year. James Ashtbrj.is well represented,
and there is a noticeable improvement in his work. In
portrait work the Hatnbridge girls, Hilly and Helen, and
Jas. Keen exhibit satisfactory work. Judged by previous
years, the verdict on the exhibition can be only compli-
mentary.
weeks ago the Sydney picture-dealer,. Aldanhpven,
.caine to Adelaide with a collection of pictures “ valued at.

i'fie Musicdl Courier suggests that Paderewski’s
recent, illness was due to a. peculiar action of the right foot
in pedalling. Instead of resting his right heel on the floor
while using the pedal, he raised- his right-foot; and con-
sequently his right leg and his entire right side were
under strain. It is suggested that this has caused nervous
prostration, respiting from a stiff neck. More likely P.
has had his first touch of hemiplegia. He is just the full-
blooded, full-nerved man whom one wpuld expect paralysis
to claim as victim. . -V - '.

It is now quite clear that English verse of the future
will be moulded partly by'rhe-q uantity of words, that
feature which generations of critics have assured us could
not exist- in English verse. Note the little blots above.
Wooed may be held as long as mood, but practically the
- e makes a.difference to the, eye at least," with the result of
a rhyme not quite beautiful. Another faulty line is “On
the right side and the wrong.” On the and and the are
objectionable in themselves, and there are too many
t-sounds ; but tbe second the spoils the metrical effect by
obliging one to slur the long vowels in right side. The
, last line is good : doesn’t gallantly give a fine image of a
plumed cavalier on a caracoling horse approaching the
combat. ’ ’.'A
G. B. Shaw's paternal grandfather, according to
a “ near relative,” was high sheriff of comm Kilkenny.
His father was a younger son. The present represen-
tative of the Shaw family in Ireland is Major Sir
Frederick Shaw, of Bushey Park, Dublin. Shaw’s mater-
nal grandfather was Walter Bagnall Gurley, a landed
proprietor whose entailed estate G.B.S. inherited in 1898,

Eight Years' Suffering.
A PERMANENT CURE.
The Case of Mrs. A. WEAVER,
(By a Syd.iey Reporter.)
No one can tell the self-denying work
which good men have done in the early
days of this country, neither can we
exaggerate the nobleness of spirit
which animates present-day people in a
desire to ameliorate distress as much
as lies in their power. Therefore, the
public should be informed of some of
these efforts, one of which was made
by Mrs. Alice Weaver, of .No. 7 Louis-
street, Redfern, who replied to the re-
porter by saying :
“About this time last year I was
living in Telopea-street, and it was
just before I left there for this place
that I was fortunate in casting aside a
complaint that had worried me for no
less than eight years. What a fearful
life of suffering mine had been all that
time, to be sure, and to think that
nothing in the world could benefit me
made my spirits droop lower than
they wili ever be again, I hope/'
“What made you think that nothing
could benefit you '{” queried the
writer.
“L arrived at that conclusion because
nothing had done me good so far, and
I believe I tried every medicine going.
At the time my second child was born
1 w-as under a doctor who did every-
thing he could for me, but still my
health remained as bad as it could be.
Indeed, at that period I was nearly
dead with indigestion, although I diet-
ed myself and did all that could be
done to keep my torments in check.
It was of no avail though, for when I
ate anything I could hardly breathe
owing to a feeling which came like a
lump in my chest, from which place a
sharp pain went right through to the
centre of my shoulders. I then remain-
ed in the utmost agony until the food
was vomited up again, after which I
felt so exhausted that I could not
think of doing a thing in the house
until I had had a good rest. It would
surprise you if you only knew how
poor my appetite was. You would
wonder how anybody could keep alive
with it, and as for my head—oh !my
sufferings with it we re awful. The most
piercing pains at one time and the
most depressing aches at another.
Really, 1 think it was a marvel how I
kept my senses with the agony I en-
dured in the head, and it’s strange
that I didn’t meet with many a nastv
accident through the giddiness which
used to overtake me."
“Why ? Were you ever seized with it
in the street V”
“Often I was attacked when walking
down the road, when my sight would
leave me, and I felt horrible ; and suf-
fering so long had played havoc with
my nervous system, too. I would stand
for an hour at the corner of a street
before I would dare to cross it, and
when 1 made up my mind it wasi gene-
rally just as a tram or something was
passing, and I’d have to run bade out
of the way. That was always my
trouble. I could not make up my
mind to do a thing for a long while,
and when I did it invariably turned
out wrong, which showed that ner-
vousness can throw a person’s mind
into a state of confusion. But I’m not
at all like that now. I can dodge in
and out of the thickest traffic,' and
never have the slightest fear of get-
ting down ; I can walk about in "the
dark at any hour of the night, and
not feel the least bit atraid, though it
is only a year since I was so nervous
that l couldn’t do such things if 1 bad
been given a thousand pounds."
“How do you account for such an
extraordinary change ?"
“It’s easy to do that. It was Cle-
ments Tonic which effected the altera-
tion, and to my last day on earth 1
shall feel grateful to that remedy for
the way it pulled me round, for when
1 started with it I was so reduced in
flesh that there was only the frame of
me left, and often and often I felt lit
only to go away and lie down and die.
1 was a misery to myself and every-
body around me, and to tell you the
honest truth I never thought I would
live through it all. All the years 1
was ailing there didn't seem to be a
solitary thing that went right. Alv
eyes used to feel heavy enough to drop
out of their sockets, and 1 was affected
in the mouth by such a bitter taste
that it made me feel sick of a morning
after putting in the usual restless
nights that had done so much to-
wards making a wreck of me. If I
slept at all 1 dreamt such awful
things about the children that I used
to call out for help, and on awaking
would find myself bathed in perspira-
tion and trembling like a leaf. E en
in the daytime I was always imagin-
ing something terrible was going- io
happen, although it never came to
pass, but still that fact did not keep
me from getting as gloomy and sad-
hearted as ever woman was. And
these were just the very thing's that
Clements Tonic showed itself to be the
master of, for it sent such vigour
through my frame when I had been
taking it a couple of weeks that i
could not help feeling joyful in the be-
lief that at last my health had taken
a beneficial turn. 1 got more Clements
Tonic, and as each bottle was finished
so did my prospects improve, for I
commenced to take my meals with a
degree of heartiness which astonishec
my people as much as it pleased them,
and to my own satisfaction and de-
light the former discomforts attending
my meals were Quickly out of exist-
ence. An overloaded sensation in the
stomach, which made my clothes feel
too tight for me, had been one of my
annoyances, and I used to belch up
wind frequently ; but a short course oi
Clements Tonic got those troubles un-
der, besides removing from my loins
the aches that were particularly pain-
ful whenever I had to bend down for
anything. Oh, dear ! what a changer
woman I am now to then ! My limbi
do not ache from weakness like they
formerly did, and 1 can go about my
work as cheerfully and as actively as
any housewife in the country. I do not
spend any restless nights now. never
dream during my sleep, and the pains
that used to abound have long since
been driven away from every part oi
my bo'dy.”
“And have you had no physical
troubles whatever since you were
cured ?”
“Not in any shape or form, and now
when I go to see any of my old
friends they laugh to see me looking so
well, and it’s enough to make them
considering I was so like death a year
ago. Well, Clements Tonic made me
the healthy woman I am, and for the
sake of other sufferers 1 trust you'll
publish this interview in any way you
think best.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, ALICE WEAVER, ol No. 7 Lotiis-
street, Redfern, in the State oi New
South Wales, Commonwealth of Australia do
solemnly and sincerely declare that T have
carefully read the annexed document, consist-
ing- oi four folios, and consecutively numbered
from one to four, and that it contains and
is a true and faithful account of mv iiiness
and cure by Clements Tonic, and also con-
tains my full permission- to publish in auy
way my statements—which I give voluntarily,
without receiving any payment ; and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believ-
ing the same to be true, and by virtue ol
the provisions of the Oaths'Aft, 3900.
Subscribed and declared at Redfern this
sixth day of September, one thousand nine
hundred and four, before me.
L. BREARLEY, J.P.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Camp and Town.
Over the flat the river flows,
Singing its song in the same old way.
Ripple and eddy its surface shows,
Touched with the gold of a sunset ray ;
The green grass, dotted with daisy-heads,
Like a snow-flecked sheet of emerald, spreads
Over the banks ’neath the gum-trees grey.
Old Time has marked with heedless hand
The winding way where the waters glide ;
Hard-by. the homes of a township stand.
And all of the old bush glories hide.
But to me, as I sit in the closing day,
My thoughts go wandering far away,
Back ’gainst the flowing of time and tide.
Ghosts of the sixties pass again
Over the dusty, trailing track ;
The bullock-team in the lumbering wain,
And I hear the swish as the long whips crack ;
For here, where the ti-tree thickest grew,
Was the crossing-place we teamsters knew,
Ere the axe had driven the bush-line back.
Yet vain are the visions my fancies share
Of bearded mates of the olden days,
Who drank at the inn of its rough-brewed beer —
Of their swearing devil-may-care wild ways—
Those free and easy old times are sped,
When we slept with a blanket of stars o’erhead,
Warmed by the camp fire’s roaring blaze.
On the Christmas days we were loyal souls
To the old-world custom, kind and rough,
And the blackened bucket, over the coals,
Sent savory steam from the boiling “duff.”
It helped our journey to kingdom come
When we washed it down with shanty rum—
Some of the real old deadly stuff.
I miss that crossing of days gone by—
A bridge spans now where the waters play;
Instead of the wild-dog’s startled cry
One hears the house-dog’s watchful bay,
Swelling the sounds of a township’s strife
Like the quickened pulse of a fretful life,
That onward spreads in its listless way.
And a new inn stands where the old inn stood-
And I see the sun slope down to the west—
To an old man’s vision of bad and good,
Some of the old days seem the best;
And I often-think, when I’ve got to go,
I should like to sleep where the waters flow,
Lulled by their lazy voice to rest.
VIO.
T. H. ORD.
Now and Then.
Does a fellow ever wonaer as ne sees his fingers
flying
Across the copy-paper why his digits are so white?
Does he marvel why on earth he took to scribbling
and to lying,
When, just a while ago, he humped a swag and
camped at night
’Mid the box-trees on the billabong, and kicked
some leaves together,
Flopping down to rest upon them, with the Murray
for a view ?
Does he feel ashamed to raise his city gamp to
meet the weather,
When for years the sky was good enough ? If he
doesn’t, well, I do !
Has he ever sat in office, and cast his eye above
him
To the peg whereon reposes his superlative new
hat ?
Does he mind the old felt cady, with the corks
suspended from it,
That he wore from Lower Murray all the way to
Ballarat ?
Would he use that nice hard-hitter to remove a
boiling billy ?
Would he wear a plaited greenhide round the
“ chappo ” that is new ?
Does he hanker after old times till the longing
drives him silly ?
Does he curse the city head-gear ? If he doesn’t,
well, I do !
What about the little handbag that is lying on the
table ?
It contains the morning paper and some sandwiches
for lunch,
And recalls the set of “nosebags” that has never
seen a stable
(It was hell to fill a couple, let alone the blooming
bunch).
When he swaggers in the cook-shop, planking
down a nimble shilling,
He’s bound to think of tucker that has cost him
ne’er a sou—
Of the stations where the storekeeper was any-
thing but willing
Does he hunger for a “Johnny ” ? If he doesn’t,
well, I do.
Does he ever, as he climbs the car and sits upon
the cushion,
Wish the whole caboose to blazes and a saddle
’tween his knees ?
Would he swap the spark electric for old “ Spark,”
the well-bred bush’un,
Or exchange the iron standards for the gnarled
old blue-gum trees ?
When he rises in the morning at eight-thirty
growling badly
At his collar-stud, his razor, or his breakfast all
the time,
Would he chuck the inside billet ? Does he ponder
very sadly
On the days now gone for ever ? If he doesn’t,
well, I do.
M. Ju

MEDICATOR.
Catarrh is inflammation of
the lining' membrane of the
nose and adjoining passages.
If this inflammation is not
arrested it invades the pas-
sages which lead from the
nose to the head, ears, throat
and lungs. It injures the
sight and hearing, destroys
the sense of taste and smell,
renders the breath offensive,
breaks down the affected tis-
sues, consumes the nasal car-
tileges, and rots away the
small frontal bones of the
skull. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and sto-
mach, causes dyspepsia, also
consumption. Do you want
relief and cure? If so, try
our great remedy.

Prick jo.
SINKING.
A French paper recently reported that Australian Half-Premier Reid was going to Europe to swim the English Channel. He is busy
swimming another channel just now, and the land looks a very long way off.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, adv-.rtise-
menls, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not- be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY. JULY 6, 1905.
FOE THE NATION?
At its birth in Australia the Labor party was
charged with being a “class” party, and its first
lispings were directed to a denial of that accusa-
tion—to a claim that it spoke for the people, and
not for a mere section. The fact that, for many
years, the interests of the workers—the artisans
and laborers, the “hands” dependent on wages
for existence—had been neglected was given as a
reason why the party should have on its platform
many planks of an exclusively “ labor” character.
And the reason was sufficient, seeing that the
wage-workers represent the base on which society
rests, and that their interests had been much
neglected in the past. And, in general, the
party has to be credited with a patriotic
purpose. It has met many new issues as they
arose, not always with courage (the failure of
the N.S.W. section to declare for honest finance
at the last elections was a heavy blow to the
party’s reputation), but always with, seemingly,
an honest intention to do what appeared to be the
best for the people. Now approaches a crisis
when the Labor party will be put to a test, crucial
in its history. It has to declare for Protection
and Nationalism or “ Fiscal Agnosticism ” and
Sectionalism at a great crisis in the Common-
wealth’s history.
* * * *
By a series of accidents the Labor party in Aus-
tralia was born without a fiscal policy, just as,
occasionally, a baby is born without arms. The
direct impetus bringing the party to life was
the great Maritime Strike. In that strike the
cliief participants were workmen whose callings
made them easy victims to the Freetrade de-
lusion —coal-lumpers, wharf-laborers, miners arid
shearers, all men to whom the benefits of Pro-
tection are not nearly so obvious (though quite as
real) as to boot-makers, wool-spinners and metal
workers. The Freetrade workmen of N.S.W.,
coming to the sorrowful end of a feverish epoch
during which the borrowing madness had followed
the gold rush and been followed by the land boom,
were tilled with bitterness at the woes coming to
them at the hands of a Freetrade Govern-
ment—at the Gatling guns of M’Millan, the
callousness of Bruce Smith and the carelessness
of Henry Pakkes, dreaming amid the thickets of
his hair while the soldiers were being collected to
shoot down protesting toilers. But these Free-
trade workers didn’t blame Freetrade. The Free-
trade Government they detested, but the Free-
trade principle they still accepted with pathetic
folly. It was not a time for a clear reasoning and
a calm examination of facts, which would have
shown that depression, unemployment and desti-
tution were the natural after-products of the suc-
cessive booms under Freetrade conditions, and
were only to be adequately relieved by a policy of
High Protection to open up factories for the
surplus population, and of financial honesty and
self-reliance to lift the crushing mortgage off the
public estate. Thus the first section of the Aus-
tralian Labor party started life as a “ fiscal agnos-
tic,” and with, if anything, a leaning to Freetrade.
As subsequent sections were organised that mis-
take was not always repeated ; in Victoria
and South Australia the Labor party was from
the first rigidly Protectionist. But when the
various units of the party came to he federalised
into the Australian Labor party there was enough
of the Freetrade element left in the N.S.W. sec-
tion to induce the Federal party to also declare
itself of the neuter gender on the tariff question.
So it has remained to the present time, arid the
Labor conference to meet on July 8 will have to
decide whether it will still remain so.
* a 0
The political circumstances have a good deal
changed since the last session of the Labor confer-
ence. The Labor party in Parliament has won to
a term of office, and in office was obliged to
administer a tariff, and found that it could not
“ sink the fiscal issue.” It did not. declare to the
Customs officials that it was immaterial whether
imports were taxed at the various ports or
not. It did not even adopt a “ sink-the-fiscal-
issue” compromise by tossing up to decide
whether duties should be levied or not —a fair and
reasonable way of deciding a matter which the
party professed to consider utterly unimportant.
The party is also now a claimant for office ag tin,
and a claimant with excellent chances of success,
and, if it gets to the Treasury benches, will not
only have to administer a tariff, but also amend or
refuse to amend a tariff upon the report of the
Tariff Commission, and thus declare itself Protec-
tionist or Freetrade. For the Labor Party to
remain a fiscal neuter under the circumstances will
call for an acrobatic agility superior to that of
even Yes-No Reid. But, important as are these
political changes, they are as nothing to the recent
national developments which bring Australia
within very sight of its day of fiery trial, and
hinge directly on the fiscal issue.
9 * * *
A year ago it seemed as if Australia, if it chose
to be so foolish, could, without danger, drift along
on the old path of laissez faire for another quarter-
century—growing a little wool, herding beasts for
their hides and their tallow, making an odd penny
by delving in the ground for minerals, generally
doing the rough work of Creation and trusting to
a loan to squeeze through a bad winter or a
drought, and borrowing at other times just to keep
its hand in. On this, our little selection, such a life
seemed safe enough—inglorious certainly, but
secure so long as the interest on the mortgage
could be scraped up and the bailiffs kept out.
But all that has changed. With peal of thunder
and flash of lightning, the Yellow Man at our
very door comes to the front as the Warrior of
the Earth, and looks ominously towards our empty
spaces and towards the breaks in our fences, as he
forges his guns and builds his ships. Only a fool
can imagine there is anything of the old security
left. Right in the path of the awakened Mongol,
flushed with victory, stands almost-empty Aus-
tralia, unarmed, with no factories for arms. Even
a fool’s degree of prescience warns Australia to
take steps to save itself or prepare to perish.
Within view of this lonely outpost of the White
Race come the flotillas of Asia, and the garrison
must prepare, soon or late (and it may be very
soon), for stern struggle or inglorious surren-
der. As to which—it is for the Labor party,
more than any other party in the Commonwealth,
to say. That party is now dominant. The
respect it has won in the past by its sinoerity, its
honesty, and its patriotism puts it in the position
of arbiter of the continent’s destiny. If it declares
for the right course that course will be taken. If
it declares for the wrong, the right will be nearly
hopeless of accomplishment
» * * «
As to the essential steps of defence there is no
reasonable question. For defence, Australia must
have arms and the means of making them in Aus-
tralia, and only Protection can give that; must
have more population for the fleet and the-army,
which only Protection can supply; must so
organise its industries that, were the sea set with
hostile fleets, all the processes of civilised life
could still continue, and, again, Protection alone
can supply the want. On these points there is
no room for difference of opinion. The statesman
and the soldier agree that in the event of war
there is no hope for the nation which cannot
supply its own arms and ammunition. Only last
week Major Legge added to the volume of
warning :
When the male population of Australia has to take up
arms in defence of its country, it will be lucky if its
available stores work out to one rifle for every 10 men,
and about four rounds of ammunition per head. And the
arms factory, the shell foundry ? They are in Eng-
land or Germany. The months that should be devoted
by such works to running three shifts a day would be
taken up in a frantic attempt to create the machines we
now prefer to run with foreign labor in a foreign country.
We are living in a fool’s paradise.
And what he, as a soldier, says in regard to gun
factories, any statesman with powers of percep-
tion must echo in regard to locomotive and steel-
rail factories, ship-yards and textile mills. To
face what is to come, Australia needs engines,
ships, steel goods, and fabrics, just as much as
guns and powder.
* * * *
Now, is the Labor party, at this juncture, ready
to strip itself of thedast remnants of its old Free-
trade folly and come out definitely on the National
side, to cast aside all small sectional interests—
which would frighten it with the bogey that a few
lumpers will lose their jobs if the stream of im-
ports is blocked —and declare that it is for the
people as a whole, and for the nation’s safety ? It
is no mere question of day-to-day politics. It is
an issue which touches the very life of Australia.
Should the Labor party fail to "meet the call that
the Commonwealth now makes upon it, Aus-
tralian Nationalists will have to look around for
some other means of salvation. Fidelity to the
Labor party cannot be allowed to bring the nation
to the verge of destruction. Should the Labor
party refuse to take the right course, to follow that
Kwill be to vote against Australia, and
icrats must organise another party which
will be just as much the friend of the masses in
regard to the principal questions of politics, and
will offer in addition some sane hope of maintain-
ing a White Australia. All “Labor legislation”
drops into insignificance beside the prospect of
Australia being over-run by the Mongol, and Aus-
tralians becoming the mean whites of a Japanese
satrapy. That possibility, is near, appallingly
near; beside its imminence, there is nothing else
of anv real importance. The Labor party "must
face that or it betrays Australia.
“THE BULLETIN’S ” ATTITUDE
TOWARDS THE N.S.W. LABOR PARTY.
Holman, M.L.A., writes concerning the State
Labor Party and other things :
The time is at hand when The Bulletin will really have
to make up its mind in State politics as to (1) whether it
is for us ; (2) whether it’s against us; (3) whether the
whole of its powerful intellect is so wrapped up in the
question of dirt-money for Samuel Griffith or for the
interesting capitalists who run the Sunshine Harvester,
that it really can’t be bothered about State politics--t.be
mere management of land and railways and education
and finance — at all. If The Bulletin is going to take up
the third position—that the Tariff and the High Court
are the only tvvo things that the truly intellectual can
bring themselves to think about in the way of politics—
then the sooner the democracy gets warning of it the
better. We can keep off the grass. But if it has
any opinion on public matters on the State’s level,
then that opinion must be either favorable to the
Labor party or hostile. The Labor party is old enough,
and has done enough to be definitely bLssed or banned.
We are entitled to a clear declaration, and not to hinted
ambiguities and hesitated dislikes, such as make up the
articles —one dealing with myself and one with the party—
that appeared in last week’s issue.
The Bulletin alleges that it sees some subtle connec-
tion between the Labor platform and its pet quackery of
High Tariff. We don’t. We want to nationalise indus-
tries as they develop—not to assist by our platform in
building up a fat plutocracy to resist all approach to
nationalisation. The Bulletin asks us to build up great
steel and iron works by a high tariff in order that the
capitalists who run the works may make large fortunes,
and run armies of Pinkertons, and savagely fight every
step we take towards nationalising the works. We are
prepared to wait for the works until the people are ready
to run them on national lines from the start.
If High Tariff is to come in Australia, let those who
have an axe to gr ind barrack for it. The Labor party
won’t. Fiscalistn will never be incorporated with the
Labor programme in New South Wales without a struggle
that will rend the party asunder, and its friends will
leave the proposal alone. Its reception at the January
conference this year was a sufficient indication of the
deep-seated aversion of all Laborites to any banging on
the fiscal drum in their midst. It was brought up there—
temperately and intelligently—by one of the most pro-
mising of the country delegates. The previous question
was immediately moved and carried (I think without a
division) by a gathering in which Watson, McGowen and
all other well-known Protectionists of the Labor party
were present.
It is only necessary to steadily read The Bulletin
through to find all the reasons that a Labor man wants in
this State in favor of doing what we are doing. Thus in
p. 22 of this issue The Bulletin exhibits a picture of John
Bull closing the door (at last) against the undesirable
alien. That undesirable alien consists, in England, mostly
of the artisan who comes out of liigh-tariff Germany. The
High Tariff which The Bulletin wants us to adopt has
been adopted in the Fatherland. It hasn’t so far made
the lot of German labor so bright and happy that it stays
there with enough enthusiasm to make an Alien Exclusion
Act unnecessary in Great Britain. The Exclusion Act is
necessary only because the Germans won’t stay at home
and bask in the blessings of High Tariff. Some of the
I.abor party have been in England (which apparently
The Bulletin hasn’t) and know this (which apparently
The Bulletin doesn’t). I am one of them. I can’t
enthuse worth a cent over High Tariff or any other
tiscalist proposal. All the same I have nothing to do with
Freetrade principles. If this country is capable of any
new industries I am quite prepared to start ’em to-
morrow—on a collective basis. But I don’t propose to
amend the Labor platform so as to allow an Australian
Carnegie or Pierpont Morgan to collect his 30,000,000
dollars for starting ’em for us (under the name of High
Tariff).
Apparently The Bulletin isn’t playing chaneys with any
of the State (N.S.W.) Labor party at present mainly
because that party will follow their own platform, and
not the one thoughtfully provided for them in The
Bulletin columns. Still, I am always willing to take
my share of any frigidity Tiie Bulletin has to deliver.
I am glad, in fact, at all times to see my name in print.
But I certainly think that The Bulletin, before dealing
it out, even to a N.S.W. Labor man, might get up its
facts. The hostile rags misrepresent them enough,
without the allegedly friendly following suit. I had no
share at all in the resolution carried by the unemployed
meeting at the Statue. I went to that meeting as an
emissary from the Labor party (which was then caucus-
ing on various matters), to tell the meeting that the
party would receive the deputation at any later and
more convenient day. Having delivered this message,
and made a few appropriate remarks, I went away, and
had no more knowledge than the dead of the resolution
the meeting then proceeded to carry.
* * * *
To which The Bulletin replies as briefly as
possible (it isn’t easy to follow up the track of so
much “ sass” and ignorance in a small space):—
The first paragraph of Mr. Holman’s letter is a cheeky
piece of misrepresentation. It isn’t for The Bulletin to
make up its mind about its attitude towards the
N.S.W. State Labor party, but for that party to
decide on its own attitude towards its own prin-
ciples, and towards the most urgent questions of the
day. Re" the mere management of land and railways
and education and finance,” for example. The Bulletin
has a definite financial policy, and stands by it— the no-
borrowing policy. The State Labor party also professes
to have a policy, the main plank of which is that no new
loan works should be undertaken except such as will pay
interest and sinking fund. It doesn’t stand by its policy ;
in fact, it never says a word about it now, and apparently
it has forgotten that it has a loan policy.
As to railways and general finance, The Bulletin has a
distinct policy, which is, that all land revenue should be
set aside for railways and other public works.
It lias urged Mr. Holman’s party for years to
adopt this idea, and it hasn’t even been able to get
an answer. The Labor party doesn’t say definitely either
“yes” or “no.” It is practically impossible to get it to
say anything about finance. The Bulletin also advocates
the federalisation of the railways with a view to establish-
ing a uniform gauge, but it can’t find out whether the
State Labor party is for this or against it.
Further, in land matters, The Bulletin has called out
during a long procession of year? for a graduated land-
tax—one heavy enough to break up the large estates
wholesale and keep them broken up, while the Labor
party accepted Reid’s non-graduated tax and was
content—or else asleep. It is a very long time now
since the N.S.W. State Labor party has made any
serious move in the direction of a graduated land-tax.
Again, The Bulletin (really) and the N.S.W. State
Labor party (nominally) urges the need for a
complete Local Government system. Yet the Labor
party allows one of its prominent members (Niel-
sen) to denounce Local Government because it
involves levying rates in country districts. This
paper’s complaint against the N.S.W. State Labor
party is that it has no financial policy except drift, no
loan policy except on paper, no public works policy ex-
cept a policy of let-it-slide, not much land-tax policy save
in theory, and no visible railway policy except a policy of
occasionally inquiring why some official was suspended.
On many of the largest questions of the day Tiie Bulletin
can’t tell whether it is for the State Labor party or against
it till the State Labor party gets into a more active con-
dition.
* * • *
To the question whether The Bulletin’s intellect is
mostly wrapped up in the problem of dirt-money for “ the
interesting capitalists who run the Sunshine Harvester,”
the answer is “ No;” A good deal of its intellect is de-
voted to a class which Mr. Holman seems to forget—the
workmen who used to be employed in Australian imple-
ment works till the Freetrade policy which Mr. Holman
advocates turned them out on a cold world for the benefit
and profit of the interesting capitalists who run the
American Harvester Combine. Has Mr. Holman no
gibe to throw at the foreign manufacturer whose
stuff comes here, or does he keep all his wrath
to heap on Australian industry ? And is he such a
slave to that interesting capitalist, the great im-
porter, who sends here the output of the foreign non-
unionist, that he has no jeer to throw at him V The im-
porter is much richer, as a rule, than the Australian
manufacturer, and lives in a better house, and keeps more
flunkeys, and votes the anti-Socialist ticket much more
regularly. He and his Freetrade supporters are the
whole box and dice of the anti-Socialist league, yet' Mr.
Holman has not a word to say in condemnation of the
importing class. It is a question if that gentleman even
took the trouble to find out, before he wrote his letter, if
the Sunshine Harvester people were one-quarter as
financially bloated as many Of the dealers in foreign
goods.
Further the statement in Mr. Holman’s letter that The
Bulletin wants Australia “ to build up great steel and
iron works by a high tariff in order that capitalists who
run the works may . . . run armies of Pinkertons,,” &c., is
a falsehood. There is no other word that can express so
monstrous an assertion. Tun Bulletin wants to build
up great iron and steel works and hundreds of other
works in order to show that with Arbitration laws, eight
hour laws, factory legislation, and other items of the
Labor programme it is possible to have industries without
slavery, riot and civil war. This paper has faith i i that
kind of legislation. Apparently Mr. Holman has none;
he seems to hold that it will do no good whatever, and it
almost looks as if he has been voting for it simply by way
of ornament and ham-frill.
And Mr. Holman says : “If this country it capable of
any new industries I am quite prepared to start them to-
morrow.” It is difficult to remember when any worse
insult was thrown at the Australian workers. There are
thousands of them wanting work, and there are scores of
industries that might be established here merely to supply
our own wants—to supply ourselves with goods that are
now imported for the great profit of that interesting
capitalist, the anti-Socialistic M’Millan, and his friends.
But Mr. Holman, it would seem, isn’t sure that the
workers whom he is supposed to represent are capable of
carrying on these industries. The European, the Ameri-
can, even the Asiatic, are capable enough, but as to the
Australian, Mr. Holman has doubts. It is just possible,
it would seem, that all he is fit for is to make roads paid',
for out of loans.
Yet though Mr. Holman won’t advocate the creation of
any profitable, necessary and self-sustaining works until
they can be started on the basis of collective ownership,
he is apparently not at all-particular about pawning the
country to the individual capitalist. No one was more-
definite in his refusal to countenance the anti-borrowing
movement than Mr. Holman. He has no objection to
the country being mortgaged, body and soul, to Car-
negie and Pierpont Morgan. The Bulletin has an objec-
tion. It wants to see our greatest industry, the railways,
run on a true collective basis —not merely to earn interest
for foreign capitalists.
» » c *
The Bulletin, as Mr. Holman says, has not been in-
England ; but its conductors mostly have, which is much
the same thing. The writer of this spent 18 years in the
British isles. When he was 16 he earned his own living in
that wonderful Freehrade country—the living was 6s. a
week. In his worst days his address was in a
tenement house in Cobden-street—anyone seeing the
street would recognise the beautiful fitness of the name-
in the St. Rollox district of Glasgow, where the factories
try to run on a Freetrade basis. The writer knows the
slums of four great Freetrade cities with the knowledge
that comes of living in them. Mr. Holman, apparently,
doesn’t know much of the United Kingdom. For instance,
its aliens are not mostly, as he says, “the artisan who
comes out of high tariff Germany',” though he professes
to have discovered that non-existent fact by personal
observation. At the census of 1901 there were in
England and Wales 247,758 aliens, of whom 49,133
were Germans. The German residents in England
decreased between 1891 and 1901. (The English resi-
dents in Germany increased in the same period,
but that is another anecdote.) Germany only started
Protection in a small way in 1879, and apparently, as
old Fatherland began to brighten up under the new
regime, and to recover from the effects of Mr.
Holman’s policy, some Germans left for homo
again—or for the United States. Germany in 1903
(the last year dealt with by the Statesman’s Year
Boon) sent out 36,310 emigrants, of whom 33,649 went to
the Protectionist United States, and 856 booked, for Free-
trade Britain. In the same year Freetrade Britain, with a
smaller population than Germany, sent out 449,006 immi-
grants, of whom 259,950 were British born, the others
being foreigners in transit. Of these 259,950 fully 230,000
were bound for Protectionist countries. Nearly half the
aliens who come to Britain nowadays are from the Russian
empire (a country which is mainly agricultural, as Mr.
Holman wants Australia to be). These immigrants are
mostly Jews, and they are very largely those Jews who
are too poor to reach the United States, or too diseased to
pass the inspection of the United States immigration
officers in Europe. Britain doesn’t attract foreigners to
anything like the extent that France and Germany do,
as the proportion of the respective alien populations
proves But it gets a worse class—a class that other lands
won’t take.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Labor party, in Mr. Holman’s opinion, is
“ old enough, and has done enough, to be definitely
blessed or banned.” Well, it is almost impossible to bless
it, and difficult to wholly ban it. It has good principles,
but not nearly enough of them, and it seems to have
forgotten some of the most important of. those it
has. On the most urgent questions of all it refuses
to have any definite ideas. It tacitly connives at out-
rageous financial swindles and shameful faking of the
public accounts. And Mr. Holman personally (though it
is to be hoped that he isn’t a fair sample) seems to regard
the man who supplies work, even at good wages, for
Australian artisans as a malefactor. When new in-
dustries are spoken of he seems to have borrowed
the Argus phrase—the “time is not yet ripe.” He is
“ prepared to wait ” and borrow money. He represents
the worst class of Protectionist —the one who wants to
indirectly protect the foreign artisan, the foreign manu-
facturer, the foreign importer, the foreign non-unionist
and sweater against the Australian manufacturer, how-
ever fair the last-named may be. He believes in handi-
capping- the Australian manufacturer with necessary but
expensive restrictions in the matter of short hours, decent
wages, factory sanitation, &c.; then tells him to compete
on Freetrade terms against the foreigner, who isnot so ham-
pered. When he has failed, and lost his money, and closed
his factory, and turned his workmen adrift, Mr. Holman
seems quite satisfied. If he is only in the process of being
ruined, or still makes 2 or 3 per cent, or so, Mr. Hol-
man calls him an “interesting capitalist.” When the im-
porting company, which Mr. Holman seeks to indirectly,
but none the less surely, protect against the Australian
manufacturer and workman, declares a dividend of
8 per cent, or more, Mr. Holman says nothing—at
least, if he has said anything, it must have been said
in a very low voice. As regards the workman who
is unemployed because of the imports of the wealthy
Tory merchant whom Mr. Holman doesn't denounce, he
will get a job when industry is started on a collective
basis, and that, so far as Mr. Holman is concerned, will be
when Mr. Holman is “convinced that the country is
capable,” &c. But it may be a long time before
a fluent lawyer, who doesn’t seem to take the
least interest in the matter, is convinced, and
until we get industries we can’t get men, and while
Mr. Holman is waiting the time keeps drawing nearer
when we will want men to defend Australia, for that time
comes to every country. We are the most defenceless
white man’s country on earth —the least populated, the
furthest from European assistance, the nearest to Asia,
and the one against which, by reason of our White Aus-
tralia policy, Asia is most aggravated. And Mr. Holman
is “ prepared to wait.”
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Federal Crisis.
G. H. Reid is always kicked out with emphasis.
He gets to office by trickery, keeps office by
trickery, and when his bag of tricks is ex-
hausted, collapses dramatically. It was so in
New South Wales, where he was turned out of
office by the biggest majority the House had
known on a censure vote. It is so again in
the Australian Parliament where a record division
has cast him from the office which he stained by
holding. There is no doubt that in a measure
he specifically invited dismissal on this
occasion, and a reason for that will probably
be found in his knowledge that a full dis-
closure of the facts in regard to the High Court
made his contemptuous dismissal sure within a
week or two, and he was satisfied to lose a week
of office for the chance of getting a dissolution
and a consequent two months’ extension of power.
What is to be the outcome of the present situation
is as yet uncertain. The one stable Administration
possible in this Parliament, and in agreement with
the views of the mass of the people, would be a
Watson-Dkaictn coalition. That is what Aus-
tralia will hope for as the outcome of the crisis,
for that alone will put an end to the present
muddle of politics.


“Colonial Rubbish.”
By way of another evidence of the fact that the
way of the N.S.W. manufacturer is hard the fol-
lowing item, extracted from a Sydney morning
paper, has some points of interest: —
In the midst of a discussion on Imperial Federation, at
the Royal Society’s Rooms, yesterday, the ladies of the
British Empire League suddenly found themselves
involved in an argument as to the respective merits of
imported and Australian goods. It had its beginning in
the contention of one of the speakers that patriotism,
like charity, should have its beginning at home—in re-
spect to the choice of wearing apparel, at all events.
“ For instance,” she said, “ I do not see why ladies cannot
have costumes of colonial tweeds, just as some of the
young men are having their suits made of it.” Then
Dr. Grace Boelkk arose, and took the opportunity to
remark that “ If Australian goods were only second-rate
in appearance and quality, manufacturers only had them-
selves to blame if they suffered in consequence.” She
added, in effect, that unless a nearer approach to quality
and fashionable patterns could be obtained, she, for one,
declined to wear Australian tweeds.
Yet Australian tweeds, small as is the demand for
them, are made in a vast number of excellent
patterns, and the quality is generally far better
than that of the imported article, which unpatriotic
Australians delight to praise at the local article’s
expense. As a rule, when the Australian manu-
facturer turns out a first-class sample, the
Imperial Federation kind of person calls it
“colonial rubbish” without even looking at it.
The writer of this recently got a collection
of samples from Vicars’ tweed factory at Marrick-
ville, Sydney. It was a very fine collection, and
quite as good, probably, as anything Dr. Grace
Boelke ever purchased, so perhaps that lady only
denounced the goods on spec, and without inspec-
tion. Writer also got the names of retail tailors who
dealt in these goods. One of them was asked to
make a suit of Marrickville tweed, but he repu-
diated the idea of ever keeping such trash, and
did it with the air of a maligned tailor who was
clearing his character of a suspicion of crime. In-
spection revealed four undeniable samples of
Marrickville tweed in his shop—probably there
were many more, but these were certainties —but
they were all passed off as imported, lest the
custom of the Imperial Federation class of person
should be alienated. In Sydney many of the
finest bedsteads are of local manufacture, but
quite a number of traders dare not tell the public
so, lest the goods should either be rejected
altogether or prove only saleable at half
the price of imported rubbish. Yet the
bedstead-making industry has been established
in Sydney for three generations. Similarly,
the bootmaker, who makes high-class goods, lias
to pass off his best wares as American, lest they
should be scorned and rejected by the Australian
Imperial Federation patriot. The person who
manufactures in N.S.W. may make a proud dis-
play of his factory, and get it paragraphed in the
Freetrade papers, but because of people like Dr.
Grace Boelke, he has to disguise his goods as
carefully as if they were a dead body with the axe
still sticking in its skull. And he must expect to
be denounced at the Imperial Federation meet-
ing as a purveyor of rubbish ; and, even when he
is only making one-fourth the income of a second-
rate importer, Holman, M.L.A., when he finds
him employing Australian workmen, is liable to
allude to him scornfully as a “capitalist;” and
though he pays twice or three times the railway
freight charged to the farmer on his produce, a
person signing himself “Rupert Clarke ’ is
almost certain to write to the Herald, denouncing
him as a parasite who makes thousands at the ex-
pense of the Railway Department. On the whole,
it pays better to be an importer. The Bulletin
has serious thoughts of importing a doctor next
time its corn feels worse than usual.

The Two Treasurers—Bent and Carruthers.
A STRANGELY boneless and imitative individual is
Premier Carruthers, of New South Wales. A
week or two ago Premier Bent, of Victoria, who
isn’t imitative except in the matter of his wonder-
ful and life-like copy of a bear in politics, made a
startling announcement to his constituents at
Brighton. He stated that during the financial
year he had paid off more old debt out of revenue
than he had incurred in the way of new debt, so
that, for almost the first, if not quite the first,
time in 50 years an Australian State had started
to absolutely reduce its aggregate liability out of
revenue. He also forecasted a surplus, available
for public works and land resumptions, of some-
thing like £500,000 (taking the latest estimates).
Bent broke up all precedents by thus making an
informal financial statement outside Parliament,
but the result—even if a good deal of it was
achieved by shady means—was so magnificent
that a less vain man than Bent might have been
excused for breaking out. Then Premier
Carruthers of N.S.W. came in like an echo with
his little yap of emulation—with several yaps, in
fact—of which the following is a sample yap as
summarised in the morning papers :
Yesterday closed the financial year, and Mr.
Carrutiiers, the Premier and Treasurer, was able to
announce the gratifying result that he closed his accounts
for the 12 months with a net surplus of £187,173. As in
bis Budget, he only estimated a surplus of £84,000, the
Treasurer is to be congratulated upon having bettered his
i stimate by the handsome margin of over £lOO,OOO. In
addition to the surplus, the public debt has been reduced
during the year out cf revenue by £275,000, so that the
amount of revenue received over and above the cost of
Government was £462,000.
Carruthers’ estimates was afterwards altered a
little, but the sham remained the same. So this
and similar statements went all round the coun-
try, and the natural result is that a large section
of the community feels quite relieved, and
N.S.W. can go riotously into debt and extrava-
gance without Premier Carruthers being
troubled with many unpleasant remonstrances.
Yet things in N.S.W. are just about as bad as
ever; the only difference is that the artful gloss of
a new and oily misrepresentation has been spread
over the old disease. The exact difference between
Bent’s reduction of debt and Carrutiiers’ reduc-
tion, and between the surplus which Bent offers
and the one which Carruthers offers, is j ust about
this:—
(1) Bent, if his accounts stand careful audit, has bor-
rowed £164,000 during the year, and repaid out of revenue
£226,000. This makes a net reduction in the Vic. Public
Debt of £62,000.
(2) Carrutiiers has borrowed £2,489,332, and has paid
off £319,412 (his extravagant predecessor, Waddell, paid
off about £369,000). Carruthers, taking credit for the
debt he has paid off, and saying nothing about the debt
he has incurred, calls this reducing the public debt by
£319,412. It is really a case of increasing it by over
£2,100,000.
(3) Carruthers has charged to loans a large amount of
unproductive expenditure, which should have been met
out of revenue, and has charged all his productive works to
loans. Bent, not having loans to charge his ordinary
expenditure to, has apparently paid it, and also paid for
his reproductive works, out of revenue.
(4) Carruthers promises a surplus of £187,000 after
using over £1,800,000 of land receipts—more than
half being the proceeds of land sales—a 3 revenue.
If the Im. S. W. land revenue were set aside as a
Public Works Fund (which it should be) JosErir 0. would
have about £1,600,000 deficit.
(5) Bent promises a surplus of about £500,000 (at latest
estimates) after using about £400,000 of land receipts
(rent and sale proceeds) as revenue. If the Victorian
land revenue were set aside as a Public Works Fund
(which it should’ be), Thomas B. would have a surplus of
about £lOO,OOO. The difference between £lOO,OOO surplus
and £1,600,000 deficit is one of the visible differences
between Bent and Carrutiiers.
(6) Bent’s surplus is to be made up after the expenditure
account has been kept open till August (the revenue
account closed on the 30th June) so that all hills
properly chargeable to the year may be paid and deducted
from the surplus.
(7) Carrutiiers’ surplus is to be arrived at by closing
the expenditure account with a bang on June 30. All of
the year’s accounts not paid on that date will not be
deducted from the surplus. In fact, in N.S.W. the
accounts close with such deadly accuracy that a large
payment covering interest on debt from January I to
June 30 is not included. This sum leaves the N.S.W.
Treasury about the middle of June, when it is handed
over to the bank in London as agent for the bondholders,
but because it doesn’t reach the actual bondholders till
July 1 it is reckoned as cash in hand on June 30. George
Reid first devised this political swindle, the result being
that, in the first year of the swindle, though the annual
interest on the Public Debt was about £2,500,000, lie only
charged his accounts with something under £x,000,000.
That was the source of his one famous surplus—the one
which was scornfully repudiated by State-Statistician
Oogiilan, by Auditor-General Rennie, and by two com-
mittees of inquiry, though the S.M. Herald and Sydney
Telegraph swore to its accuracy.
On Monday last the S. M. Herald spoke in mild
—very mild—deprecation of Carruthers’ out-
rageous fake. But the Telegraph published in
its financial column, in small type, the figures
showing that Joey’s net borro wings were
£2,489,332, and his sinking fund repayments only
£319,412, so that he had increased the N.S.W.
debt by over £2,100,000. Then, in its news columns
it shrieked in huge capitals: “SOLVENT!
PUBLIC DEBT REDUCED!” And it is
largely because of papers like the Telegraph that
N.S.W. can’t get clean finance.

The Real Brigands.
A very favorite gag of Bogey-man Reid’s in his
anti-Socialistic shriek is that Socialism means
confiscation. When the State goes into an enter-
prise, is it going to compensate the private enter-
prise which it knocks out ? he asks. . Somebody,
of course, says “ No !” It is usually a Fat party
who speaks in great scorn ; but G. Reid disre-
gards that circumstance, adjusts his eye-glass, and
says: “Exactly! I wanted that admission, and
I have got it. Then you mean confiscation,
spoliation, robbery. Gentlemen, I ask you”—
and so on. G. Reid learned this gag from the
other M‘Lean, who, posing as a very
practical Scotch person, wanted to know where
the Socialists were going to get the money from to
buy up everything with, and what the people
who were bought out were going to do with the
money when the) 7 got it, seeing that the State was
going to own everything. In the first place,
the Labor party doesn't propose either to buy
up or to otherwise get hold of everything. If
either Premier said he was going for a walk, and
set off to the south, nobody would feel justified in
telling him to take snow-shoes, or ask him how he
was going to keep himself warm when he got in
the shadow of the South Pole. Nevertheless both
cheerfully assume that the Labor party
fs going to the South Bole, and when the Labor
party isn’t prepared to say what it is going to do
when it gets there, simply because the South
Pole isn’t in its programme, G. Reid says
“Exactly!” in the tone of one of Bland
Holt’s young men who knows now how he
can lay out the villain in the sth act. Mean-
time G. Reid carefully doesn’t mention what
Private Enterprise does under similar circum-
stances to those he mentions. Reid and his
crowd cut down Kingston’s tariff and let in the
Yankee Harvester Trust, which is slowly strang-
ling Australian Private Enterprise. What com-
Sensation is the foreign Trust giving Private
Interprise—our own knocked-out Private Enter-
prise ? What compensation does Private Enter-
prise ever give to the Private Enterprise which
it knocks out ? What compensation does the
Trust ever give to the factory which it closes?
What compensation does the big manufacturer or
trader give to the little manufacturer or trader
whom he shuts up ? The only compensation, in
fact, that knocked-out Private Enterprise ever
does get is from the State. When the big brewer
wipes out the little brewer, the big brewer
never dreams that the little brewer has
any claim for compensation. But when the State
decides that some of the same big brewers’ hotels
shall be closed, there is a demand from the State
for compensation, and, further, that demand is
allowed. And the people who shriek loudest
against State compensation are George Reid’s
anti-Socialistic clerical and Joe-Cook friends.
In one breath they yell that the wicked State
means to confiscate ; in the next, when the State
shows that it won’t confiscate, even in a question-
able case, they rage at it because it won’t. In
every relation the State shows far more considera-
tion when it interferes with Private Enterprise
than Private Enterprise itself does. It shows, in
fact, much more than consideration. The N.S.W.
Government interfered lately with some wharfage
business; and the State paid about twice
the value that the owners themselves set upon the
Eropertv in their own balance-sheets. The Electric
light Department in Melbourne lately found
two or three small concerns in its way. Private
Enterprise of the size of the Melbourne city
undertaking could have crushed them like so
many small beetles, and passed on. The Socialistic
enterprise paid full value and a bit more. The
State in South Australia, sometime back, resolved
to take over the last of the privately-owned rail-
ways there. A quarter of a century ago Private
Enterprise had fought for the Glenelg traffic and
two lines were built. Private Enterprise with the
longest purse knocked the other line out, bought
it for a fraction of its value, and practically shut
it up. But the State took both lines over and
paid handsomely for them—for the useless line as
well as the other. The Socialistic confiscation
bogey is the hollowest, stupidest sort of a
bogey; and a thousand instances prove it s q.
So far from the State collaring things for nothing,
it is a hard and discreditable fact that the State
is, under such circumstances, always regarded as
something that may rightfully be robbed and
taken down. It is Private Enterprise that is the
plunderer.

The Great Hide Industry.
There is no further doubt. The great natural
resource, the chief primary industry in New
South Wales, is the production of Hide.
T. M. Slattery— or should it bo the Hon.
T. M. Slattery, M.L.C. ?—was scarcely out
of the grip of the sheriff before he was
parading the chief street of Sydney to
receive the congratulations of his friends. On
what? On the fact that a gap in the law makes
it impossible to punish his huge misappropria-
tions, and releases him from a gaol to the
hospitality of which he had a moral, if
not a legal claim. A sense of our com-
mon human weak ness would suggest a charit-
able silence as to Mr. Slattery if he were to
assume a fitting attitude of reticence, and not
loom up in conspicuous places, heralded bv the
fiomp and blare of a mirror-like plug hat and the
oud remonstrance of a fresh tad-coat. But
chanty is choked by the impudence of the man
who wears his legal technicality as if it were a
halo, and sits on his shortage as if it were a throne.
Mr. Slattery —if that is any apology—beats the
loud drum of advertisement in abundant company,
lhe name of another ex-solicitor, who was sen-
tenced to gaol for a particularly noisome con-
spiracy, and by a judicial lapse escaped that
punishment, crops up now prominently in the life
of a certain suburb. Tie spv -ks at public meetings,
has even occupied the pulpit now and again in a
church. His Hide is proof against scorn. Mr.
R. D. Meagher (to cite another example from
the legal profession), whose connection with
the Dean case suggested to him once that he
should hide his shame and his guilt in a foreign
land, now blaz'n.s it out with the best of them in
Sydney public life, and was, according to the evi-
dence before the Lands Commission, disclosed the
other day as carrying on the business of a land
agent, and using the people’s stationery to urge
the people’s tenant not to pay the people’s rent.
These do not, by any means, complete the
list of culprits who should hide their heads,
but, instead, pedestal themselves in public. But
they are the most notable at the moment. Hide,
thick, tough, impervious Hide, is evidently a pro-
duct which New South Wales can grow to perfec-
tion on its public men’s cheek. There should be
some commercial advantage in the product. But
to utilise it would involve a preliminary process
of flaying, and as a community we are too soft-
hearted to give impudence anything but a tacit
approval.
