The Argentine Tariff.
Sydney Evening News contrived the other day
to produce a lengthy leading article on the great
rush of immigrants to Argentina, as compared
with the lack of them in Australia, without once
mentioning that Argentina was a country which
finds work for immigrants by building up manu-
factures under a weighty Protectionist tariff,
and that the great rush of immigrants only com-
menced when that tariff was imposed. Nominally
the Argentine tariff averages 30 per cent, all
round, but according to Australian ideas it is more
like 80 per cent., for the law fixes the value
of the goods imported, and the imports which
Argentina puts down as worth £lOO. and on which it
charges £3O duty, would, on Australian valuations,
be worth about £4O. For instance, if anyone sends
belltopper hats to Argentina he is met on the
wharf by a cold, bitter-minded tariff, which says,
in Spanish : “We don’t want to see your invoice.
It doesn t matter to us whether it is correct or
not. The law says that belltoppers are worth
13s. 4d. each, and the duty is supposed to be 50
per cent., but we call it Bs. per hat.” And that
man either parts Bs. for each belltopper or else
takes his belltoppers away out of Argentina.
After that, it seems quite natural to learn that
there were, in 1901, 227 Argentine hat factories
employing 6700 hands. Similarly, the Argen-
tine law says that ordinary men’s boots
are worth Bs. per pair wholesale, and
the duty is 50 per cent. —4s. per pair.
And the man who, in Australia, would pro-
duce an invoice showing that the boots were
worth 2s. 6d. a pair, and get off, even if we
charged 50 per cent., with Is. 3d. duty, either
pays up the 4s. or takes away the boots. It is the
same in most lines. So Argentina gets immi-
grants, and its Year Book mentions casually that
in 1901 there were 6317 factories in the country
for the manufacture of articles of wearing apparel
alone, and of these 5566 had been established by
immigrants. In 1901 the total number of Argen-
tine factories owned and worked by foreign immi-
grants was 20,532. That is to a great extent what
the immigrant rushes to Argentina for when he
learns about the tariff—though the Freetrade press
always conveys the idea that he goes there solely
to “ get on the land.”

The Call for a Land Bank.
TheS.M. Herald considered the “shares” system
of farming the other day, and came to the sensible
conclusion that it was vile. The system “kills
the initiative of the men who work under it, de-
presses their ambition, and forces them into the
same rut in which most tenant farmers live and die.
As a rule, the shares man who leaves his tempo-
rary holding sets out to a new district to make
another similar agreement, or takes up his swag,
or mounts his horse and goes looking for work on
wages.” These men “devote the best years of
their life to making profits out of the land
for the landholder.” The Herald suggests,
as a way out, “that the State should throw
open large areas.” But the Herald ought
to specify where those large areas are, instead of
waving a paw, in a lordly Jenkins way, and
telling the Lands Department to “do the thing
properly.” Lately the Government cut up an estate
which looked far better country than any, barring
perhaps a few small blocks, which the State has at
command ; and the N.S.W. shares farmers, though
they are little better than helots, hardly applied at
all. The fact is that they haven’t got the neces-
sary capital. They can go into the shares business
because they get land, implements, stock, seed,
everything but labor supplied them. But they
can’t go into business on their own account because
they haven’t the money, and there isn’t a Land
B ink to help them. How far the Maoriland Land
Bank has been responsible f or th e remarkable growth
of settlement there nobody can, of course, say for
certain ; but even an outside observer, like Rider
Haggard, reporting as a Commissioner appointed
by the British Government to inquire into the
whole matter of agricultural settlement, describes
it as a “brilliantly successful” scheme. There
certainly was no striking increase of settlement
till the Land Bank did arrive ; and since it has
arrived it has aided 12,356 settlers. Further,
its existence has decided men who never
have applied for anything to become settlers;
for they know that it is there if they
should want it. N.S.W. Premier Carruthers is
therefore cordially encouraged to go ahead with
his scheme for the amalgamation of the Savings
Banks, or with any other scheme that will hatch
an effective Land Bank. The fact that close upon
half-a-million of acres are cultivated in N.S.W.
on the blood-sucking shares system every year, yet
there was no rush for the lands recently thrown
open for closer settlement, proves that something
more is wanted; and a Land Bank seems to be that
something. And if the Fat party says anything
about shocking interference with Private Enter-
prise, as of course it will, two things may be
pointed out —one for its confusion, the other for
its comfort. The first is that monev-lending isn’t
the only Private Enterprise in the world, and
that farming is Private Enterprise too ; the other
that, in Maoriland, where the Land Bank idea
has been carried farthest, the profits of Private
Enterprise banks are by far the highest in Austral-
asia, and probably about the highest in the world.

Horace Tozer, Agent-General for Queensland, is
reported to have publicly stated in London that
the fear of Socialistic legislation in Australia is
retarding the country, and “preventing the
investment of from £20,000,000 to £40,000,000.”
Australia has put up with Tozer for a long- time,
and this last effort of his, if the report is true,
suggests that that mean and shabby backbiter is
about due to be called to order. It is the duty of
the Queensland Government, which employs
Tozer, to get from him a statement of the sums
which make up that twenty or forty millions, the
names of the people who own the money, par-
ticulars of what Australian industries it was
going to be invested in, and explanations as to
what present or possible item of Socialism pre-
vented the investment, and who supplied the
alleged investor with information about that
present or future Socialistic danger. When
Tozer has furnished these facts, the Queens-
land Government should apply to the
alleged capitalists for corroboration. And if
the statements and the corroboration are
not forthcoming it should give Tozer the alterna-
tive of being dismissed, or of publicly acknow-
ledging himself a dealer in falsehood and a low-
down slanderer of the country which provides
him with a much better living than his abilities or
services deserve. Further, if Tozer prefers dis-
missal, the Queensland Government should publish
in England, fully and clearly, the reason of his dis-
missal ; and even if he prefers to apologise and
retract, he should only retain his position on his
assurance that he will be|a much more reliable
Tozer in the future.

selves and the democratic Protectionists is just
what keeps the Democrats out of a majority and

admitting his friend the nigger in large quan-

The brother of a well-known Melbourne man
writes from the Transvaal concerning what he
saw in a Chow “compound ” on a recent Sabbath
morning :
Having been told that there was trouble at one of the
Chinese compounds, two other fellows and I went up to
see for ourselves if anything serious had happened. One
of my friends knew the man at the gate, so we were
admitted without any difficulty, and lo ! the sight was
something to remember. Kneeling around a large court-
yard were 500 Chinamen, more or less, watching their
fellow countrymen being flogged with a shambock,
a weapon which, lam told, cuts, and cuts severely. I
didn’t go near enough to see for myself the full effects of
the punishment, but with my two friends I stood within
the circle of Chows where we shuddered at the flogging
from a distance. It struck me afterwards that if those
men had risen in revolt they would have made mincemeat
of the whites, who didn’t number 50 in all. Luckily there
was no rising, and we all got away safely. The row had
started with a light between a Chinaman and a Kaffir. I
believe it finished with about 50 of the “compounders”
getting 15 strokes each, a story which the newspapers
didn't publish. This experience I have related to show
you how the mine-managers take the law into their own
hands, and are able, thusfar, to shambock their yellow
slaves with impunity. Slavery is indeed the lot of the
Chow in S’Africa, and although I don’t admire him (for he
is a coward in some ways), I pity the poor beggar under
the capitalists’ rule. Most of the Chinamen in S'Africa,
mind you, are a better class than we know in Melbourne,
but the lot I am speaking of are inferior in build and
everything else. There doesn’t seem to be any more
Chow arriving, so I guess the mines have got all they
want for the present.
Every swish of the British shambock in S’Africa is
a touching reminder of the time when the jingo
press howled against the wicked Boers for their
cruel treatment of the local blacks, and all good
Sons of the Hempire were exhorted to rally round
the one Flag that waves in the best interests of
Civilisation with a big, big C. The catch-word
should have been spelt with aD. Divilisation !

The following (from Sydney D.T.) is an approving
summary of the principles of Premier Carrutiiers’
Bill for supplying N.S.W. with local govern-
ment :
This Bill is designed with the object of placing in the
hands of the people in rural districts the management of
their own local works, so as to relieve them from the
necessity of applying, cap-in-hand, to the central Govern-
ment for each little road or bridge they may require. The
principle of the Bill is “self-help.” It creates local
authorities, clothes them with ample powers to help
themselves, and, by a liberal endowment and the transfer
of the land-tax, supplies, in some cases all, and in most
of the others by far the major portion, of the funds, leav-
ing a very small proportion, if any, of the finances to be
provided by additional taxation.
A striking feature of the measure is the elasticity of
the system therein outlined. Each local government
unit is invested, on its creation, with certain elementary
duties and responsibilities. If it feels that these are a 3
much as the area needs or can pay for, or as much as it
can efficiently undertake, there is no compulsion upon it
to enlarge its sphere. If, on the other hand, the district
is progressive, and desirous of taking up more extended
duties than the mere management of roads and bridges,
machinery is provided in the Bill whereby the council
may obtain the additional powers required. As a check
upon the hasty assumption of wider powers by coun-
cillors, it is provided that all propositions to extend the
sphere of work of a council (with a possibility of increased
taxation) may be either granted or refused by the Gover-
nor, or may be submitted for the approval of the electors
of the area. , ~ ; * /
In other words, there is to be as little local self-
government as possible and the State Government
is to supply practically all the money. This idea
combines the maximum of apologetic evasion on
the part of the Government with the minimum of
self-help on the part of the country population.
Yet this is the Bill which Labor member Nielsen
denounces lest it should tax the poor farmer. The
city worker, meanwhile, will go on paying his
local rates in full as heretofore.
It’s almost equivalent to a public misfortune that
the High Court should have, on a legal quibble,
released T. M. Slattery from gaol. Doubtless,
the quibble is a perfectly legal one, and to the
mind of the lawyer is good reason why Slattery
should escape punishment; but the public eye
cannot be expected to see much virtue in a point
that nullifies natural justice. The High Court
sets the Law in antagonism to Honesty. It is
reasonable to suppose that it had no other alterna-

SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Her Majesty’s “Tom Moore.”
Theatre Royal “ Mikado.”
Criterion “ Naughty Nancj\”

MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.

Opera House Rickards’ Vaudeville Co.
_ „ / Westminster Glee and Concert
Town Hall. \ Par ty.
The revival of “The Gondoliers” by the
Repertoire Co. at Sydney Royal on Saturday was,
in some respects, a welcome surprise, for the co.
far surpassed any of its previous successes, and
gave a rendering which can stand coni parison, with-
out suffering much, with that of the Royal Comic
Opera Co. in its palmy days—the days of Nellie
Stewart, Henry Bracy, Charles Ryley, Violet
Varley and Flora Graupner. “ The Gondoliers ”is
a fancy which deserves kindly treatment. It has
a romantic little story, graced with some delight-
ful lyrics and a very great deal of rich humor,
which plays round the themes of Royalty and
Republicanism. This humor is somewhat of the
Yes-No variety; one character is allowed to
admirably satirise the theory of monarchy ;
another, with just as good an appeal to fun, takes
a G. H. Reid view of Socialism, and prattles of
that time when everybody has to wear silks and
satins and “ up goes the price of shoddy.”
* * * *
Conspicuously successful in Saturday night’s
revival of “ The Gondoliers ” were Miss Aggie
Thorne, who, as Tessa, entered into the true
Gilhertian humor of the piece and sang with
dainty clearness ; and Miss Vinia de Loitte, who
made the part of Casilda stand out prominently
by her clever singing. The Repertoire Co. has quite
a well-stocked aviary, for, in addition to those,
Miss Dolly Castles, who had the chief soprano
part, acquitted herself creditably, and Miss Celia
Ghiloni was, in voice and figure, a fine Duchess of
Plaza-Toro. A fine merry pair of gondoliers
were Kenninghatn and Wilson—the former full of
the exuberant gaiety of the piece, shining as well
in acting as in singing, the latter scoring by the
admirable enunciation of his songs. When Gil-
bert has written the libretto to a Sullivan song,
you want to hear the words as well as the music,
and Wilson sees that you do. Kenningharn’s
voice failed a little first night in his chief
solo, “Take a Pair of Sparkling Eyes,’' mainly
through his forcing it too much, but that was the
only chance he gave in a long innings. H. H.
Wallace, as the Duke of Plaza-Toro, shows a great
deal of improvement on his Jack Point ; he will
develop into a graceful dancer, and his light voice
is improving with practice. For a youngster he
has had golden opportunities forced upon him
rather too thickly, and he needs to keep in view
the fact that he has much hard work yet ahead.
In his old, familiar character of the Grand Inquisi-
tor, Howard Vernon again scores a great success ;
and the Luiz of J. R. Ralston is well sung and
well acted. The Gilbert and Sullivan season has
been extraordinarily successful, packed houses
being the rule. It ends with the coming week,
when “ The Mikado ” will be revived.
** * *
Mr. William Anderson announces that he
will open his Sydney dramatic season at the
Theatre Royal on Saturday, July 15, with the
great Adelphi success, “ The Midnight Mail.”
Name has a sound suggestive of mystery and
rush.
** * *
Mr. William Holman has started a series
of costume recitals of the most striking scenes
from the masterpieces of English drama. The
first recital eventuated at St. James’ Hall, Syd-
ney, last week, when, in the course of two hours,
Mr. Holman and a company of his past and
present pupils put up Broughton’s one-act pomedy,
“Ruth’s Romance,” eight items of music, song,
and story, and finished with the third
act of “ Othello.” The costumes were beauti-
ful, the staging good, and the acting up
to first-class amateur standard, the only
dull items in the show being a song about a bow
and a flute solo. Songs asking “ What of the Bow ”
are as out of date as songs interrogating about the
battle axe. And this writer has only come across
two people who could play the flute, and when
people play the flute who can’t play it, then it
sounds like a machine for taking breathing
exercises on. The representation of the third act
of Othello put the golden roof on the performance.
# sfc -fc *
Andrew Mack is still in the middle of the
stage at Her Majesty’s, Sydney ; and yet, though
he is nearly always there, he is so good an artist
that you don’t notice it till afterwards. _ What
with his ease and grace as an actor, and his singing,
and his skill as a composer, a truly versatile person
is A. Mack. “Tom Moore” has probably, how-
ever, seen its best days in Sydney. There isn’t
an unlimited audience to which it appeals; but
Mack himself has appealed to that audience with
much force.

At Sydney Criterion Mr. John Sheridan,
assisted by Miss Maud Amber and Mr. Winfield
Blake, keeps “ Naughty Nancy ” up to a meteoric
standard. Miss Heba Barlow, Miss Faning and
Miss Waldon-Taylor deserve to see their names in
print as aiders and abettors of the fun.

Those popular amateurs, The Players, are
coruscating at Sydney Palace. To-night “Little
Lord Fauntleroy ” will sit on the apple-barrel,
change his grandfather’s political views, and cal]
his mother “Dearest.”
** * *
At her piano recital at Sydney Centenary
Hall, on Saturday evening, Miss Yvonne Leverrier
had a fashionable audience, including Federal and
State vice-regal sets. The young player is
generally conceded to have considerable musical
talent, which was best exemplified in her
dainty handling of the softer portions of the
music. Where force was required she aimed at
getting too much of it, and the bang and the
misuse of the pedal produced that Confused con-
catenation of sound which immature players are
apt to deem delightful, but which makes the
judicious hearers grieve. When Miss Leverrier
ceases from making such fierce attacks upon the
piano (which is, after all, an inoffensive animal
when treated properly), and also cures herself of a
mannerism suggestive of playing from the
shoulders, she ought to be fit for good
work. As it was, there were man ' moments
of pleasure during her programme. , By the way,
how is it that no one will ever present a
piano programme nowadays without includihg
Bach’s Toccata and Fugue in D minor ? Performed
on the organ, as written by the great John Sebas-
tian, it has nobility as well as brilliancy, but, as
improved by Tausig for the piano, it loses. all its
dignity, and becomes a mangled procession of
octave gymnastics.
** * *
Golden is interrupting himself to tell a
new batch of -stories at Sydney Tivoli, and the
last batch (he moves on at the end of this week)
doesn’t contain as many weeds as the first batch
did. As to his matter, Golden might improve;
as to his manner, he’s good enough as he is. Since
last week the most warmly welcomed new-comer
is F. Yorke, who has been out prospecting for new
jokes and has found a few ; and of the people who
aren’t new the one best worth seeing is Guerra,
the dancing lady, whose extra-long Sydney season
is probably due to the fact that, between dances,
she has been nursing her sick husband, Kara, the
star juggler, who has had a bad bout with ’citis.
He is getting better now— a man who has a wife
as beautiful as Mile. Guerra to live for would
exhibit shockingly bad taste if he did anything
else.
** * *
Jessie MadLachlan, the Scottish vocalist,
“ struck oil ”in Australia. Apart from the fact
that Scots always roll up well to hear a bard of
their own nationality, her success was due to the
intense dramatic power and magnetism that
linger about her singing. Her mezzo voice denotes
good training, but she is not at all particular as to
her method of vocalisation. When she has a
rousing song to deal with she sets resolutely to
work to shout her audience to enthusiasm with
declamatory force, and she generally succeeds.
The Town Hall was crowded for the final enter-
tainment. The singer was encored for every item,
and showered with floral tributes, and the close of
the concert was like a Salvation revival, when
General Booth’s long banner of white beard flies
out horizontally in front of a gale of applause,
like washing blowing down the street. In the
last verse of “ Auld Lang Syne” the audience
joined in and the great chorus was swelled louder
than ever it swelled in Sydney Town Hall before.
It was indeed a Night. The Scots who had their
wallets bled for reserved seats were resolved to get
their siller’s worth in enthusiasm. The audience
was full of haggis and whisky, and emotion,
and towards the end it wept and cheered
violently at the smallest prompting. One woman
in the audience found herself embraced in the
midst of a love song by a sentimental Highlander
from Jerusalem or thereabouts, who told her in a
whiskified and objectionable voice which strug-
gled through the rye that she was a bonnie lass ;
and in the midst of one of Miss Maclachlan’s
Gaelic songs another Scot surcharged with Scotch
cried out the singer’s name in the soggy voice
of sentiment, and seemed likely to propose to her
on the spot. He was run out. Yes, it was a braw
nicht.
*# * *
At Her Majesty’s (Melb.) the blue and
tiresome drama by Sudermann met with all the
neglect that its performance deserved. A revival
of “ Elizabeth, Queen of England,” rose from the
ashes of “The Fires of St. John ” last Saturday,
and the loud, angry Queen with the elevated
heels and ruddy-tinted hair is reigning for six
nights. “Elizabeth” presents Miss Nance
O’Neil in a series of commanding situations,
followed by a long death scene, in which she
is supposed to be many years older than
she looks. The death scene this time is not so
dimly lighted as of yore, when the aged Sovereign
Lady amounted to little more than a voice from
the gloom. In making herself visible as well as
audible the actress adds a touch of human
interest to her monologue without .establishing
a solid claim to shine as a “star” tragedienne.
It is by comparison with the rest of the co.
that Miss O’Neil shines. The performers who
impersonate lords and ladies at the court
of tempestuous Queen Bess are not a
very brilliant collection of artists, but they carry
their decorative clothes in a fairly satisfactory
manner, and appear to be glad when Her
Majesty is dead. If the real original daughter of
Anne Boleyn and Henry the Eighth was half as
red as the drama paints her, the stage courtiers are
Erobably true to history as regards their desire to
ear the last of the old spinster. Next Saturday
the same co. will appear in “The School for
Scandal.”
“ The Skirt Dancer,” revived last Satur-
day, is keeping Stephenson’s Musical Comedy
forces on active service at the Princess’s until
Friday. Then comes “Beauty and the Barge,”
under the Brough-Flemming management, with
Melbourne’s trusty friend Robert Brough in what
is asserted to be one of his happiest characters. A
few weeks ago that excellent comedian was a sick
man, and the great greeting in store for him will
have a note of hearty congratulation in its warm
burst of welcome.
#* * *
“ The Flood Tide,” a fine and fickle melo-
drama wherein the path of wickedness is strewn
with jokes, and the bloated capitalist carries his
boots upon his shoulders, came back to notice at
Melbourne Royal on Saturday. When Cecil
Raleigh writes a drama he turns some of the old
conventionalities inside out and mixes them
with new situations. “The Flood Tide”
makes very few appeals to the feelings of
persons who go to the theatre for cheap
and noble sentiments, but forget to take the goods
away with them. It doesn’t even punish the
guilty, save to the extent of leaving Miss Frances
Ross stranded on her luggage at a railway ter-
minus whilst the other characters—who had
mostly dropped into dishonesty and drink —are
going away to enjoy themselves at Paris. In fact,
the wages of sin are prosperity. Bland Holt, as
the fraudulent Thompson of the story, makes
half a million profit by the sale of a dia-
mond mine worth two hundred and fifty
thousand pounds, or less. Mrs. Bland Holt is the
female accomplice who intends to marry the
fraudulent Thompson after he becomes a half-
millionairer, and Miss Ireland is the other female
accomplice who expects to enter the bonds of
matrimony with the other half-millionaire. The
scenic effects in “The Flood Tide” are some of
Brunton’s best, also Walter Baker scores a spec-
tacular triumph by beginning his honeymoon in a
state of full regimentals.

An Adelaide correspondent :
Adelaide has had a chance of proving its claim to love
the Hig'hest when it sees it, by supporting “ The Walls of
Jericho ” through a.long season pong for Adelaide), and
has done it so enthus.astically that an advertised revival
of “The Passport” was dropped. “Jericho” even
improves on a second hearing. It lias its weak spots,
of course. Twice near the end it doesn't join its flats
neatly—so to speak ; and at the start two important
characters are given three,l,lies each, and then fade right
out of the play, though either one or the other is being
talked about all through. But these are trifles. The title
is the funniest thing about the play. Advertising
posters show An angel blowing* on a trumpet, and various
towers in the background toppling headlong ; and the
towers can’t be merely those of Jericho, because they
have illuminated clocks in them. But London doesn’t
turn* a hair when the Man from Queensland blows his
blast. His own wife, even, -flatly refuses to obey him, and
he is reduced to retiring* in a huff with the Baby to Goon-
diwindi, or thereabouts. A great play, all the same, and
the acting is excellent. Miss Major, as a duchess who
wins £4l at bridge and then retires with cold feet, is an
incidental delight; other merely occasional triflers are
played by half-a-dozen of Adelaide’s noted amateurs. The
season is drawing to a close with “Quality Street,” which
shows J. M. Barrie in a less fantastic mood than usual.
“ Melb.” : “ The Fires of St. John” baffled every prog-
nosticating Pitite (they have a Pit at that house, appro-
priate for such a devilish piece). The hypotheses were :
(1) Ricca Allen cannot be Nance’s mother; (2) Rankin
must be her father ; (3) she will shoot Robson; (4) she
will suicide. All wrong. Sudermann adopts Hamlet’s
last (stage) words: “ The rest is silence.” It is an easy-
way out.

The Wild Cat Column.
Treasurer Kidston, of Queensland, has done a
notable thing. His loan expenditure has been less than
any other Treasurer has shown for close upon 30 years :
and, in spite of the enormous load of interest, he has
escaped a deficit, and produces instead a surplus of
£15,995. Assuming that both lots of published figures
will stand audit, this achievement of the Labor Treasurer
is at least as big as Bent’s, in Victoria. The latter’s public
debt was £42 19s. 4d. per head at the end of 1904 ; Queens-
land’s, £79 11s. sd. ; for that year, Victoria’s interest
and charges were £1 15s. 9d. per head, Queensland s £2
19s. 6d. But that wasn’t all ; for Queensland's
deadweight of unproductive debt is vastly larger.
In 1904 Victoria had only to provide £439,605
of Cohen’s interest out of taxation ; Queensland,
with much less than half the population, had to provide
£959,403. In this respect the position this year has not
changed much in either State. Kidston’s scope for
retrenchment has therefore been much more limited (for
even a Scotch Treasurer can’t retrench Cohen), and his
success is therefore all the greater. Three years ago then-
Treasurer Cribb showed a deficit of £528,189 ; so that
liidston and Queensland between them have made up
leeway to the extent of £542,184, besides cutting
£987,020 off the loan expenditure.
* * * #
Financially N.S. W. has done pretty well during
the year just ended, and Treasurer Carruthers’ press
barrackers tell some magnificent untruths about it.
Sydney D.T. almost beats even its own record by printing
these headings
LIVING WITHIN OUR INCOME.
OUTGOINGS EXCEEDED BY HALF A MILLION.
PUBLIC DEBT REDUCED.
The alleged revenue was £11,514,328 (“alleged,” because
it includes about a million, the proceeds of land sales)
and the expenditure was £13,385,845. The ex-
penditure included £319,412 payments by statute
towards various sinking' funds. Deducting this,
we have £13,066,433 as the year’s expenditure,
against £11,514,328 received. Instead of “ living within
our income,” N.S. Wales has therefore spent £1,552,105 in
excess of its income. Instead of “ reducing the public
debt,” N.S. W. has increased its debt from £80,033,581 to
£82,522,913. Treasurer Carruthers, of course, makes an
arbitrary distinction between expenditure on revenue
account and expenditure on loan account. By charging
£1,940,348 to the latter, he shows a surplus of £187,174 on
the year’s transactions. Obviously, so long as there is a
huge loan account into which to shoot awkward items of
expenditure, no reliance whatever can be put on alleged
surpluses. They are large or small according to the elas-
ticity of the Treasurer’s conscience. And, anyhow, this
doesn’t affect the statement that the debt has been
reduced, which is simple, unadorned prevarication which-
ever way it is looked at.

During the year 3331 unallotted shares, 7s. 6d. paid, were
issued at a premium of Bs. ; the whole 25,000 shares have
now been issued, and all carry a liability of £3 17s. 6d.
The paid capital is now £9375. On the average capital,
together with reserves as at the commencement of the
year, the profits were at the rate of £l6 19s. 6d. per cent.
Last year the Perpetual of Sydney made £2O 6s. Od. per
cent/; but except that co. no trustee office in Australasia
improved, as the result of a whole year’s work, on the
Dunedin co.’s figures, though the Westralian Trustees
has done it for the past half-year. The dividend is
also the highest paid by any trustee co. in Australasia.
Its securities are represented by £1324 cash and deposits,
£5500 in debentures, and £10,234 on mortgage. It has
floating debts of £6262 owing, and floating balances
against it of £6102. The accounts show that to earn
£3860 in commissions in the past year, rents, salaries, and
office expenses amounted to £2091, whereas to earn £3540
in 1902-3 the same charges amounted to £2447. This is a
cheerful feature in a generally cheerful situation.
Bulletin has within the week received numer-
ous inquiries from angry insurers with the Mutual Lie
Association of Australasia, of which this, from Queei -
land, is a sample :—“ Will you explain the position of the
M.L.A.'? Five years ago it" reported that I was entitle,
to, say, £5O as bonuses, but now I learn that something
has gone wrong with the works, and besides giving n <•
nothing for the five years, they have reduced the £5O t< ,
say, £46. Is £4O coming next ? And after that chaos •
The M.L.A. formerly assumed that it would earn 4 per
cent. Later on it reduced this estimate to 3£ per cent,..
and iu some cases to 3 per cent. Three per cent, requires
a larg'er reserve to earn the amounts which the office
has contracted to pay on its policies than 4 per
cent, did ; therefore the reduction in assumed
rate called for larger additions to reserve than
had been made hitherto. When the notice was
issued that the bonuses amounted to, say, £5O, it
was accompanied bv the condition that it was to
be regarded as a provisional bonus, payable only in the
event of the maturing of the policy within five years ; if
policies lasted over the five years the next notice would
show the bonus calculated according to the reduced
assumed interest rate. Reduction of assumed rate (and
therefore of bonus) isn’t a sign of decay ; on the contrary ,
the sounder the office the lower, as a rule, the assumed
rate. While the reserves are being brought up to the
higher level, bonuses, of course, suffer ; but it is only a
temporary set-back, which is more than counterbalanced
by the increased security afforded. The action of the
M.L.A. doesn’t therefore" mean chaos ; it rather means
more prudent management.

Some Australian life-assurance managers howl
regularly against the taxation of their business, and have
given the Fat party the idea that it amounts to a crushing
impos tion unknown in civilised countries civilised
countries being, of course, all countries that haven’t any
Parliamentary Labor parties. The little Temperance and
General is a particularly large howler. Yet the annual
report <>f the Scottish Widows Fund, just received, shows
that, last year, on an income of £1,768,654 it paid
£33,966 in income-tax alone, whereas on an income
of £162,520 the Australian T. and G. only paid £1175
in taxes and license fees of every kind. If the Britis.i
office only had to pay as much as the Australian office,
it would have paid £12,780 instead of the £33,966 which
it actually had to pay ; or if the T. and G. had to pay as
much as’the Scottish Widows, the bill for income-tax
alone would Wave been £3llO, instead of £1175 lor
income-tax and all other sorts of taxes. The Australian
Fatman’s habit of howling against paying- the bdi
amounts to a positive disease.

The new electric light installation of the Mel-
bourne Exhibition Cycle Track, formed a great attraction
of the recent “ Austral ” meeting. The main feature of
the system is the “ Ganz ” brilliant arc lamp, forty of
which are distributed round the track, suspended on iron
brackets. The light is absolutely steady, soft and
diffused, doing away entirely with the injurious glare
common with other lamps and overcoming the objection-
able shadows in and under the globe. Special globes are
used which, while diffusing the light, do not cut off an
undue quantity and the effect is a light known
scientifically to be the best for all illuminating purposes.
The candle power is more than four times that furnished
by other lamps at the same current consumption. Electric
lighting by the “Ganz” arc lamp is cheaper than any
other form of illuminant, both in first cost, in fixing and
in maintenance. The contractors for this installation
were the Austral Otis Engineering Co., Ltd., corner
Hannah and Kavanagh streets, South Melbourne, Sole
Agents for Ganz and Co., Budapest, Hungary, who will
gladly furnish estimates and expert advice upon similar
or any electrical schemes.*

The British Broken Hill Silver Mining Co.,
after careful consideration of all types of boilers, have
placed an order with Babcock and Wilcox for three large
boilers, each of 500 h.p. Superheaters, chain grate
mechanical stokers and economiser have also been
ordered from the same firm. This now makes 40 boilers
of the Babcock and Wilcox type supplied to the various
mines and industries at Broken Hill. Wherever economy
in fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. Hence the recent unanimous decision of the
Expert Admiralty Committee to install Babcock boilers as
the standard for the British Navy.*

HY. MARKWALD,
Melbourne, Sydney, Adelaide, Brisbane, Fremantle
Auokland, Wellington, Dunedin, Christchurch.
The sober Vic. friendly societies are asking for
protection from the Cheap-Jack Royal-Festive-Dromedary
sort of society. The latter hasn’t raged in Victoria to
quite the same extent as it has in N.S.W., partly be-
cause of the greater influence of younger societies which
have had some regard for the fact that you can’t get a
quart out of a pint pot. Present Acts provide for a valua-
tion of assets and liabilities every five years ; and, though
that is a considerable check on the Cheap-Jack, it still
doesn’t stop the Cheap-Jack from going into business.
Also it doesn’t stop him from getting members, because a
considerable number of those who make use of friendly
societies wouldn’t know what a valuation report was if
they met it on a 6-inch plank. The Vic. societies
which don’t work on Cheap-Jack lines are, therefore,
asking that no society be registered in future unless its
contributions are certified to be adequate to provide the
promised benefits. But, after all, seeing that friendly
societies are not local concerns, and that membership is
transferable pretty well all over the Commonwealth, the
whole of this work of officially probing into insurance
business ought to be a Federal undertaking. And, of
course, it should not stop at the friendly society. The
insurance office calls for investigation just as loudly—
especially now that the insurance office struggles after
the fourpence-a-fortnight industrial policy. The right of
the Commonwealth to absolutely prohibit any insurance
office or friendly society trading under rates which seem
to the State insurance inspectors to mean smash and
disaster to the insurers or their dependants, will probably
take some fighting for ; but a generation which has got
quite used to the Plimsoll line on sea boats shouldn’t
have much difficulty in swallowing a Plimsoll line on the
vessels that carry the bread-and-butter of its widows and
children.
• * * *
There was talk in N.S.W. lately of some pro-
jected farmers’ mutual fire insurance associations; but
the proposal was met in the doorway with the news that
in Canada similar concerns had been a ghastly failure.
But as a matter of fact the ghastly failure stopped short
at the assessment concerns. Where the premium has
been fixed beforehand the associations have thrived;
where a levy has been made at the end of a season, the
farmer who hasn’t lost anything has grumbled
about having to part up to make good other men’s
losses. M.L. Farmers’ Union a little time ago sought
some information from Canada on the subject; and
the inspector of insurance in Ontario reported,
in reply, that there were in existence 71 mutual
co.’s, 33 of which had been going for from 20 to 30 years,
10 from 30 to 40 years, 10 from 40 to 50 years, and two
for more than 60 years—which is pretty good proof that,
as the inspector says, this style of insurance “ has long
since passed out of the experimental stage.” The foreign
fire insurance co. has a firm grip on the business in
Australia ; figures for the whole Commonwealth, or even
for a whole State, are not available, but it is reckoned
that nearly two-thirds of the whole business is done by
foreign offices. In Sydney metropolitan area £31,100,000
of the total of £73,083,000 of risks is held by the foreign
co.’s, notwithstanding that there are a couple of N.S.W.
offices which confine themselves almost exclusively to
metropolitan business. The mutual principle in life assur-
ance has secured very nearly the whole business in that
branch for Australian offices ; and it looks as though the
same principle will have to be applied to secure for
Australian offices the Australian fire insurance business.
And a State office would, of course, be merely an exten-
sion of the mutual idea.
* * * *
The row over the Equitable (which seems in a
fair way to be settled now that vice-president Hyde, the
cause of the trouble, has retired) has prompted the
Mutual Life of New York to invite the Superintendent of
the N.Y. Insurance Department to make a thorough
investigation of that office. The department, which
made a similar special investigation two years ago, in
addition to the ordinary annual examination, has accepted
the invitation. It’s a fortunate country that has a
department to protect insurers in this way. Australian
insurers have no protection beyond the mere say-so of the
offices themselves ; and the more shoddy the office, the
more worthless the say-so.

This co., which was struck first by the drought (when it
had to pay famine prices for its raw material) and then by
the glut (when it had to write off large sums from its
stocks bought at famine prices), is in a much less un-
healthy state than it was. Overdrafts, which stood at
£77,443 in 1903 and £64,952 in 1904, have been reduced to
£46,875 ; mortgages have moved from £16,850 in 1903 to
£14,050 in 1905; and other liabilities have been substan-
tially reduced, partly out of profits, partlv by reducing
stocks. Book debts (£25,469, against £24,286) are still a
heavy item. The principal asset consists of freeholds,
machinery, &c. ; the original purchase was £148,500, from
which, apparently, nothing whatever has been wr.tten off.
But, besides making additions (size undisclosed,) out of
profits, £2021 was written off the previous additions
made by the co. Why it should be necessary to
write down the value of additions, and why it isn’t
necessary to write down the value of the older original
assets of the same character, isn’t explained. The re-
serves are probably, in the circumstances, wholly or
largely nominal. In addition to the 56,000 shares issued
in part payment for the business, the co. issued 89,953
shares for which the public paid £1 each. Present value
is about 2s. This is another of those bloated manufac-
turing concerns that the Freetraders talk about.
* * * *
Bank of New Zealand shares have been quoted
lately at a price which gives only from £2 13s. 4d. to £3
per cent.—the lowest return required of any Australasian
security. Expectation of higher div. explains this. The
position is that, while the Assets Board exists, no more
than 5 per cent, can be paid. The Assets Board at March
31st had £403,000 worth (book value) of land to realise :
and it is expected this realisation will call for an aid of
£158,882 from the bank. As soon as that is paid, and the
Assets Board dissolved, divs. can be increased. Ordinary
capital is £422,674 ; pref. capital, £1,500,000. Latter must
participate in divs. over 5 per cent, to the extent of one-
half ; that is to say, if the ordinary shareholder gets 9
"per cent., prefs., held by the State, must get 7. But the
pref. shares cannot get more than 10 per cent.—which was
playing it rather low down on the State, unless the State’s
idea was that no bank ought to make more than 10 percent,
out of its customers ; and, if it has this belief, so far the
State hasn’t taken any steps to put it in force. In the
past year the profits, if distributed to the last penny,
would have paid 10 per cent, on pref. shares and 40 per
cent, on ordinary capital. But the bank has practically
no reserves ; so that a large slice of profits will no doubt
be retained to build up something substantial. And the
sliee may be larger than usual with the idea of paying off
£1,000,000 of the preference capital in nine years’ time.
• * *
Melbourne heard the last of old Jimmy Munro’s
Real Estate Bank last week, when the liquidator an-
nounced that the creditors had got 2s. 7£d. in the £, and
that there wasn’t a copper more left for them or the
shareholders. The shareholders have, in fact, paid
£140,247 in calls, and so provided about two-thirds of the
creditors’ poor little dividends. The bank reckoned its
properties worth £1,701,828 ; but after the mortgages had
been paid off (£821,685), these and the other assets panned
out for £30,999. Poor old Jimmy Munro was the great
financier who, only a few months before the smash, told
his annual meeting that the story that a call was pos-
sible was rot and humbug, that the properties were in
splendid order, and, though other mismanaged con-
cerns might get into difficulties and have to press
for calls, the Real Estate was the real Mackay
concerning which such things were unthinkable.
Another director followed that up with the assurance that
the whole £500,000 of capital was intact. Yet this awful
“ bank ” had been hopelessly rotten for two year;,.
Amongst its securities, by the way, they dug up parcels
of its own shares on which £51,000 had been lent. And
there are still some people who sigh for the good old
times before there was any Labor party, and when things
were going swimmingly—swimming down a cataract, so
to speak.

the whole or most of the uncalled capital will be required

cent, per annum from plant, the gas business shows a
profit at the rate of 10J per cent., and the electric light
business of 29 per cent, on the ayerag'e amount invested
in them ; and a return of that size seems to suggest some-
thing larger in the way of allowance for depreciation than
a yearly 5 per cent., even though it should mean pulling
down the div. a trifle below 33i per cent.
* * * »
More commotion down in the perfumed wool-
store area of Circular Quay, Sydney ; the Co-operative
Wool and Produce Co. and Dalget.y’s, on behalf of the
non-co-operators, have been throwing words at one
another. Both crowds profess warm affection for “ the
great wool-growing industry ” ; but there is the difference,
to start with, that one crowd lives by wool-growing and
the other on the wool-grower. What one crowd charges,
it charges itself; what the other crowd charges, it charges
somebody else. Tfie Co-op. wool-growing crowd is in-
terested in keeping rates down ; the other crowd, which
isn’t necessarily a wool-grower at all, is interested in
keeping rates up. And that naturally makes bad feeling.
The Co-op. lately decided to give the grower a 20 p.c.
bonus on his per cent, commission ; in other words, to
practically charge 2 per cent. ; and the other co. hits
back with the remark that it has only been charging 2 per
cent, for some time past, and that the Co.-op.’s claim to
a halo i 3, therefore, bogus. But, though the grow r er cer-
tainly only pays 2 per cent, directly in each case, the
Co.-op. scores when it affirms that it charges the buyer
2s. 9d. per bale," whereas non-co-operators charge Jd. per
lb., or, -say, 4s. 3d. per bale ; and it seems to be a pretty
solid fact that the grower has to pay that odd Is. 6d. per
bale—the wool-buyer doesn’t seem to be the sort of person
who would pay it out of his own pocket. The co.-opera-
tive principle struck a hard, cold Conservative quarter
when it pushed into the wool-selling business ; but the
best proof that it is able to hold up its end of the argu-
ment even there lies in the fact that it gets so much
attention from its non-co.-operating rivals in the business.

For many years the society wrote off a large amount
annually from profits to cover “doubtful personal bal-
ances ”—l9Ol, £793 ; 1902, £776 ; 1903, £682 ; 1904, £541.
This item has now disappeared, and the special writing-off
this year, in addition to the usual writing-off from con-
tingent reserve, is on account of repairs and additions to
properties. If this means that no further doubtful
balances have to be provided for, it will be extra-good
news for shareholders. The contingent reserve is appar-
ently used chiefly to maintain properties, and the charge
of close upon £5OO per year on that account will probably
be permanent. In some other particulars recent progress
has been as follows :
1898. 1900. 1904. 1905.
Deposits .. .. £5,906 £10,153 £12,389 £14,455
Loan Advances .. 31,849 35,305 37,441 38,649
Overdraft .. .. 1,464 3,054
Due on Mortgage .. 7,079 4,467 8,200 8,204
Cash Balance .. 777 1,737
The society has been seeking deposits, and has been
getting them, it looks, rather faster than it has been able
to find work for them. With fines, fees, &c., the average
loan brings in a gross 8 per cent. ; and there ought to be
a wide enough margin here for expenses and a bit over.
It’s a risky business, of course, this of borrowing to lend
on the cheap cottage that nobody seems to want if
the occupier once gets out of it; but while things go all
right there’s money in it. Management expenses are
creeping up—l9o3, £507 ; 1904, £561; 1905, £671. But if
it does its work and keeps clear of rocks, the building-
society management deserves liberal pay ; and possibly
this society is getting full value for the extra money.
With the increase of deposits it is certainly taking extra
chances.
* * * *
B.H. Prop, has made a three-years contract
with the Australian (German) Metal Co. for the sale of its
zinc product. Price will of course move with the zinc
market. Thus, in the absence of Australian industries,
the co. has to hand itself over to the tender mercies of
foreign buyers who can make the market just about what
they please.
* ♦ * *
Broken Hill Junction has given all hands
notice, and will try to make a recovery with the diamond
drill. It’s a suitable mine to try the drill in ; all the
same, not much good is likely to be done till co. sinks
deeper. But for a pure gamble, the shares at eighteen-
pence or thereabouts are probably the best on the
Barrier.
* * * *
Broken Hill metal recoveries by concentration :
South, 74 p.c. of the lead and 51 p.c. of the silver ; North,
73 and 51; British, 62 and 45 ; Block 10, 64 and 35 ;
Junction (now closed), 58 and 39 ; Junction North, 57and
32. B.H. Prop, and Sulphide smelt their own concen-
trates. The figures tell pretty well the milling quality
of the ore, though the South, for instance, has more
secondary processes than some of the others, and
therefore shows up better. Still, as its costs are
the lowest of this bunch (and these figures are not much
good unless they are read in conjunction with costs), it
easily heads the list. (As before remarked, B.H. Prop,
and Sulphide Corporation are notin this list.) Practically
no better recoveries are being made now than when mill-
ing started 10 years ago. There have been many improve-
ments, but either they have been in the direction of
greater economy, or they have been counterbalanced by
changes for the worse in the character of the ore handled ;
wherefore, it still stands as a poor makeshift of an extrac-
tion process which can’t get out, at most mines, more
than half of two metals, and can’t get out any at all of the
third metal.
* » * *
As to B.H. Junction North, it seems important
for investors to remember that, the magnetic process
having practically failed, the mine is in about the same
position now as it has been any time the last 10 years.
In 1896 and 1897 it paid eight divs. of 6d. each—nothing
before or since. It has ore, as it always has had ; but it
hasn’t got a process for turning it into cash on a basis
which will show regular divs.
Though the old Wickham and Bullock Island,
Newcastle, colliery is worked out, co. isn’t to disappear.
Co.’s paid capital was £104,370; apart from the mine, it
had plant, &c., supposed to be worth £22,000. Holders of
the 20,000 pref. shares, who are entitled to £1 per share
on liquidation, preferred to have their 205., or as much of
it as liquidation would yield ; ordinaries, of course,
wanted another mine, so as to retain their interest in th,e
assets. N.S.W. Equity Court has now decided against
prefs., holding that co. has a right to buy another mine ;
so presumably co. will go ahead with the Cessnock pur-
chase, already approved by shareholders.

Hillgrove Prop, shaft (N.S.W.) is now down
below 1250 feet (No. 10 level), and when the level is
opened out it will correspond with Baker’s Creek No. 14.
Developments below 14 will be closely watched by both
co.’s. Scare over the explosive ground, seems to have
subsided. List of serious accidents at Hillgrove is small
as compared with Broken Hill shows, into which the
public are fearlessly planking their cash. Gn strength of
recent good yields co. pays a div. of Is. in August, which
is ahead of time when directors led shareholders to
expect it Last crushing, 375 tons for 15390 z. ; but the
tonnage included 100 tons of screenings from the sorting
floors.
* * * *
Tumbarumba, in W. Lynes district (N.S.W.),
is having a little silver boom. The enthusiastic boomers
report that the lode has been traced five miles—about
three times as far as the main Broken Hill lode has been
proved, though when B.H. was as young as Tumbarumba,
it talked about five miles too. Picked stuff has gone from
5 to 40oz. silver and 13 to 63 per cent, lead —which is no
more than picked stuff ought to go. Assays are valueless
for practical purposes, though, and nothing in' the way of
a boom is justified until Cockle Creek ha 3 spoken. It is
28 miles from the 5-mile lode to the nearest railway sta-
tion, and a lot of gilt will wear off on a journey of that
length.
* * * *
Hunt’s Extended, Gaffney’s Greek (Vic.), after
fooling money away for years, has come to its senses, and
proposes to sink a main shaft in the basin of the dyke,
right underground that, years ago, paid several thousands
in divs. Present shaft is over 1000 ft. north of basin, and
t'ne gold dips south. Co. has a winding plant and battery
of the jam-tin sort; the only modern thing about it is the
whistle.
* * * •»
In the way of profitable low-grade mining,
Central Ellesmere, Fosterville ("Bendigo district), put up
Australia’s record last year. It crushed 11,500 tons,
which averaged ljdwt., and paid £314. The Carlisle, at
Bendigo, paid £3OOO out of 19,861 tons, which averaged
4dwt. sgr. And Bendigo doesn’t rely on either Ah Chow
or the Kanaka. When it was getting ioz. stuff, there
was a cry that the ore had become so poor that the cheap
Chinaman would have to be got in. But they got brains
in instead : and now they can pay on Hdwt. If they had
listened to the must-have-cheap-labor cry, it is pretty
certain that the improved methods wouldn’t have been
evolved, and the chances are that Joz. or so would still
have been the pay-limit.
• • • *
Victor’s quartz mine, in patchy Woods’ Point
(Vic.), has closed down. Claim has had a varied career.
A syndicate took up the ground fourteen years ago, but
had to abandon it after doing' 100 ft. or so of driving. Few
years later it was taken up by Victor Bros., who came on
payable stone after extending the drive 40ft. A battery
was erected and a couple of lower levels driven. In Nos.
1 and 2 the show paid well for some five years, but pay-
able stone was never found in No. 3 level. Crushings
went 2oz. for a start, but came down gradually to almost
as many dwt. Mine was fairly expensive to work, owing
to swelling nature of the ground and the amount of tim-
ber it required. If left shut up for long it is almost cer-
tain to close in. Co. owning parent mine, South Victor’s,
has wound up. It had one good crushing, 2670 z. from
150 tons. During last month or two it made calls,
although it had money, and only four men were employed.
Calls were probably to test the faith of shareholders. But
what is going to happen to the £6OO said to be in hand ?
* * * •
It may be cruel to ask the question, but will
someone give a reason why there should be a market for
South Star, Sebastopol Plateau (Vic.)? Co. has got £5OO
from the Government to carry down its shaft 500 ft., but
every cent of that and £2500 of calls have to go for that
work alone. Co. has a big liability to the bank apart from
this prospective debt to the Government, so there isn’t
much on which to make a market, even at ninepence. Of
course there may be flats who will relieve the people who
revived the venture of their shares. If so, they deserve
their fate. The proper course is to let the crowd hold
their scrip, and take their profits out of divs.—when th.y
come.
* f * *
Crinoline Extended is a new show up Donnelly’s
Creek way, Gippsland. Reef was cut recently, and said
to be 2ft. wide. Visitors reduced that measurement to
five inches, and there vvas a flurry on the part of one or
two to get out sharply. A little over-selling accompanied
the realising, and there was every chance of an interest-
ing share situation had not Brer Rabbit sounded the
alarm. Had the speckers been nipped, they would have
only got their deserts, so that this aspect of the situation
may be dismissed with regrets. It is quite another thing,
though, to keep visitors out of the mine. Nothing ought
to be hidden, even should the most captious critic come
along. If the show is good, it will live criticism down ; if
it is bad the sooner that is known the better.
* * * *
“Tadpoles” is the Stock Exchange name for
West Berry Consols, Ores wick (Vic.), chiefly because they
have tailed so far behind the other ventures on the Berry
lead. Now they have got into the West Loughlin pad-
dock, exchanged with the Madame Berry West, they have
got good gold and look like wiping off their overdraft once
again. This will be the third real start they have made
after divs., and as they are about to work a bend of the
lead, they may manage to accumulate a bit of cash. Their
plan should be to sink a new shaft, put on a big haulage
plant, and work the mine on a big scale.

Bendigo shows up somewhat less favorably for

Taking the three and a-half years shown in the table
together, the record is the best since the seventies ; and,
although the past six months’ work indicates a serious
decrease of nearly £90,000 in the dividends paid, the
general outlook is still satisfactory. The Eaglehawk mines
were, a while ago, the mainstay of the field, but now other
producers are emerging. For instance, the Catherine
Reef promises well, and it looks as if some of the .mines
south, on the New Chum line, may open out profitably.
Virginia also may replace the Great Northern, and if
position counts for anything Specimen Reef stands well.
Out of the big group of properties on the field, only 12
ranked as dividend payers last half-year. South New
Moon is an easy first with £44,800, New Moon next with
£24,000, then New Argus with £12,000, and Virginia heads
the rest of the contingent with £6500. It only needs the
New Chum Railway to get on to the gold on the new
formation at 4200 ft. to galvanise another set of mines
into life, just as those at Snob’s Hill have been vitalised
by the Catherine Reef strike.

Mount Morgan Co. (Q.) pays a div. of 3d. for
June, and proceeds with erection of furnaces to treat its
cupriferous stone. Exactly how Mt. Morgan will fare as
a copper proposition is not quite clear. Co. has not
appointed a man to run the furnaces, or, if it has, he has
not loomed up yet. Shepherd, of Chillagoe, is superin-
tending erection of furnaces ; but he is an engineer, not
a metallurgist. Looks as if co. will presently import a.
man to suit the plant. If he is procurable, well and
good ; but it would have been safer to secure a man to
design the plant to suit the stone. It’s far easier to
design the plant than the man.

WHISKY.
The moral of Slattery’s release. Put not
your trust funds in the hands of a solicitor. You
cannot even punish him if he misappropriates.
* *• * *
A lawyer writes :
The High Court quashed T. M. Slattery’s conviction for
larceny upon the ground that the circumstances in which
he received the moneys belonging to Mrs. Scanlon, which
he misappropriated, did not constitute him a bailee within
the meaning of sec. 125 of the Crimes Act, under which he
was convicted. Now sec. 177 of the Crimes Act provides
that no person accused of fraudulent misappropriation in
a fiduciary capacity can be convicted if, before being
charged, he disclosed his deficiency in any compulsory
proceedings against him. And, long before his prosecu-
tion was decided upon, Slattery consented to a decree
being made against him in the Equity Court for his
deficiency. No blame, therefore, can be attached to the
Crown Law authorities for their procedure. They had
either to charge Slattery w'ith larceny or let him alone.
The course which they adopted possessed the sanction of
previous practice, and precedents established by hitherto
unquestioned State Supreme Court decisions. It is simply
that there is a bad hole in the law, and Slattery has crept
through.
« * • *
At Hawthorn (Melb.), the other day,
while a burglar was operating on a house deserted
by its family, the milkman called, whereupon the
marauder appeared with a jug and said, “ A pint
and a half, please.” Thinking it over later
“milk-o” decided it was curious and spoke to the
Law. When the Law got there, however, the
burglar had departed with his bundle of loot, and
had drunk the milk.
If Thomas Bent carries out his stated in-
tention to buy a swift motor-car for the benefit
of the Melbourne police m chasing motorists who
break the speed regulations, there should presently
be great doings on the St. Kilda-road on Sunday
afternoons. There is some pretty sprinting done
there at present, but when the goggle-eyed youth
knows a speedy car is after him, and there is a
certainty of a fine if he be caught, and a chance of
immunity if he isn’t, the devil in him will
assuredly tempt him to give the policeman a run
for his money. And when the pair reach St.
Kilda Junction —where the road narrows and five
ways meet, and four tram lines converge—there
will some day be a notable smash, and somebody
will quit racing, there and then.
* * * »
The Englishman who knows England is
pretty often a - good deal better Australian than
the Australian is—the Australian, anyway, who
doesn’t know any other country, and has been
brought up to think of his own country as a poor,
miserable weakling which has to lean up against
some other country for just about everything.
Just when he was leaving Adelaide, Bishop
Harmer told the local parsons that before they
sent out of Australia for another bishop they ought
to quite make up their minds that there wasn’t
anybody in Australia whom they could fitly pour
into the gaiters. But the other day, when the
local parsons met to consider the question of a
boss, they jumped straight off into a motion
that the selection should be made by half-
a-dozen English bishops. Yet, when some in-
conspicuous person at the back drew their atten-
tion to Harmer’s strong advice, and suggested
that they should tell the bishops that they had
looked through Australia and could n't find the
material, they weren’t game to commit themselves
to anything of the sort. As a matter of fact, most
of them hadn’t looked, for fear they might see
some good Australian fit for the job.
A few doses of the medicine that the newly-
imported Bishop Clarke, of Melbourne, is giving
his parsons, may, however, do something in the
way of a cure. This importation who, when he
arrived, was boomed as much as if he had been
a great winter clearing-sale, and was generally
encouraged to regard the local product as poor,
slop-made goods only fit for the tuppeny counter,
lately sent to England for a man to fill the fattest
incumbency in the State. Now it turns out that
the new man is not only to have a fat incum-
bency, but is to be an archdeacon as well.
Whether the present archdeacon is to be sacked
or not isn’t clear; apparently, however, the new
billet is created just so that the imported person
may wear an extra yard of merveilleux, and
without any idea of his doing anything so common
as work in return for the glory. And when the
extra yard of merveilleux is trailed over the
humble Australian parsons, all that the real
Australian is likely to say about it is that he hopes
it will do them good.
* * * *
“ G. Elburg,” on the Norwegian vacancy :
Although the screw is good and the work very light,
the king business can hardiy be called a comfortable and
steady job. When once a king bloke loses his billet he
rarely gets another opportunity, the heads of the other
“houses” invariably having a long line of descendants
waiting for the situation as soon as it becomes vacant
through old age or dynamite. Now that Norway has
given its old man the sack, the royal unemployed will be
getting their crowns out of pawn and sending in their
references with applications for the job. But if the Ice
Country is wise it will have nothing to do with these
effete, in-bred old relics. In the beginning, the king was
elected by himself after a bit of trouble with the other
chap, and only stuck on his throne while too strong to be
pulled off. He was the physical boss of the land. The
Norwegians should invite applications from Sandow,
Hackenschmidt, and all kindred strong men, get them
into a ring, and let them argue it out, the survivor to get
the job. At even £1 per head the show would attract
immense audiences, and the proceeds would pay for the
new king’s crown and reigning apparatus, and then leave
him a trifle over for beer. Of course, as an alternative,
the billet might be raffled, but that would introduce the
gambling curse into a land which has hitherto been moral
and much addicted to a plain fish diet.
» * * *
George Reid let himself go as the whole-
souled philanthropist at a shivoo got up by Mel-
bourne Women’s National Club in Melbourne.
Fearful of having it thought that he was identi-
fying himself with the gilt-edged contingent at so
critical a moment, George launched out as the
benevolent patriarch—the father of the poor and
the lover of the lowly. He jibed at those Aus-
tralian people who prided themselves on their
aristocratic pretensions and their blue blood, till
many of the gilded dames present squirmed in
their seats. “ I value above all political creeds,
above all the Acts of Parliament in the world,
and above all other things, the personal cultiva-
tion by men and women of real love and a feeling
of sisterhood and brotherhood,” cried George,
with a little artful catch in his voice. Only to
regard the poor men and women they passed in
the streets as truly their sisters and brothers—
that, he said, was the really desirable thing, and
George carefully picked two tears off his cheek,
and put them in his pocket for future use. Now
George anxiously awaits proof that his affection
is reciprocated ; but it is probable that the people
will dissemble so badly at next election that their
conduct will almost amount to ingratitude.
* * *
A trio of sweaters at Kalgoorlie (W.A.)
have been sentenced to five years penal servitude.
They were sweaters of sovereigns—not of human
beings.
His “ right reverence ” Archbishop Carr
attended a church bazaar held in South Melbourne
Town Hall, the other day, and there was a large
assemblage of the faithful on the toepath to give
the dignitary a reception. But when the triumphal
Carr stepped down from his brougham, the public
demonstration was spoiled by a smudgy child of
the people, a little girl of seven, grimy with weep-
ing and treacle and accumulated dust, and dressed
like the street orphan in the melodrama, who took
the centre of the stage, with her knuckles in
her eyes, and howled. The dolorous youngster
was right in the track of the substantial churchman,
and he stopped to soothe her with a plump,
patriarchal hand, and a few well-chosen words.
His grace was told that the kiddie wanted a doll
she had seen in the bazaar, and wanted it badly.
“ Well,” said the Archbishop, “ she shall have it.
Come with me, little one.” He took the smudged
calamity by its wet, grimy paw, and led it into the
building, and the populace cheered wildly. With-
in Dr. Carr asked the youngster to point out the
doll, and the bedraggled infant led him straight
to a gorgeous creation inches higher than herself,
and behold the price was £5 ss. To the credit of
the church it must be recorded that the prelate
kept his promise, and the youngster tottered away
with her magnificent encumbrance.
One of Victoria’s amazing honorary magis-
trates is just now in an unaccustomed and very
unpleasant position. His business partner having,
as the result of a dispute, taken home the firm’s
books, and refused to deliver them up, this ama-
teur beak, who also happened to be a mayor, had
him arrested, in defiance of the objections of two
constables. He said he would “ take all responsi-
bility,” and that has turned out to be worth about
£250—£175 damages and costs. Besides which
Judge Eagleson handed him down some weighty
and insulting remarks to the effect that “it was
high time that men like him should be taught a
lesson in a vigorous fashion.” Yet, presumably,
this strange being will go on serving out “justice ”
to people who haven’t sufficient money to get
Judge Eagleson to make the further remarks that
the case calls for.

A family motto must be sometimes a
ghastly sort of thing to have about a house. A
London working man, giving evidence in a domes-
tic case the other day, swore that he had “ nearly
every day ” to interfere to prevent the Hon.
C. W. J. H. Blake from battering his wife. And
the Hon. Blake is the son of a baron, and his
family motto is “ Virtue alone ennobles.”
The quaintly-
I named Henry
Lewis Pehanagay
Don Hendrick has
transpired once
again in the Mel-
bourne courts. On
a former occasion
Henry Louis was
ordered to pay his
wife £1 a week for
maintenance. This
he did for two
years, then Pehan-
agay faded away
to Maoriland.
Lately Don Hend-
rick returned to
view the scenery,
and was arrested.
There is nothing
interesting about
him—even his name
is only unusual; it
is Mrs. Pehanagay
&c. who focuses at-
tention. He is a Cin-
galese, she a British-
er. She admitted in
cross-examination
that Don Hendrick
was her fourth hus-
band, the first being
still alive for all she
knew to the contrary.
She was first mar-
ried, she said, at the
age of 15, to Saun-
ders. Then, a few
years later, Byers
captured her young-
affections, the knot
being tied in Bris-
bane, and Saunders
being still alive. By-
ers speedily got “full
up,’’ and she hap-
pened to Sydney,
where another man
(name unknown) took
her to a registry
office, and made her
his unblushing bride,
Saunders being still
in the flesh and Byers
probably in the same
substance. What
occurred to the hus-
band whose name
was forgotten she
didn’t clearly remem -
ber, but in some
fashion he evapo-
rated. The lady ad-
mitted that she had
been young and fool-
ish when she commit-
ted bigamy and trig-
amy, but she assured the Court that when she en-
tered into quadrigamy with Henry Louis Pehanagay
Don Hendrick, at Geelong, she told him all. There
was no evidence to show that any more husbands
were in existence. His Honor A’Beckett decided
that there was proof that Don Hendrick had
married her, and that there was no proof that
Saunders was alive at the time, or indeed that
there ever was any Saunders or Byers or the other
man. So the jury convicted Pehanagay of desertion,
and as he was put in gaol the much-married wife is
no better off, unless the report of the case attracts
Byers or Saunders, who may come along and sup-
port her. On the other hand, they may proceed
against her for bigamy.
* • * *
The easy and unobtrusive manner in which
some incompetent prisoners escape from custody
in M.L. puts the hardened criminal and the old
gaol-breaker to shame. At Wellington lately a
policeman looked inquiringly at a person in the
prisoners’ room at the police court, and the young
fellow apologised for his intrusion. “Get out,”
said the copper angrily. He got out, but was
re-arrested next day. Same week a would-be
suicide, confined in Dunedin goal, was missed.
How he left is a mystery, but he appears to have
just sauntered away. He was on his road to
church when he was met by a warder.

The daily newspaper worship of Japanese
habits is having its effect in Australia. A white
woman at Bourke (N.S.W.) last week committed
suicide by hari-kari, using a pair of scissors to cut
herself open.
* * * *
Latest Jap idea is to hold a big naval
demonstration on the waters of the Yang-tse-
Kiang-—the giant Chinese river which can be navi-
gated for 200 miles from its mouth. This will
show the sleeping Chow what his little fighting
brother Bill is capable of, and will help the Con-
solidation of the Yellow Man against European
civilisation? Also Japan has taken in hand the
building of a new Chinese navy; and a future
English Drake may heroically insist on finishing
his whisky at the bowling club before starting out
to meet the Yellow Armada.

A SCOTCH AGONY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ma certes 1 Maisfcer Bulletin,
It seems tae me an awfu’ sin
That savages wi’ murky skin—
(A’ kinds o’ aliens)—
Should be allowed to grow akin
Tae white Australians.
I’m no mesel’ a native chiel,
But yet I wish the country weel,
An’, mon, I canna aye but feel
There’s unco danger
In letting come by ilka keel
The dusky stranger.
E’en noo, we see the ill effect
Of oor auld Mither State’s neglect
Tae mak’ some law that wad protect
Each honest neighbor,
Whose weelfare will, I trow, be wrecked
By alien labor.
Anither ill that grows apace,
And yin wi’ which we’re face to face,
Is the admixture o’ oor race
Wi’ bluid forbidden—
The strain to-day that we can trace
It can’t be hidden.
For, gin ye will but look aroon’,
Ye’ll see, amaist in ilka toon
Whaur dwell the Children o’ the Moon—
’Mong oor pet lammies—
Wee bairns o’ color whitey-broon,
Wi’ pale-faced mammies !
Guid kens, it grieves me unco sair
Tae think a lass wi’ features fair,
Twa rosy cheeks an’ gowden hair—
Sa blithe an’ bonnie—
Should gang, her lifelong lot tae share,
Wi’ heathen Johnnie ;
Or tell her parents choice she’d made —
That wedding plans had a’ been laid,
An’ Nick himsel’ could not dissuade
Or turn her from it,
For through Life’s stream she meant to wade
Wi’ Sheik Mahomet!
There’s lots o’ reasons I could gi’e
Why fair Australia should be free
Frae Afghan, Lascar and Chinee,
An’ mony ithers,
An’, for mysel’, I’ll no agree
Tae ca’ them brithers.
It’s vera weel for men tae preach—
In . ilpits, halls and parks gie speech—
An*' this stale doctrine strive tae teach :
“ All men are equal ” ;
But' Bar some races,” I wud screech,
“Or dread the sequel! ”
An’ n ', dear sir, although I lack
The S( ttish Bard’s poetic knack,
Upon this skirl don’t turn your back,
Though ’tis a mad yin—
We don’t want White Australia Black !
N.S.W.
Yours, JOCK McFADYEN.

While the Orient Co. was trying to squeeze
an extra £60,000 or so out of Australia for the
swift carriage of M‘Linen’s order for more calico,
.7 „-u i ,■ j ...
the whole importing crowd mentioned the alter-
native poundage rates as an antediluvian thing
that no self-respecting shipping co. regarded ex-
cept with loathing and contempt. But just at
that time a similar trouble cropped up in the West
Indies. The Royal Mail Steam Packet Co. had
had the European mail contract for 63 years, and
i t had grown to be such a very weary co. that the
Colonial Office proposed to give a rival line a
show. And then the R.M.S.P. Co. started bar-
racking for all it was worth that there shouldn’t
be any contract at all, but that the fair, just,
equitable, business-like, &c., poundage system,
which had been so successful elsewhere, should
be adopted. And in the end that was done,
Which shows what a hollow fraud all the agitation
in Australia against the poundage rate really was.

SOME HAZY RECOLLECTIONS OF THE SYDNEY ARTISTS’ BALL.
The operation epidemic has reached such
a stage that hospital idioms are passing into
common speech. In one particular household a
roly-poly was spoilt the other day—utterly spoilt,
by escaping from its bondage, and destroying its
identity in the boiling fluid. Suzette explained,
“ Oh, dear ! I forgot to put the operating-stitches
in ! ” The covering had not been sewn round the
raw material, and the pudding had become an
open wound, in a medical way of speaking.
Senator Macfarlane, of Tasmania, has not
tbusfar offered an apology to the people who
? uffered indignities on account of his awful local
importance in February last, neither has the sec.
of the Tasmanian Tourists’Association, who stated
their grievance to the Commissioner of Police at
Hobart, received any report as to whether certain
missing articles of Macfarlane underwear have
turned up in the wash, so to speak. There are
some irate Melbourne citizens who breathe heavily
when the name of the Senator is mentioned in
their presence. It seems that the aggrieved ones
chanced to sleep a night at a Port Arthur board-
ing-house what time the Macfarlanes were lodging
under the same roof. Early next morning the
four inen of the tourist party set out for Eagle-
hawk Neck, leaving two young lady friends to
follow later in the day. Just as the girls were
about to depart a policeman burst upon the scene,
and scared them with a demand to see their
luggage. Two. articles of frilled under-
clothing had disappeared from a Macfarlane
and these lost or mislaid garments
were to be recovered. The pair of distressed
innocents having come triumphantly through
their ordeal, the police inquiry was turned towards
the four male members of the Eaglehawk expedi-
tion, who were also suspected of having packed up
the missi linen b Lcident. When the lady
friends joined them at Taranna another policeman
boarded tbe boat and took the names and addresses
of the male suspects with a view to future and,
per haps, still more desperate proceedings. As far
as the six tourists were concerned the search for
the Macfarlane family belongings went no further
than thi but if the i tems in q B uestion have since
come to light it is “up against” the Senator to
publish the glad tidings,

Some hundreds of copies of the “ Record ”
(otherwise the Coronation) number of the Illus-
trated London News have lately been sold in
Melbourne, and the children of that city are re-
freshing their “ loyalty ” in the delusion that King
Edward is a lovely monarch of towering stature,
blessed with an equally colossal Heir Apparent,
It looks as though the shipment of those Record
numbers must have been postponed for three
years that they might not follow too close upon the
elevated heels of the actual Heir Apparent whose
appearance gave Australia so severe a shock.
If it listened very hard, Australia might
almost hear a row that has broken out among some
of its nearest neighbors—in the Dutch island of
Celebes, which isn’t as far from the back door of
the Commonwealth as Brisbane is from Melbourne.
The Sultan of Gau has had his own ideas about
who owns t' ;e island where the spice comes from,
and the Du', h propose to settle the argument with
50,000 troops or thereabouts. As the bigger East
Indian islands go, Celebes is a place of large
empty spaces, with only 30 people to the square
mile, against Java’s 600 ; and the Dutch soldier,
who is mostly Javanese, will probably get very
tired looking for the comparatively rare enemy.
