Little “ Bert ” Langford, the most excit-
able of Melbourne mining speculators, has been
charged at Launceston (Tas.) police court with
having climbed through a railway carriage win-
dow whilst the train was in alleged motion. A
natural defence in such a case would have been
that Langford didn’t know the thjng was in motion
—that what he had always known as motion in
Victoria was so different that he mistook this for
catalepsy, and rushed at the train to give it “first
aid.” This point seems to have been missed.
* # * *
The remarkable yarn that a Broken Hill
doctor told a local policeman, to the effect that a
business man had paid him £5O in notes to give
the business man’s wife an overdose from which
she would never recover when she had a tooth
pulled, broke up badly, even before it came to the
man’s turn to give his version of things. About
the best support the yarn could have had was the
pile of notes ; but they had been blown out of the
window or otherwise mislaid anyway, they
weren’t there and the police hadn’t seen them.
Also there wasn’t any visible hole in the man’s
not too large resources which looked as though it
might have yielded £5O. The wife-murdering
proposal was also alleged to have been made at a
pub., between beers; and the wife who was to be
murdered gave evidence that she wasn’t treated a
bit like a wife who seemed to be in the road ; and
what between this, and the beer, and the £5O that
wasn’t produced as a guarantee of good faith, the
remarkable case dropped through the large cracks
that gaped all around it.
* * * *
In a medical case recently heard in Sydney
before Judge Rogers a number of experts were in
attendance. After looking thoughtfully at their
feet around the court for a couple of hours,
Dr. Jones, house surgeon at the Prmce
Alfred Hospital, wished to get away. The
Judge wanted a reason, whereupon the medico
explained that he had many patients under
his care, some of whom might die. “Yes,” re-
plied the man on the Bench, “and they might
live through your absence.” Later on the same
witness was asked if, in chloroform cases, it was
usual to pay the second doctor a fee. The Judge
beamed, and told counsel that medical men were
all there when fees were about. Dr. Jones re-
torted that in matters of cold cash the doctors only
followed in the footsteps of the legal profession.
It was a weak retort. In the matter of hunger
after fees the legal profession is so far ahead that
the medical one can’t see it with a telescope.

“N.”: Booth’s Australian visit was all
business. His main anxiety was fixing up the
properties—he is the property-man of the skies.
An inquisitive reporter said to one of the myrmi-
dons : “ Of course English money went into the
immense Salvarmy Melbourne properties.” “Oh
dear no !” was the reply, “all collected locally.”
All made up by stalwart men and women abjectly
cadging for pennies every night. And stili they
rattle the begging box. A window at the Melb.
Salvarmy shop is now devoted to high-class and
very expensive boots and shoes, and another to
luxurious travelling portmanteaux.

The first thing the jury in the MTntosh-
Dun case, in Sydney, did, according to the yam
of one man who ought to know, was to write
down, each man, what he thought the damages
ought to be. Then an average was struck, and it
came out at something over £4OOO. But after that
they argued for about four hours, and the final
verdict (as far as the jury was concerned) was £BOO.
Decided by the High Court last week that
in the case of a debt which involves a “ tort,” the
aggrieved party cannot endeavor to “take it out
of his hide” as well as his pocket. A governess
secured a verdict against a squatter for slander.
Now a verdict for slander or libel, or for damages
for criminal conversation, does not come under the
heading of an ordinary debt; the debtor cannot
save himself from penal consequences by bank-
ruptcy. *He must go to gaol for not paying the debt,
even if he files his schedule. The squatter didn’t
pay. The governess thereupon put his estate into
bankruptcy. Not realising the debt in that
way, she attempted to take the other step—open
to her at first—of having him arrested. High
Court declares that that is not still open to her.
She had her choice to attack his estate or his body.
She chose the former and lost the right to tlie
latter, though if the man had sequestrated his
estate on his own initiative, and it had not sufficed
to pay her claim, the woman would have still had
the right to gaol him. The decision further
limits imprisonment for debt, even when the
debt is incurred by a tort—that is to say, is in the
nature of a fine for an action, which might, under
other circumstances, be punished with gaol.

Tiiere’s glory in the glint of wings
And beauty in each star ;
There’s poetry in meaner things,
Because you are.
Though fair to other eyes—to me
A lone and dreary spot
God’s glorious summer world would be,
If you were not.

* w *
Evidently there isn’t much demand for
soft drinks in rural South Aus. Years ago Mrs.
Evans, a sister of millionaire John Howard Angas,
erected a temperance hotel at Keyneton, near
Angaston, with the intention of keeping sinful
sellers of beer out of the neighborhood. Of late,
things haven’t been too bright, and at time of
writing, the tenant, who has been paying 3s. per
week for the house, has stated his intention of
leaving. It is now freely stated that anyone of
good character can rent the place for the small
sum of nothing per week until someone comes
along and offers to pay 2s. 6d.

all the chinaware bears pictures of Baden Powell

when you’re hewing your way through your

Powell lurking in the shadow of your cold corned
bullock. That restaurant would have been rushed
in cohentingent time.

near, and see this
y woman where she

Defiant still, despite gyved

There's an accursed sin all

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, July 4, 1905.
My Dear Arini.
The Artists’ Ball was a splendid success, and
the carnival was as fast and furious as a tortoise,
or a, broody hen. The Ladies’ Auxiliary arranged
their processions and sets in a way that deserves
the best descriptive adjectives. Both artists and
Auxiliary headed the processions with a handsome
girl. And the difference in the types of beauty
of the two girls was startling. Miss Irene
Marks, in white classical swathings, and a
long blue velvet train from the shoulders, looked
exuberantly Junoesque only the Absent-yet-
Present expression of pathos in her eyes
saved her charms from being wholly material-
istic. The artist girl—Miss Mackenna—carried
in a car, and under a shade of young
gums, was of a spirituelle mould and
suggested a Dryad. One of the best designs
in the room was White China. One piece—mascu
line—was marked “Cracked,” which was very
honest. In the artists’ fancy-dress section, the
best make-up was that of Mrs. Julian Ashton, as
a baby in long clothes, and legended “ While I
Live I Grow.” She actually looked in her close-
frilled cap and “comforter,” a giant baby, a few
months old.
After having “foregoed” their tableau of the
Haunted Czar on the ground that it would be an
insult to Nicholas, and therefore offensive to
the Governor-General, the artists allowed a
half-caste Japanese gentleman, got up to
resemble Togo, to lead a large bear
around the Hall all ball-time. An insult to the
Russian monarch was not to be tolerated, but an
insult to the Russian nation was of no account.
Anyhow, the ball was an immense financial
success.
Recently be-knighted Samuel M'Caughey is a
confirmed bachelor, but, as a millionaire and a
likeable man, he might have married almost any-
one he pleased. They tell that he was once show-
ing a Very attractive widow around his domain,
and was as pleasant and gallant as any lady could
wish. “ There’s only one thing this place wants,”
he added pathetically as the last bit of the bache-
lor homestead was shown. “ And what is that ? ”
said the widow, with a little fluttering at her
heart. “More water, ma’am ; more water.”
Mr. Justice Cohen’s family connections turned
out gloriously at the Montefiore Home ball, and
Mrs. D. B. Cohen made the finest diamond show.
She had a gown of “ moth’’-colored chiffon. I
have seen tropical-latitude moths that shone like
the head-lights of an engine, but the moth from
which Mrs. Cohen copied her gown, was grey.
Perhaps it was an old moth that had suffered a
good deal of anxiety. Mrs. Michaelis was also
beautifully greyed, with the addition of grey
velvet flowers appliqued on to her raiment. The
new Rabbi, Rev. Francis Lyon Cohen, and his
wife were present —the latter scrutinising the as-
semblage through long-handled glasses. An
authority tells me that quantities of uncommonly
beautiful and valuable lace were worn —the kind
of lace that you leave in the Safe Deposit with
your other valuables when you go away to see if
London stands in the same place. Another
achievement in attire was that of Miss Essie
Davis —a gown of amber and silver brocade, with
the new chromatic bead trimming, chiffon sprays,
and a corsage buckle (at least ten inches long)
worn lengthwise.
A lady of my acquaintance, in a neighboring
State, formally adopted a young girl, and gave the
mother, a respectable married woman, a big sum
on the deal. A fortnight later she sent the
child back to its mother with a letter saying that
nothing would induce her to keep such a little
fiend. None of the-servants would stand its
temper. It scratched, and bit, and screamed, and
swore ! Later, she confided to me that it tried to
ogle its adopted father. The child's mother re-
fused to take it back, and, after prolonged nego-
tiations, the foster mother actually paid another
sum to induce the real mother to re-adopt her own
infant.
A Sydney girl, writing from London at end of
last May, actually has something, good to say of
the weather. She had been there eleven days—
all fine ones. At the Hyde Park Hotel she ran
against Divorce Justice Simpson and wife, looking
very high-class English—not plain English. As
for Acting-Agent-General Coghlan, he was so
beautifully tailored that she was constrained to
,ask, “Does the creator of such symphonies also
compose clothes for women?” It looked like, a
suit that had been cut to slow music. Mr. Justice
Matthew Henry Stephen and wife and the (Dr.)
Taylor-Youngs were at the same hotel. There
were Sunday night concerts there, at which Amy
Simpson, who is doing well, was the singer. Now,
which of Australia’s vanished song-birds is Amy
Simpson ? I cannot place her. But the persons
who are keeping up Australia’s reputation as a
beauty culture ground are the David Anderson
girls. They and their mother, Paquin-gowned,
have surpassed themselves. The girls were the
rage at Nice during the Carnival, and artists asked
to be allowed to paint them.
A down South paper announces that a fashion-
able marriage “ will take place easily in October.”
Does this mean, I wonder, that the happy man
is only a mile or two ahead now, and the lady is
sure she will have no difficulty in overtaking him
by the date mentioned, provided she doesn’t fall
over a root ?
“The squatter’ad a little ram, its fleece was
w’ite as sneow” —Hie - rest, of the parody, droned
out by a blind singer near the gates of the Show
Ground, went on to tell how the wool-man sold
that ram for a thousand guineas. Sordid financial
details like this sully the whiteness of the metre of
Mary’s Little Lamb. Lady Northcote wrapped
herself in furs, and with His Ex. and Lady le
Hunte went to the ground and was instructed in the
Vermonts, Shropshires and crossbreds by President
Francis Suttor, who knows the subject well and
could make you giddy with baa-baa statistics. Her
Ex. seemed to relish the bleatful information that
was poured on her. But everybody is like that just
now. Sheep are in the air, so to speak, and you
inhale lamb when you breathe, and if you hear a
large, prosperous-looking man saying that Some-
thing is “ of a beautiful bright quality ’’ you know
it is sheep’s fleece, not woman’s hair.
A fortunate circumstance for Miss Leverrier
that her lines have fallen across those of a
musicianly Excellency General. Much Society
would care about the pianiste’s brilliancy and
finish if Lady Northcote didn’t tell it of these
qualifications in very determined tones —-and semi-
tones and over-tones. All the Sydney ladies
whose absence would make a blank in society
turned out to the Leverrier concert and took
parties with them. Mesdames Barton, Salomons,
Fairfax and M‘Millan were there, and the Federal
Government House persons sat on the right side
of the clock, and the Acting-State, party on the
left. Miss Leverrier, in sea-foam crepe de chine,
was handed on and off the stage by Mr. South-
well. Not to know Mr. Southwell is to argue
yourself outside the musical pale, and then you
might as well be dead, and if you are dead you
might as well be buried.
Mr. Lucius O’Brien, barrister-at-law, who died
recently of heart disease, was called in 18G7,
and practised for a short time, but gave up
law for science. He belonged to an old Sydney
family, which, after a career of trade in Lower
George-street, settled at Bondi Beach on a pro-
perty known as “The Homestead.” It has
attached to it a private mausoleum, in which Mr.
O’Brien’s remains are laid to rest.
A couple of N.S. Wales historic families joined
forces last week at West Maitland when Mr. E.
H. Robertson, son of the late J. B. R. Robertson,
of Oakhampton Park, and nephew of one-time
Premier John Robertson, was united to Miss
Jessica Agnes Jones, of Walla Walla. The bride
is grand-daughter of the late George Chisholm, of
Sydney. The Chisholms date from the earliest
days of N.S. Wales, and the pioneer Robertson
arrived in 1820.
Mr. Charles Norman, for some years sub-editor
of Sydney Daily Telegraph, has now returned
to his old profession of music, and takes
pupils for the piano and singing. Before taking
to journalism, Mr. Norman was organist and
choirmaster at St. Paul’s pro-Cathedral, Dunedin,
and was conspicuously successful there. Added
to his high musical qualifications, Mr. Norman
has a Paderewski crop of golden hair.
“Brescians” troupe of actors and singers, who
are coming here under Edwin Geach’s manage-
ment, by-and-bye, consist of four brothers mar-
ried to four sisters. I think this makes each man
a sort of brother-in-law to his own wife, but my
head began to ache before I had sorted it out.
The Redemptorines—an “enclosed order” of
nuns that Miss Madeline Toohey will join—do
not teach or go a-visiting the sick. They make
vestments and altar appointments for poor clergy
and chapels, and spend the rest of their waking
hours in prayers and meditation.
The Prestons, of Nestle’s Swiss milk agency,
who bought Mr. Justice Barton’s house, go to
England, probably by the Orontes. New arrivals,
the Piers-Hattons, are said to be the buyers of
that isolated, romantic place known as tbe “ Her-
mitage’ at Rose Bay. Another large-gardened
|and many-shrfibbed property, that of the late Dr.
Bowker, at Darling Point, has been leased by Mr.
Owen Cox. (If I fail at the ostrich industry I
ought to be able to turn a dishonest penny in a
house and land agency.)
Writes my Melbourne correspondent :
“They say a great deal in satire about the
widow, her brief season of weeds, and then her
sudden notice of life and man again; but a
widower of this city, high up in his profession
is one of the strong instances of the brisk widower.
Two days of funeral worries were quite' enough
mourning for him. Then friends switched off
their sympathetic sighs and slapped him on the
mourning clothes as a sign of good cheer.
“ A month or two ago Miss Florence Young
was expressing new sentiments re Australia—the
land she was so jubilant at leaving—and casting
affectionate glances this way. I took that as a
sign of disappointment in Uncle Sam’s Land and
its theatrical managers. That was right. Miss
Young has now definitely expressed a horror of
the low salaries and continuous bustle of stage-work
in the land of the sky-scraper, and has made a step
towards taking on starship again in the good old
country of the ’roo. They all say it in time, but
this comic opera warbler has said it sooner than
most.
“ The two Yank starlets revolving under the
Southern Cross at the present hour did not fall
into each other’s arms on meeting for the first
time. They simply bowed sweetly like two
Society rivals, and said sugared things in the dead
calm of the unexpected meeting.
“ One of the Fitzgerald clan—Richard of the
Caetlemaine stock—was married last month, to
Miss Florence Templar Hine. Ah interesting
romance in connection with the event was the
long term of the probation, during which time the
groom persuaded the fair one to follow the faith
of his fathers. Mr. Dick Fitzgerald is a plump
and prosperous member of the Stock Exchange.
“Society, mostly in a pill-box hat pendant over
the left eye, turned out strong to the opening of
the Mid-Winter racing carnival. Flemington
had rare weather for this event —blue and gold
without a break. The State Ex.’s clitnbed to
their pen and kept in the shade of the palms most
of the time. Some indigo-bluemountings relieved
the Grey Dame’s dressing of gloom. Two roly-
poly figures held rival kow-tows on the riles. John
Forrest was in fine fettle, but Georgie Porgie,
close by, clowned it bravely, and performed to the
Tfiorak flock as a Yorick of infinite jest. Dame
Forrest was dressed up to a thermometer of a cen-
tury in the shade, and not once did she add a wrap
to her frail muslins. Two jaunty widows—the
inevitable Dame Janet and Mrs. Wesley-Hall—
were foremost with velvets, the dowager wearing
eminence purple, and the younger Mrs., mole cor-
duroy. Miss Muriel Cumming, Mrs. Ernest
Raleigh and Mrs. Seton Williams were a trio in
the pill-box flight. The chubby girleen made a
mistake —the other two were safe. One should not
hang a toque of this order above a button nose.|
“ Girls have a horrid habit of growing up while
at boardinsr-schools. The other day, a skittish
mamma, who had not seen her eldest daughter for
over a year, was confronted by a massive young
woman—fat, sleek, fresh, and knowing. She wore
a dress to her ankles, and in school uniform dis-
played to the full her ample curves. The gay
young parent nearly fainted. Tears splashed
through her series of interrogations. ‘ What have
you done ? How old are you ? Fifteen ! Oh,
why did you grow like this ? And fat ! I was
never fat —never for an instant. I can’t trot you
out. Oh, Tripper, darling, what made you spring
up and burst out like this? Now, if I’d been
home, instead of gadding about with your father,
this wouldn’t have happened. Don't laugh, you
silly girl. I’d have boiled you down or had you
starved. Fancy one’s own daughter turning
round to make her mother old ! Tripper, you
have broken my heart.’ But Tripper laid her
Jonathan-apple cheek on the moist, pearl-pow-
dered face of her plaintive mamma, and probably
promised to iron herself out. Many mammas
suffer in the same way. The tragedy is a common
one.
“Serious motor tragedies in this part of the
globe are, so far, unknown. So the vagrant cur
which gives up its last bark to the toot-toot of the
motor is a matter of paper report, and a rooster
flattened out for spatchcock by its rubber tyres,
causes a sensation. Reason of this immunity, I
believe, is partly that the general run of motor
cars in Australia, so far, couldn’t get into trouble
except on a charge of ‘loitering in a public
thoroughfare.’ When real fliers come out there
will be some mortality, and an occasional news-
paper magnate will run over somebody of no im-
portance, as in England. But even with scorch-
ing motor cars, I can’t reckon that the Australian
will fall a victim to anything like the same ex-
tent as the folk of older lands. We have been
broken in by the trams, and the Flying Angels
Push, and other circumstances, to looking round
us when walking.
“The humors of the London mote-casualties
have really no end. A cyclist riding peacefully
along a wide thoroughfare, known as Bath-road,
was suddenly dismissed into space. When he
came to, the machine was lying with several ribs
broken, and a petrol chariot was raising a whirlwind
close to the horizon. As the victim was counting
out his injuries the charioteer whirred back to
offer him a shilling !
“ The variety of male millinery at the State
Parliament opening tickled the female observers,
whose toques, cart wheel hats, and ‘early Vic-
torians’ ’ were all eclipsed by this display of mascu-
line vanity. Gov. Talbot sported a full Spanish
rooster tail in his head-gear. The infant Aide
flaunted the tartans of the Buchanans (nothing to
do with the spirit firm), and his hat flew a mar-
vellous set of ostrich boa-ends. Private Sec.
Buxton wore a chimney-brush that pointed up-
wards. It looked half like a fashionable toque,
half like an annoyed cat. Bishop Clarke was
also there, decked in scarlet, purple and black,
but he wore nothing remarkable in the way of
bonnets. Premier Bent’s classic brow was, as
usual, robed in perspiration.
“Gov. Taibottle didn’t make a success of his
recital of the Parliamentary bill-of-fare for the
session. He*made several mistakes, touched his
helmet’s rim at the wrong places, corrected him-
self with aristocratic unconcern, touched his lid
anew, and sped on inarticulately.
“ For President Wrixon’s opening show society
folk gathered in strength. The hostess, who has to
perform double smiling duty at these rallies because
of ashy, absent-minded spouse (he hides behind
the tea-urn), welcomed her Toorak chums along
with the musty political clan. Lady Talbot was
present, dressed in stone-grey, with electric-blue
flushes, and she showed her condescension by
dropping her loi\-nette when she sugared her tea,
and conferred so\ne really attractive smiles on
Premier Bent. Mrs. Harry Payne was the
smartest dresser this day. . She was in the blues,
her severe deportment heavily whale-boned.
“At one point of that function, Mr. Tommy
Bent was asking the Bishop of Melbourne in a
loud voice what he meant by a certain letter
received a day or two previously. ‘ I’ve read it
again. What are you after?’ The Bishop threw
back his scarlet drape testily, and murmured
something. Tommy didn’t hear, and the Bishop
gave the matter up. It’s a difficult thing to
murmur effectually to a man built on the Bent
plan. How can a bishop get close to the ear of a
rounded Thomas without leaning over ? And a
bishop never leans over.
“The Federal Parliament opening clashed on
Wednesday with the Box-Mitchell marriage. The
few invited to both shared the shock of the brief
opening, grappled with its abruptness, worried
past the alleged joke of the thing to its smileless
sequel, and scuttled over to the wedding feast at
the Grand. As regards the Parliament show, the
three young hopes of the half-Premier came along
with Mrs. Reid. The two Reid laddies were in
kilts. The lady herself wore brown and white,
with plots of violets about the quarter-deck and
the look-out. Mrs. 'F. Clarke, under the wing of
her ma-in-matrimony, wore snowy-white with sky-
blue gleams here and there—more here than there.
These demure daughters-in-law are a droll study.
Several of the sort keep Toorak tickled.
“ The three dames to seize the purport of that
Commonwealth opening bon-bon were Mrs. Dun-
bar-Hooper, Mrs. C. Gavan Duffy, and Miss Tur-
ner (the Treasurer’s well-informed chick). These
had much ado explaining the light and airy jest
to those light-minded feminines who never listen,
but only look at chiffons. Other women supposed
to be ‘ up ’ in all the political games were not as
wise as usual this time, but they swam along the
tide of ejaculation with an enigmatical smile.
When in doubt or ignorance, a mysterious smile
is, like honesty, the best policy.
“The relatives and friends of Melba’s dad in-
vited to the Box-Mitchell wedding gathered first
at Scots Church, and afterwards at the Grand,
which was gay with a riot of greenery and flags.
The scenery was many times repeated by giant
mirrors, and was softly illuminated by artful pink
lamps of Magog height. The bridal attire was
usual but for the wonderful lace over-dress. Miss
Annie Mitchell has the Melba figure, her dominant
head-poise and iron reserve of feature. But in-
stead of Melba’s dark thatch, this sister has a
finer one of pale gold. A sister —Miss Dora
Mitchell—was one follower, and Miss Box,
sister of bridegroom Harry Box, another.
‘ The Cream Wedding ’ the Mitchell affair has
been dubbed. It might, with as much appropriate-
ness, be called ‘the Cheque Wedding.’ The
cheques lay—dozens of them —in sealed envelopes.
Melba’s, they say, will pay the dress bills for
quite a time. The new couple are going to reside
at ‘ Doonside ’ with dad.
“The one sad feature of the Federal opening
was the appearance of C. C. Kingston with the
Reps. The Tariff-framer was immaculately
dressed. A. bright buttonhole adorned his coat.
And he looked to strange eyes still a big force in a
meditative frame of mind. But to old, admiring
friends the long illness had affected the political
toiler sadly. It has taken the gruff heartiness
away and left that pale, solemn air that acute or
long suffering brings to sick Samsons.
“At present, the Bigjohn Forrests are located
in a green Garden-of-Eden suite of rooms at the
Grand. Visitors think that Roly Poly John sug-
gests a great over-grown Jonathan apple in his
gardenesque sitting-room.
“ A novel notion at a May marriage feast was
that the wedding bell, at a certain auspicious
moment, opened its floral mouth and the tongue
shed on the bride a shower of rose-petals. None
of the guests saw the mechanism working, and
were in bewilderment about the odd happening.
The old ladies translated it as meaning Heaven’s
blessing on the bridal head. But it wasn’t
Heaven—only stage-management, like the white
dove which fluttered over Westminster Abbey at
King Ned’s coronation, and supplied an auspicious
omen. (It also supplied pigeon pie to the second
gentleman’s gentleman of the Lord High Stick-
in-Waiting the next day, having played its little
part in the royal mummery.)
Yours Affectionately,

“ The Jew was really invented by a kind
Providence to account for every internal evil in
every nation.” —Israel Zangwill.
David Storey, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), has let
the cat out of the bag—his strong objections to
Socialism are founded on an erroneous belief that
the theory limits its disciples to one suit of clothes
and one hat in the year. Storey, who, as Barton
once said, owns ten thousand hats and not enough
brains to fill any of them, could never consent to
anything which limited the demand for hats.
The Wesfcralian Labor party was invited
the other day to curse Daglish’s syndicate railway
proposals, and wouldn't. Daglish’s plea is that
the wealth of Pilbarra district justifies a railway,
but that the State can’t afford to borrow the
money. All the same, he is willing to guarantee
the syndicate 3 per cent. ; so, if the railway pays,
the syndicate collars the profit, and if it doesn’t pay
the State humps the loss. The State, in short,
can’t afford to play two-up in the ordinary way ;
therefore Daglish decides that it should play two-
up with a man who uses a two-headed penny.
As for the idea that a State with, in proportion
to population, the largest revenue on earth, might
save up a little money and pay for its railway—
the politicians present would have died on the
spot if it had been suggested.


DEATH IN THE LUNGS.
Weak Lungs IVlacie Strong by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pilis.
BUT for Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills I should
be dead,” said Mrs. Margaret L.
Symons, Broken Hill. “My lungs
were in such a state that the doctors
were afraid of Consumption. They did their best
for me, but I grew thinner and weaker. When I
started Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills I was a regular
skeleton. I took seven boxes of them, and now I
am the picture of health.
“ The hot weather in Adelaide, Broken Hill,
and Hergott Springs, where I used to live, under-
mined my health for years,” Mrs. Symons went
on. “ After nursing my brother-in-law through
Pneumonia I broke right down. My own lungs
took the inflammation. I was a complete wreck.
There was hardly a drop of healthy, red blood in
my veins. And when the pneumonia was cured, I
found that I had some terrible internal disorder.
“Sharp, shooting pains ran through my back,
shoulders, side and stomach. Steaming hot
flannels gave me only a little relief. Then my
nerves broke down, and fits of hysteria made me
scream like mad. The ticking of the clock
sounded like the noise of the machinery at the
Proprietary mine. Even a footstep set me trem-
bling all over. My heart used to jump and
thump for no reason at all. The cuttir.-g pains
that darted through it made me think I would die
of Heart Disease. Even when sitting quietly I
have collapsed from sheer weakness.
“ Often, when trying to do a little housework,
my head suddenly started to swim, and I fell into
a dead faint,” Mrs. Symons added. “If I went
outside to sit in the sun, I would be so weak in a
few minutes that my husband would carry me in-
side like one dead. I was deadly pale, and as thin
as a skeleton. I could neither sleep nor eat. Life
was such a misery that I don’t know why I wanted
to live—yet when I heard how Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills had cured other cases like mine, I made up
my mind to try them.
“ I got a supply from Locke’s Store in Argent-
street N., and the first box gave me a better appe-
tite than I had had for years. I kept getting fresh
strength and energy with every dose. Gradually
the color crept back to my cheeks, and my cough
eased up. I could feel the blood racing through
my veins, right to the ends of my fingers and toes.
Every day I gained in weight and strength. To-
day T have more strength for my housework than
most women in Broken Hill. Mrs. Norris, of
Argent-street North, who used to help me in
housework, can toll you how completely Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills cured me. And, best of all, they
also cured my husband, when a heavy cold settled
on his lungs, and he, too, started to slip into Con-
sumption and Decline.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People cure
anaemia and the lirst signs of Consumption,
because they actually make new blood. In the
same way, they strike straight at the cause of all
other blood diseases, like rheumatism, neuralgia,
sciatica, backache, kidney and liver troubles,
biliousness, indigestion, debility, spinal weakness,
and the secret sufferings that only a woman
knows. But, of course, imitations or substitutes
-can’t cure you. You must get the genuine Dr.
Williams Pink Pills for Pale People—always in-
boxes, never in bottles. At all chemists and store-
keepers, or sent, post free, by Dr. Williams’
Medicine Co., Sydney, on receipt of price, 2s. 9d_
a box, six boxes 15s. 3d.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Mr. W. H. Wood, the new Chairman of
Committees in N.S.W. Assembly, kept a store at
Delegate (N.S.W.) before taking up politics.
Dead at Melbourne, Thomas Radclilfe,
Eight-Hour pioneer. In the early days he did a
lot of Government contracting, and he bore the
banner of the Plasterers’ Union in the first eight-
hour procession.
Sad news comes from Italy of the clever
painter Nerli, well known in Australasia. Soon
after succeeding to his father’s title (a marquisate)
and a substantial fortune, he died in an asylum
for the insane.
Grattan Grey, whom Maoriland, in its
blood-drunkenness, fired out of its Hansard staff
for expressing sane opinions about the S’African
unpleasantness, has been appointed editor of
Perth Morning Herald.
Sydney is beginning to grow a trifle tired
of the frequent information that J. B. Mimna,
solicitor, has applied to be released from Darling-
hurst. No man has a right to be quite so numerous
in the papers as J. B. Mimna.
T. M. Slattery is said to contemplate
applying to be restored to the rolls of the
Legislative Council and of the solicitors. After a
cursory glance at the rolls in question one is
inclined to ask “ Why not ?”
Pursey Chirnside, who has never done
anything but inherit and put to poor use 36,396
acres of the finest Victorian soil, has been made a
C.M.G. In his case C.M.G. evidently means
Champion Monopolist Grazier.
Tom Mann, the loose-jointed misfortune
of the Australian Labor movement, no longer
blathers Freetrade and such-like on behalf of the
Vic. Political Labor Council. He discourses on
his own irresponsibility, and takes up a collection.
Some time ago a rumor stated that the'
Vic. Government contemplated the appointment
of an expert mining representative in the Big
Smoke. Now, Peacock, of the Shriek, is reported
to be seriously considering a trip to the Cold
Country. H’m.
In 1883 Dan Cooper got a baronetcy for
his contribution to the Indian Relief Fund.
“Salmon” Wilson got a knighthood for giving a
big hall to the Melbourne University. Now Sam
M‘Caughey(pronouncedM‘Kaeky), the greatsheep
king, gets knighted for his £5OOO contribution
to the Bushman’s Contingent Fund. The price is
going down, but even now the title seems hardly
worth the money.

boards as an actor, and his old rnanagerhrt the

tinued in the profession. But at the best it is an

aotor W. N. Willis would have been leading a

odsend it is out here ! lam so far away from

The iron merchant, John Keep, who died
at Broughton Hall, Leichhardt, Sydney, on Sunday,
aged 81 years, was one of a large number of success-
ful merchants attracted to New South Wales in the
gold-fever days, about 1853. He commenced busi-
ness in George-street, next moved into Barrack-
street, and finally to the corner of King-street and
Clarence-street. Mr. Keep took no part in
politics, and, though he was a liberal man, he
never bought a title.

Although the fortune of politics has thrown upon my
side for the time the support of the wealthier members of
the community, their support— whilst I respect all sup-
port— is as nothing in my mind with the confidence and
support of the people generally.
The whole career of GReid may be summed up in

KEEPING ORDER.
Premier Bent has decided to supply the Melbourne police with a motor car, so that when
they see a motorist going too fast and endangering the public safety they can overtake him by
going faster still. And when the offender puts on an extra spurt, and the police put on another
extra spurt, and they both spurt some more, things will happen.
They have found in England a new use for
the King. It is remarked admiringly, by several
of the British papers, that, motoring from Reigate
to London (20 miles), King Ned took I j hours,
thus showing an example to his people not to
exceed the legal speed limit. Thus the motoring
habit provides another excuse for the continuance
of monarchy.
Paris Nesbit, K.C., has come to the con
elusion that sentiment is out of place in politics.
Had he stood for Adelaide or Torrens at the last
elections, as the Labor party was anxious for him
to do, he could have made a certainty of a seat ;
but he preferred to stand for Barossa—one of the
most Conservative districts in the State —because
he was born there. At next election Nesbit
intends to be a candidate for the Federal Parlia-
ment in the Labor interest.
A suitor for divorce in Melbourne is
Dr. Paul Wilkes Fraser, whom his wife shot
in the head at St. Kilda some six years ago.
Since a sympathetic jury accepted the lady’s tale
that the occurrence was a most unusual accident,
Kathleen Fraser has travelled a bit. In 1900 she
quitted Victoria for America, and on April 7th,
’O3, she wrote to the doctor informing him that
she had got a divorce from him in Mitchell,
U.S.A., on March 18th, and had made haste to
marry one Theodore de Budden on April Ist.
The lady next wrote to mention that Mr.
de Budden had been killed in a railway acci-
dent in the following July, and that she proposed
travelling America as a musician. Since then she
had penned brief epistles from Seattle, in the
North-West, and from a mining camp in Alaska,
where she was earning tea and toast by music and
by mining investments. Kathleen Fraser is evi-
dently a woman of parts—many parts—and there
is no saying when she may take it into her erratic
head to return to her loving ex-husband and claim
his affection once more; so perhaps it will be as
well for the ex-husband to have his divorce notice
gummed on the door for her to read before she
rings the bell.

A belated report of the wild Bendigo
function at which Premier Bent sang a comic song
reveals the fact that that remarkable statesman
also said “ Blow Captain Cook ! ” This is prob-
ably the first time Captain Cook has been publicly
“ bio wed,” and there is no knowing what depre-

“K.” : S.M. Herald of Saturday, in not-
ing some relics of old Sydney, dug up in Pitt-
street, at the corner of Underwood-street,
mentions the find as being in the neighborhood of
Reibey House, the “residence of Margaret
Catchpole.” Old Thomas Reibey, ex-Arch deacon
of Launceston, and once Premier of Tasmania,
will probably tear his old sermons in disgust at
this re-awakening of the silly old legend. Also
the Innes family in Sydney will not call round to
thank the Herald for the statement that their
great-grandmother was identical with the Suffolk
girl who illegally used a horse. As a matter of fact
it is very doubtful if Margaret Catchpole ever
lived outside Cobbold’s novel.
By a man who knew him :
The late explorer Augustus Ohas. Gregory was
connected with Australian interests as far back as 1837.
In his latter days he battled against tlie infirmity of old
age by pretty regular attendance at Queensland Upper
House, but his voice was so feeble that when he spoke
Hansard reporters got a speli. In politics Gregory was
nearly always opposed to the Government of the day
and regarded it as dangerously revolutionary. When
payment of members menaced the Tory opposition of
Bananaland and shocked its soul, Gregory constituted
himself a stumbling block by declaring that the
Appropriation Bill making provision for the charge could
not lawfully be passed, but his technical point fell through.
He was a bitter foe to Federation, and complained that
union would lower the credit of Australia and block
the Commonwealth from obtaining loan money. He
always had an eye to loan money. Always he put the
interests of the squattocracy first, and old Bananaland
politicans recollect the great fight, with Augustus and
his brother on one hand and the farmers on the other,
re the opening up of the rich Darling Downs. Even
though the settle! s succeeded they found to their
mortification that they were obliged to camp on the hills,
whence they looked down and saw the squatters gathering
huge estates on the rich black soil fiats —some of which
the Morgan Government has resumed to facilitate
closer settlement,. When Gregory was Surveyor-General
he told the Premier of the (lay that he could not find any
more land for settlement, and the Premier replied that
unless he found it Queensland would find a new Surveyor-
General. And it was while Gregory to a great extent
swayed the destinies of the settlers that one of his friends-
in the Q. Upper House declared that there was no land on
the Darling Downs fit to raise as much as a cabbage.
Australia has something to thank Gregory for as an
explorer ofvote, but Queensland has a big debit against
him becaCiseyj|'liis continued battle against progress and
reform, -jjeg&fm-y was a bachelor, and had been known,
to gsggjSSwerely about the declining birth-rate.

The virtuous determination of N.S.W-
police authorities to suppress betting shops by
holding the owner and not the tenant liable
barked its shins over a sharp snag when it found
the owners, in several flagrant Sydney cases, were
wealthy absentees. New legislation will be
awaited, and in the meantime virtuous determina-
tion will lie up for repairs. The simple process is
to fine the absentee £lOOO, and make his agent pay
it out of the rents.
THE ARTIFICIAL INFANT.
Mr. J. B. Burke, of the Cavendish Laboratory, Cambridge, while-
experimenting with radium and sterilised bouillon (broth), placed them
together in a test tube. The result was the development of cultures-
presenting appearances of life such as growth and subdivision. Mr.
Burke considers that the cultures fit exactly Herbert Spencer’s definition
of life.— Cablegram.
The population question is expected to enter into a fresh
phase shortly.

A tragedy happened at Magga (N.S.W,)
recently. John Hanes was sinking a well through
drift sand, which toppled in and buried him to
the waist. His son ran for assistance, and
fa e n r cim g nliX d l , A f s» h
fall completely buried him. A neighbor des-
cended and released Ins head. The sand kept
sliding down, the rescuer working frantically to
keep breathing space for his entombed friend.
what time the latter coolly gave directions for his
own release. Then the LL came dXn Sh a
run, and the tragedy was complete.
A relic of old Lagdom was unearthed al
West Dapto (N. S. W.) not long ago—two rust-eater
leg-irons, apparently 80 years old. They were ol
the hand-cuff type, with 12 inches of chain con-
nection, and as they were locked, it is assumed
that the wearer was buried in them. They were
unearthed at the site of an old quarry which was
worked by convicts before the year 1840. It was
from this quarry that the prisoners hewed the
stone forming the foundations of the bridge at
Brownsville. In 1842 Governor Gipps ordered
the demolition of all prisoners’ stockades or bar-
racks in the locality, and wiped away, so far as he
was able, the traditions of lagdom.

“ Nulla ” : Drought has nothing to do with
crows raiding orchards (B. 26/5/’O5), for drought is
the crows’ harvest-time. All the shrike family
(crows, magpies, butcher-birds, etc.) are fond of
fruit—apples and quinces especially. Parrots,
however, are extra rough on fruit in a dry time.
Green leeks used to be knocked out of trees in the
Cumberland district with a stick during the last
drought, and I’ve known a single cartridge account
for 23 in a cherry orchard near Orange. The gill-
bird is also bad on fruit, and around Sunny Corner
is known as the “ cherry-picker.” The sparrow is
specially fond of grapes, and the leather-head of
figs. [The crow is not a shrike. —Ed. B.]
“War E.” : From careful observation I
have concluded that the fox does kill lambs ; also
that the “ tongue-theory ” is more or less mythi-
cal. In eating a lamb, the fox starts on the head,
and eats his way to the heart and liver, leaving
the remainder of the carcase to the crows. In
cunning the fox isn’t a circumstance to the dingo.
I have poisoned scores of foxes with beef or
mutton baits thrown on an aniseed oil trail, and
have found that handling the baits makes no
difference. I once skinned a rabbit, rubbed in
strychnine with my hands, and scored three
foxes. Reynard is a timid animal, and easily
“bluffed.” A farmer who was losing lambs
nightly “belled” an old ewe in the flock, and no
more lambs went.

‘ ‘Marwood” : Recently on Paringa (W. A.)
mine the cage got away when hearing the landing
stage. Falling 1300 ft. it struck the water and re-
bounded like a cheery balloon, throwing the one
occupant upwards and sideways into a drive. Just
after it had thrown him into this refuge a cataract
of broken steel rope fell on the cage and obliter-
ated it. The shift boss came down expecting to
find only a hat and a red blotch, and his hair stood
up when a voice addressed him: “ Say, boss, if
you mean to keep me down here all day I hope
you’ll send some tucker.”

“ Ray X.” : One need not go to the alleged
heathen for superstitions in the wind-raising line.
White sailors, deep down in their
well-holes, have belief in such
things. They originated with the
first of navigators, who used to
promise young Japhet a ride on
the megalosaurus if he brought a
fair wind during his trick at the
wheel, or three pulls at the missing
link’s short tail if he brought her
two points nearer her true, course
for Mount Ararat. Such uncon-
scious belief is part of the sea,
and grows upon sailors as grow the
barnacles upon their vessel’s bottom.
No doubt it had influence oyer
even the wandering viking—like
the spirit of Eric the Red himself—
and it has similarly influenced all
shell-backs down to the present
day. As a nipper I have made
more than one voyage on a “ wind-
jammer,” and remember one skip-
per, who had special favorites
among the crew, whom he would—
when head' winds had driven him
to drink—send for, in the sure and
certain hope that they would bring
the vessel at least a few degrees
nearer her course before morning.
Many a good cigar and pound of
tobacco did he promise to give me
—when I grew up—for supposed
services in making her “ lav her
course ” after some previous Jonah
had “broken her off.” In fact,
although half-blinded by the flood
of light thrown upon this subject
by various native liars, I believe
in my own powers in this direction
as firmly as I believe in the powers
of George Reid to make a paper
surplus.
Yarruin • -Re gunpowder and boys.
Once saw a youngster putting half a flask of the
explosive into a stump on a patch of land then
being burnt off. Asked him what he was at; and
he said, “ Only going to give the cocky a bit of a
scare." Next day saw the cocky tiring that stump,
a few of his children playing around' it the while.
The powder didn’t happen to be there, a humble
instrument of Providence having removed it. The
boy was in a standing position in the distance, a
bumble instrument of Providence having hit him
so bard on the baggy section of the pants that he
couldn’t sit down with any comfort,

“ Okfcis ” : Note how our language, even,
points to the Darwinian hypothesis of man’s
descent. Witness the expression, “ That puts the
matter in a nutshell.” There can be little doubt
that this expression was evolved during a far-away
period, when man lived principally on nuts and
scratched himself in the tree-tops, and when a
nutshell was the most convenient thing for him to
put anything in. He probably kept his clothes in
nutshells then.

“Gip” : Riding through Monaro bush I
struck a strange, rudely-written inscription nailed
to a wattle-tree : “This is the tree
what Charlie Owen’s young horse
walked round for 9 days.” A
deep groove was worn
round the tree. I asked
the history of the affair
and was told that, a year
or so back, a man riding
a young horse was
thrown, and lay in-
sensible for some
time. He recovered
and got home, but
no trace could be
found of the horse.
On the ninth day
after the occur-
rence a stockman
riding by saw a
horse’s back pro-
truding from a hole
beside a wattle
tree. It was the
missing colt, re-
duced to skin and
bone, and in a state
of exhaustion. A rope attached to its neck had
become entangled in the tree, and by continuously
walking round, first one way and then t’other, lie
had contrived to wear out a trench from which
only his back and head protruded. The horse
recovered and is still alive.
* * * *
The discovery, by black trackers, of a girl
who had be,en roaming around for five days in the
bush, near Daylesford (Vic.), is said to have
caused much excitement among the birds of the
district. When the trackers were getting near to
the lost spinster they detected strange notes of
comment and information in the cries of the on-
looking birds. These sounds must have been
intended to give “ the office ” to'the blacks, or.to
warn the girl of their approach. Probably the
latter. The sympathies of untamed creatures are
with human wanderers at large.: If a hungry
jungle tiger had to choose between a lean, respec-
table citizen and a plump gipsy) it would go for
the scraggy person in the tall hat every time.

THE HOME OF THE BLACK SWAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
On leafy pools, by lonely deeps,
Fenced in by brown rock wall,
Whereon a mountain shadow sweeps,
A silence over all,
Far from the gales, the black swan sails,
Through rushes green and tall.
Ills borne is where the blue-winged teal
Oft hide the sun from sight;
Down where the misty shadows reel,
And, leaping in the night,
The black bream break, upon the lake,
The silver spray of light.
There on the edge of islands green,
His little cygnets play ;
Dodging the lilies, in between,
And all the live-long day,
The black duck rings, the reed bird sings,
And giant rushes sway.
From billabong to billabong,
Where glassy waters flow,
Aceomp’nied by the magpies’ song,
His young ones learn to row,
While fisher kings, with flashing wings,
Are passing to and fro.
Hound where the wagtail builds its nest,
The robin learns his hymn,
He sees the lyre-bird comb its breast,
The bronzed sw 7 allow skim,
While all around the spoonbills sound,
And darters dive and swim.
In golden light of noon-day sun,
On sandy shallow slides,
The snipe and spurwing plover run ;
And round the steeper sides
Toe damp rats creep. Down in the deep,
Ornithorhyncus glides.
In eucalyptian sweetness, where
The soft winds come to tell
Their story through the gum-tree’s hair,
Of mountain, tree, and w 7 ell,
He hears the call, the rise and fall,
Of the honey-eater’s bell.
There w'hen the day begins to die,
And dingoes prowl and creep,
His brood comes to his trumpet cry,
And out upon the deep,
From all his foes, the black swan rows
Among the stars to sleep.
VIC.
H. BUCHANAN.

“Dave H. R.” : Be “Cronje” and the
caterpillars (B. 15/6/’O5). “Cronje” says: “J
killed the leader. The next in command took the
lead ; then I killed him.” Why all this eagerness
to kill something, as if we were all undertakers or
butchers, or Red Indians out on a scalp-hunt ?
Couldn’t “Cronje,” if he was eager to try an ex-
periment, just as easily have removed the leading
caterpillar to temporary durance vile, or some
other convenient spot, and still have made his
valuable observations? Surely we have enough
jackass-bangers and lyre-bird extirpators in the
country without augmenting the bloodthirsty list
by the addition of obliterators of caterpillars !
With reference to the brand of caterpillar that
“Cronje” inquires about, it is the “white-haired”
or “spotted” variety—very common out-back.
They build huge hanging nests in myall, boree,
and other trees, and when the young are born they
instantly swarm over the tree and strip it of every
leaf and bit of bark. If a person in climbing one of
these trees happens to break a nest and receive,its
contents down his neck, the result is, almost
invariably, a series of large, red lumps, which
itch .to such an extent as almost to drive the
sufferer mad.
* * * *
“Janor”: To place snakes in the order
of-their deadliness (B. 15/6/ 0.5), I should say the
adder first, then the “ tiger.” Have seen a dog
die in 20 minutes after meeting the striped gentle-
man, and a man in three hours. Think the black
and brown tie for third place. “ San Remo ”is
right about the ignorance which prevails in the
bush re snake bite and its treatment. A man re-
cently bitten in Gippsland was treated by a
neighbor with a decoction of the seed fronds of
tree ferns. The patient recovered, because the
biter was a carpet snake—that is to say, he re-
covered from the beastly fern soup. But the
bush doctor took all the credit, because the popular
belief is that all snakes are poisonous. He is quite
proud of his remedy, and would rush with great
violence to treat the bite of an adder with the
same hog-wash.
■* * * *
“ Werrimee” : Be “San Remo” on snakes
(22/6/’O5). I have often seen the brown snake with
frogs, but never knew it to crush them with its
coils. I don’t think any of the smaller Australian
snakes crush anything ; small birds and animals
that are caught are certainly swallowed alive. I
once saw a green snake th ust its head into the
mortice of a road post to get a large green frog
that was crouched behind the end of the rail.
Having seized the prey, it immediately tried to
swallow it; but Batrachus objected, and puffed
himself up just as he got across the swallowing
apparatus, and thus pinned the snake in the mor-
tice till an aggressive agriculturist came out and
killed it. “San Remo” also speaks of a 40ft.
boa. Gunther (among other experts) says the
1 srgest specimens of boas and pythons seldom
exceed 20ft. And where did “ San Remo ” see a
17ft. carpet snake? M‘Cooey stated (Bulletin,
dO/l/’OO) that “ Morelia variegata. never attains a
greater length than 13ft., but Liasis childrenii
(rock snake) may attain to a length of 24ft.”
Against this, Krefft says: “An Australian rock
snake at 10ft. or lift, must be considered a mon-
ster. ... A diamond or carpet snake which
measures 10ft. in length may be more than 20
years old.” They don’t extend lengthways, as a
rule, after their 20th birthday. Most bushmen
measure by stepping; “San Remo” must take
very short steps. What is the longest snake killed
in Australia (measurements by Jim’s whip or Bill’s
feet not admitted) ?
> * * *
“ P.L.F.” : I once shot at and wounded a
big dingo, which made off down the mountain
side, falling every few yards. After chasing
him about 500 yards, I overtook him, and he
turned on me. While I was finding a stick he
kept biting at his foot, and when I had killed him
I found that he had nearly bitten off about three
inches of his leg. Now was he trying to eat him-
self so that I wouldn’t have him as a trophy ?
IN THE NEAR FUTURE—ONE OF JAPAN'S FIGHTING LINES WHEN
CHINA IS AWAKENED.
“The Prooshan ” : Be aboriginal methods
of trying suspects. The Edwards River tribe
places the accused on his knees, and gives him a
shield, which he is allowed to hold before him,
nose high. Those immediately concerned in his
prosecution then stand off a prescribed distance,
and throw nullas at all the physiognomy visible
above the culprit’s shield. On no account must he
cover in defence or flinch. If struck, he is ad-
judged guilty, and dealt with according to code,
provided the nulla leaves anything to be desired
in that respect, and it usually takes a good deal of
nulla to persuade a nigger’s head to die.

By “ Alex. Zander,” the Unreliable :
Re “B.” on the dugong (B. 22/6/’O5). “B” saj's the
dugong carries its young one under its flipper. He
evidently hasn’t seen a mothers’ meeting in Dugongland.
I have seen a party of ypung and old dugong ladies
sitting up in the shallows nursing their kiddies, and
discussing Georgie’s whooping cough or Araminta’s scar-
latina, and scandalising like ordinary Christians. I have
often seen them spanking their offspring with seaweed.
The dugong uses both flippers nursing its baby, like any
other woman. As to the assertion that the skin of the
dugong is about two inches thick—we caught an old
fellow one day whose hide was so thick that we had to
ringbark him with an axe.

“Long Un ” : I know an old fellow over
60 who has never worn a shirt of any kind for 20
years, and he says he has never had a cold during
that time.

“ Jim Pan : Just performed the night-
mareish two days and two nights continuous
coach journey from Broken Hilt to Milparinka.
The only decent expanse of green, herbage was
that which now lies in and round'Silver City,
Saw only three rabbits. Things must be bad
when bunny vanishes. The drought promises to
do for this North-West corner. Outbackers say
that there'll be no shearing this year unless rain
comes soon. One may travel for miles without
seeing even a wild cabbage patch. The only
animals that look at all well are the goats. They
feed on quartz and ironstone and red sand.

• * • *
Harry Stockdale re record jump by kan-
garoo : —•
I cannot say what is the record, but the animal’s jump-
ing powers are extraordinary. About 33 years ago I was
driving on Burrabogie station, on the way to Hay
(N.S.W.), with the late Mr. Dow, generally called
“ Jacky ” Dow, and we saw a “blue-flyer” approaching,
pursued by a dog. When the fugitive sighted our buggy,
it made a bound which fairly astonished us. Having a
tape with us, we measured the jump, and found that it
covered a few inches over 41 feet, thus beating the horse
record (Chandler’s) by 4ft. The kangaroo’s ordinary
bound is from 12 to 15 feet.

“Gip”: Last winter I tamed a magpie
sufficiently to make her eat from my hand. _ In
spring she took unto herself a mate and built in a
gum tree, handy to my hut. By degrees she
coaxed the old man to come to me too, and as
soon as her three babies were old enough she
proudly brought them down. Result, at day-
break, five maggies sit on my verandah-rail and
yell at me till I get up and feed them. If tire
youngsters follow in their mother’s footsteps, I’ll
have to get extra rations next summer.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“ Wonga ” : Some bush stores are very silent
places. The other day, whilst visiting a strange, dead-
looking- old township with two other girls for company, I
turned into the defunct-looking store to buy some sweets.
A man, lolling on the counter with a newspaper, rolled
off, grunting with surprise, and, after a suspicious in-
spection, advised me to knock hard, as he thought “ they
had some visitors away at the back.” I knocked hard,
and the man, who seemed an obliging sort of creature,
shouted and kicked hard on the counter. Out rushed a very
young male person looking much like an ant whose hill
has been poked. The other man introduced me with
a grunt and a jerk of his thumb. The ant spoke
politely. “Was I desirous of seeing Mr. Jones?” I
wanted to see the storekeeper, I said. He vanished.
Entered presently an astonished-looking man. Some
heads peeped round a distant door. I gathered up my
courage and boldly asked for sixpenn’orth of mixed
lollies. For an instant the proprietor gazed through my
head into space. Then, like a man who suddenly recalls
a long-forgotten habit, he dashed behind the counter, and
spread out his hands, with an alluring smile. He hadn’t
any mixed lollies, but he had any amount of flour, castor-
oil, and camp-ovens, if they would do.
* * * *
“Vulcan” : A man’s sister getting married, he
deferred giving her a present until she’d sorted out her
wedding haul and had found what was lacking. A clock
being a felt want, he purchased an expensive one from a
reputable Sydney jeweller, and, after paying for it, gave
directions for it to be sent on. Three months later his
sister wrote him firmly re his present’s non-arrival. Call-
ing on the jeweller hastily, he badly wanted to know
things. Jeweller was astounded ; he almost wept with
annoyance; the matter had completely escaped his
memory. A sister (or a near relative at most) would be
the one person who’d venture to write the sender in the
circumstances, and I begin to speculate on the number of
other presents which continue to lie snugly on the shop-
man’s shelf what time their givers face a secretly-resent-
ful bride in blissful Unconsciousness.
« » * *
“Uroka” sends this from Queensland as a true
bill The postmaster in Thurgomulla broke his only
lamp-glass, and by the light of his last candle wrote a
requisition for another, (getting no reply, he borrowed
a candle from the local policeman, and penned a
petition to be allowed to purchase a few candles. After
many days his first letter returned, marked in red
ink, '“P. Master, Thurgomulla, to report in tabular
form.” After much digging amongst old vouchers,
and more drawing on his imagination, the post-
master managed to concoct a fairly reason-
able-looking report. Having despatched this, he
proceeded to build for himself a “ fat lamp.”
He had only been burning his “ fat lamp ” a few months
when his application for candles pro tern, returned, with
a note in red ink, calling his attention to reg. 150,
“which distinctly states that officers of this de-
partment are expected to practise the most rigid
economy during the present year.” This letter was
marked B C—which didn’t mean “Before Christ,”
but “This letter to be returned.” He wondered how he
could have been such a fool as not to see such an apparent
thing as that at once. Nothing occurred to break
the monotony until one day tie was informed that he was
65 next year ; also he was required to show cause why he
shouldn ; t be sacked for the same. To take his place came
a spruce, up-to-date young postmaster, who found the
“fat lamp.” Of course, he immediately wrote to head
office. After a few months he got an answer—in the
shape of the original application for a lamp glass with
beautiful red notes all over it—“ To P.M. Thargomulla for
report,” “ Refer to Govt, storekeeper,” “ Price to be
obtained for this article,” “To be held over pending
return of P.M.G.,” “ Should have been applied for in the
annual requisition for stock”; and finally, “Vote ex-
hausted, to be held over till the end of the financial year.”
By the next mail came another communication. No other
than the old application for candles marked with a big
red “Approved.”
-X * * *
Light was thrown on the “splendid natural
resources ” of Sydney’s beautiful harbor lately, when a
man gave evidence that he made a living by sweeping the
harbor bottom for articles which had strayed overboard.
One man in Balmain, living on a water frontage, has
stocked his poultry yard from flotsam on the harbor
waters. Providence and the tide send him firewood ;
and a tempting dummy, erected on the general principles
of Aunt Sally, brings him coal from the ferry boats. One
day a porker appealed for assistance at the water edge,
and there was pork and rejoicing in the family for days ;
and a fish-trap provides his breakfast pretty regularly.
There used to be an “ industry ” in stealing rowing skiffs
at Manly, bringing them to Sydney, repainting and sell,ng
them ; but it doesn’t thrive nowadays.
* * * *
“ Gilrooney ” : T’other clay I met a peregrin-
ating picture-seller on the Great South-road. After
chatting awhile he grew confidential. “You see, I
get a list of the people I’m to call on during the day,
and of course to be forewarned is to be forearmed. When
I come to Murphy’s or Maloney’s I’m an exemplary
Catholic, selling only good Catholic pictures. Then
I call on Hicks, to whom I explain that I’m selling
Protestant pictures for my living. And when I meet
a pagan I sell him ‘ Love’s Young Dream ’ or ‘ The
Ballet Girl’s Bathroom.’ A man has to subdivide his
carry-all into half-a-dozen sections, and try to please
everybody.” Whereupon I told him I was a bitter and
persecuting Baptist, and for the sum of fifteen-pence
I bought a fascinating litho. of a maiden disrobing.
After telling him that he’d be sure to sell some Catholic
pictures to the next farmer (a whole-souled Orangeman),
and some pagan works of art to the grazier further on (a
sincere Catholic), I hugged the “Disrobing Maiden ” to
my heathen bosom and departed.
.* ■* * . •
“Gestan”: Lately an order was received in
Sydney, from America, for a number of young, live kan-
garoos. Intended to liberate them in some part not
adapted for raising cattle or sheep, and see how they get
on. If they thrive, more kangaroo, and a big and profit-
able business in skins, &c., is expected. But the Aus-
tralian trapper will wonder how the other fellow is going
to make a profit. Taking as a guide the average price
paid to Australian shooters and trappers, although it
might just about pay to get skins if ’rods were naturally
plentiful, it certainly would not pay to raise them. The
American trapper won’t, however, have to send his skins
to the other end of the earth before he reaches a market;
for Protection has built him a market in his own land.
That will save thousands of miles of cartage and about
five middlemen’s profits, and will give him a big pull over
the Australian until the Australian develops the Ameri-
can’s sense in tariff matters.
* * • . *
“8.W.”: A couple of months ago I saw an
advertisement from a Northern district employer in a
Sydney daily paper, and took the job at miserable wages.
Agreement was that if I didn’t come up to expectations
within six months I was to lose the advanced boat-fare. All
went grandly for two months ; then the owner of the place
decided that, to put him on the safe side, I should have
to repay my boat fare (already advanced) by instalments,
which would be refunded in a lump sum at the end of the
six months agreement. Had no option but to consent.
Just when the advanced amount was wiped off, but before
the six months were up, I was peremptorily sacked. Have
made inquiries and find that the practice is generally
carried out by gome up-country employers to take the
unfortunate down for his fare.

i “Cosmos ” : Some youngsters gathered outside
my window, t’other day. “ Play shops,” said the eldest
(about 6 years). “ I’ll'keep the shop, and you come to
buv things, and you don’t bring any money, and I’ll give
vtm some things, and you’ll say you’ll fetch the money
to-morrow, and I’ll ask you your name and you’ll give me
a wrong name, and when you have bought a lot of things
you’ll shift away and don’t pay me at all—see ! ” Oh, yes,
they’d all play: and thus the game commenced that
would last right through their lives.

“W.J.W.”: Strolling home rather late, met a
drunk who asked the hour. Whilst I felt for my watch
he was telling me what a good time he had spent; also he
was clean broke, and didn’t know how the devil he was
going to get home. Just then an idea struck him. He
had a bottle of whisky that he was taking home. “By
Jove, old chap,” he said, “you might (hie) do a fellow a
good turn (hie). Say, will you give me a cab fare for
this? It cost me flve-and-six. You can have it for four
bob.” I remonstrated that, as I neverdrankwhisky.it
was no good to me. However, he being insistent, I com-
promised with half-a-crown. Got home, opened the
neatly-capsuled, genuine-looking bottle of whisky, and
found colored water.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The anti-Socialism campaign is dead. Let
os now get to business.
N.S.W. Premier Carruthers speaks mov-
ingly to the Lands Commission about “the mis-
fortune of holding office.” The misfortune is not
all on his side. The country carries the larger
half of it.
An amending Arbitration Act may secure
preference for Australian unionists over Austra-
lian non-unionists; but only a higher tariff can
secure preference for Australian unionists over
foreign non-unionists.
With the retirement of Mr. Moule from
the “Usher of the Black Rod” business, N.S.W.
Premier Carruthers had an opportunity to quietly
drop the silly mummery into oblivion. But a
new appointment to the useless office has been
made by this “Reform” Government.
At N.S.W. State general election of 1898,
George Reid directed that his party should not
put a single candidate into the field to contest an
electorate against a Labor candidate. For the
forthcoming Federal elections Yes-No calls on his
party to fight the Labor candidate in every
electorate.
Thus a Crow-eater pressman :
It is very difficult to predict what will happen when
Premier Butler meets the S.A. Parliament. On paper the
Labor (15) and Liberal (9) parties have 24 votes against
any combination which the Conservatives and the Govern-
ment can possibly arrange. But politicians in South
Australia are shifty cattle, and the only organisation one
can be sure of is the Labor party. Butler is confident
that he can get enough supporters to his standard —the
banner of the turned coat and the political wild cat —to
worry through with, although nobody else thinks so. Still
Jenkins, in last Parliament, existed on a majority of one
for two sessions.
EXPLAINED.
SMALL BOY: “ Dad , why's Mr. Reid always got
a dog with him? ”
DAD (taken unawares): “For Protection, my
son.”
“ Revenue proper : Centennial Park land
sales, £4576,” says Reformer Carruthers’ revenue
statement—the statement on which he bases the
yarn that he has a surplus of £187,173. These
Centennial Park lands were actually bought with
borrowed money, and the loan is still as good as
new ! And yet it’s “ revenue proper.”
There is a Bill before the Federal Parlia-
ment to exempt from service on jurifis various
gublic officials, beginning with the Governor-
eneral and ending with the Commonwealth
public service clerks. From this, it would appear
that, until the Bill becomes an Act. Lord North-
cote or Chief Justice Griffith is liable to be called
upon to attend at the Coroner’s Court and “ sit on
the body.”
Last week a very violent letter, signed
“Rupert Clarke,” appeared in S. M. Herald,
denouncing the “enormous freight concessions”
made to the Eskbank Ironworks (Lithgow,
N.S.W.). Yet even after all these “enormous
freight concessions” the Eskbank concern paid
higher rates than the N.S.W. farmer pays
for the carriage of his produce. And with all this
no one writes to the Herald denouncing the
farmer as a parasite who lives on the community ;
instead, he looms up as the strong, manly, inde-
pendent backbone of the country. Truly it is a
crime to be a manufacturer in Freetrade-sodden
N.S.W,
If the coming Labor conference doesn’t
absolutely plump for Protection as an essential
plank of the Federal Labor policy, it is more than
probable that a fierce battle will rage over the
question as to “why not?” Of S.A.’s six selected
delegates, five are Protectionists. Victoria’s will
certainly be all of a like fiscal faith. Queensland’s
ditto, W.A.’s two-thirds likewise, and at least
one-third of N.S.W.’s probable delegates will be
the same. Seeing which, if the Protectionists will
but fire the question at the conference, and then
keep a good fierce draught going, it’s as certain
as anything can be before the event that the ques-
tion will emerge in a red-hot affirmative. When
the Labor party, as a party, says it has no fiscal
beliefs, it only means that it hasn t any on some
things. Writing to the secretary of Ballina,
(N.S.W.) Labor League on Friday last, Leader
Watson stated that the party, as a party, “had
determined to secure an extension of the sugar
bonus.” Thus the Labor party, as a party, has
a fiscal belief on some things.
Sydney Telegraph is deliriously angry
over Leak in’s upsetting of the coalition cart. It
speaks of his “ fratricidal conspiracy, ” of his
“ confidence trick,” and of his “false part,” and
of his “unworthy political trickery.” A year or
two ago Dealcin was to the Telegraph
something close up to a murderer and a
drowner of harmless nigger sailors —the sailors
who didn’t even get wet except with spray.
Yesterweek he was a high-minded patriot.
To-day he is a devil. But Leakin’s attitude has
been, ever since Reid announced his anti-Socialism
campaign, candid enough. He refused to join
that campaign, though Reid made it the pivot of
his policy ; that was a straight enough assurance
that the coalition, so far as he was concerned, was
“off.” Reid didn’t take it in that light because
it didn’t suit his purpose to do so, clinging des-
perately, as he did, to the hope that Deakin might
be again bamboozled or threatened into com-
plaisance. But it was just as apparent, three
months ago, that Deakin had broken with Reid
as it is now.
The attitude of the in-
habitants of the Flyspeck State
towards the White Australia
question is an amusing
mystery. They have
had no experience
whatever of black
labor, and, so far as
their own apple-
orchard is con-
cerned, the mat-
ter is of no im-
portance to them.
Farm-laborers on
that little island al-
ready work double
the hours for half
the wages paid for
similar work on
the mainland, and
the nigger is not
yet discovered who
will pull his fore-
lock more humbly
and respectfully to
his boss than the
Tasmanian native
who follows the

10s. per week.
Even the shearer
—the bellicose
brigand whom
the mainland
squatter is sup-
posed to keep off
with difficulty by
means of an axe
—is, in Tassy, a
subdued serf who
handles his mas-
ter’s sheep re-
spectfully at 10s.
per hundred, and
never grumbles
at the tucker.
Yet three-fourths
of the “ni c e
people” in Van
liobson Land
grow quite indig-
nant at the idea of keeping out
the poor dear blacks and the
humble Chinkies.

There is no place in the known world—for climate, soil,

fiscal-sinking .party—for his chief support, Aus-
tralia would' be in this weird position : It would
be governed by a party of. say, 42, of whom about
23 would disclaim all responsibility as regards the
biggest issue in Australian politics, ihe lJ or so
who accepted responsibility wouldn t be numerous
enough to do anything. As the shortest way out,
I suggest that the Protectionist party should re-
fuse to take office unless it is assured of cordial
support from the Labor party m a High l rotec-
tion policy. If Watson takes office the price of
the Df i kin-Isaacs party s support should be the
adopt.on by Watson and Co. of a High I rotection
policy. If Reid gets office again the Protection-
ists should join Watson in throwing him out, and
then resume the old attitude. Then the Labor
party must either adopt Protection (which is easy)
or join Reid (which is impossible). In short, the
Protectionist party should offer cordial support in
return for concessions, and should put, its High
Tariff down with a crash and say: “ I hat s the
concession.” __
HIS NEW PUP -THE SOCIALISTIC RED HERRING
It is now stated that N.S.W. Premier
Carruthers has carefully selected Judge Heydcm,
the most bitter and uncompromising Tory on the
State bench, to preside over the Arbitration
Court. The new President is a man who regards
the Arbitration law as a huge and unmitigated
evil, and however hard he tries to attain absolute
fairness he will be in the position of a violent
Orangeman trying to take an absolutely unbiassed
view°of the Pope’s character, or an importer
endeavoring to lay aside all prejudices m con-
sidering a Protectionist tariff. And it is commonly
supposed that these were just the reasons why
Premier Carruthers reckoned Judge Heydon the
man for the position.
Curiously enough, in the published pro-
gramme of measures for the new Federal session—
the programme which was abandoned after Allied
Deakin’s Ballarat speech—there wasn’t a mention
of the Anti-Socialist Bill, or the Bill for- Dis-
couraging Socialism, •or the Bill to Repeal the
Socialistic Errors of the Past. This world is full
of disappointments. For instance, the obscure
Wilks has held his seat all along on his opposition
to Roman Catholicism, and he hasn’t .introduced
one Bill or one motion-: to do anything to make
Rome less rampant than it was before. Can it fie
that anti-Socialism and anti-Catholicism are only
the devices of dull, empty men, who have no con-
structive ability, and have to live in a political
sense by being “ agin’ something easy
Already the beneficial results of the Butter
Commission are being felt. Butter freights
to London are now down to three
eighths of a penny per lb., and the
loss of the trade by the mail companies
has set them “cutting” rabbit freights.
Whereas 70s. to 80s. per ton was
charged two years ago, and 40s. to
60s. last season, the Oroya took oOOU
crates from Melbourne last week at
20s. per ton, equal to about lid. per
crate. But the fruit business is still
where it was, last season’s figures
being from 655. to 775. 6d. per ton from
Melbourne to London —yet both fruit
and butter keep better than rabbits.
Of course, the 20s. for rabbits is only
a “cut’ ; as soon as the mail com-
panies and the lines going round the
Cape come to an understanding,
bunny freights will go to about the
same old figure, unless the Common-
wealth takes a hand or Bent builds a
few of what he calls “blooming ships.
Will some Freetrader with a
logical habit of mind, say Fowler or
Clemons, explain how it oomes. that
he and his like persistently bellow
to the effect that a tariff creates
trusts, rings, combines. &c., yet want
the Commonwealth tariff kept at the
lowest possible figure in the interests
and for the direct advantage of that
mercenary, soulless combine, the k tan-
dard Oil Trust, which is identical with
the Harvester Combine? Is it only
the possible Australian trust that must be
squelched, while the actual foreign trust must be
aided by every underhand device possible .
Joseph Cook, has been holding
forth at Albury (N.S.W.) about how the Labor
party’s Socialism means Destitution with a stu-
pendous “D,” and Degradation with a D i 50ft
high. Joseph Cook was himself about the most
aggressive member of the N.S.W. Labor party
in Its early Socialistic days, till Reid offered him a
portfolio. Then he sold himself and his principles
for office—sold himself openly and publicly, like a
cow in a paddock, or a horse in a sale-yard—and
got his price. As a Minister he rs commonly re-
puted to have been an economical man who lived
on the perfume of an oil-rag until he saved enough
to be independent. And now there isn t a build-
in"- in Sydney large enough to hold the D
with which Joseph Cook defines the Depadation
that arises from his old faith—the faith that he
sold.

Yic. Assembly has been formally notified
of Mr. Outtrim’s intention to introduce a Bill to
prohibit the use of tobacco, cigars, and _ cigarettes-
by children. An Anti-Juvenile Smoking Act is
widely called for; moreover, it promises good
sport. Every good citizen, male or female, will
perceive in every smoking street urchin a reflec-
tion of his or her child, and will grab the little
sinner by the collar, and drag him to the nearest
policeman, or smite him on the “ior” and steal
his cigarettes. Then will the infant Jim battle
hard for his besetting sin, and the King’s High-
way will be enlivened by active hostilities.
Recently five questions were asked in
N.S.W. Assembly about the height of the foot-
boards in the Ocean-street (Sydney) trams. Yet,
for a while past, Premier Carruthers has been
telling a wild tale in the press to the effect that,
having paid off about £320,000 of the public debt
out of- revenue, he has reduced the debt this de-
spite the fact that he has borrowed another
£2,500,000. This seems a matter calling for some
explanation, but not one question has been asked.
The same Assembly appointed a Select Committee
in 1901 to inquire into the suspension of Roberts,
bookbinder in the Registrar-General’s Depart-
ment ; and another Committee in 1902 ; and it is
now invited to appoint a third Committee in 1905.
Yet it hasn’t appointed one Committee to find
out why about £1,000,000 a year, raised by the
sale of the public estate, is used as revenue, though
this sort of fraud has been going on for half a cen-
tury or more.
ALL THAT IS LEFT OF THEM.
Lyne, the other day, pointed out that
Socialistic enterprises have not destroyed indivi-
dual effort; and Sydney Telegraph tells him
that “he would find it difficult to name those
enterprises ”
If he refers to the railways and tramways, we must
remember that the railways largely wiped the teamster
off the country roads, and that the tramways wiped out
the Sydney omnibuses.
The sins of Socialism are mounting up ; the dis-
appearance of the bullock-dray and Mr. Randal
Carey's ’bus-horse is now added to its other
abominations. Still, a party that reckons upon
winning behind a bullock and a bus-horse_—even.a
Randal Carey ’bus-horse—does seem as though it
might get rather badly left in 1905.
