WILL CRIMINALS ESCAPE DETECTION
BY REMARKABLE DISCOVERY OF
ROCHESTER SCIENTIST?
Man Increases Height and Cheats Bertiflon System,
HOW ANYONE MAY GROW TALL
United States Government Official Offers Himself as
Proof that the Height an be Increased.
SEVERAL INCHES MAY EASILY BE ADDED BY
MR. MINQES’ NEW SYSTEH.—HOW
IT IS DONE.
(Special Correspondence.)
Brighton, N.Y., February 4.—At last, after
years of study and endless scientific experiments
for increasing the height of those who are so un-
fortunate as to be short in stature, a Rochester
scientist has solved the problem, and given to the
world a greater gift than Edison’s electric light;
Marconi’s wireless telegraphy; or the Bell tele-
phone ; for what can be more inconvenient or pro-
ductive of more discomfort, than to be short ? The
short man or woman is always at a disad-
vantage; in business, society, on the stage or
rostrum. The great orators, statesmen, gene-
rals, presidents, have rarely been short men.
y/y,
'l
4
l
Mr. K. Leo Minges, a Rochester
Scientist, who Makes Short
People Grow Tall.
Not that the short men are not just as brainy, but
their stature is against them. The great society
leaders are not short women. It seems there is no
longer any excuse for anyone being short, if you will
just follow out Mr. K. Leo Minges’ discovery for
increasing the height. Mr. Minges himself once
knew the disadvantages, the trials and tribulations
that beset the path of the short man. He deter-
mined to grow tall. He tried everything he could
think of, and everything anybody could suggest.
He delved deep into the study of anatomy and
physiology. Finally, when he was almost
ready to give up in despair, he discovered one
of Nature’s marvellous secret laws a law
by which the human body can actually be made
to grow. Mr. Minges tried his new discovery on
himself; it worked like a charm, and to-day Mr.
Minges is 6ft. 2in. tall, and as straight as an arrow.
He next solicited others to try the system, and
behold their height was also increased. He adver-
tised far and wide to get persons extremely short
in stature to try his system and put it to the most
severe tests possible. The results were simply
astounding. The following extract from the New
York Herald shows what can be done: —
“Post Office Inspector Williams says the
Bertillon system of identifying criminals is
a failure; and he offers his own person as
proof of his assertion. Mr. Williams was
measured by the Bertillon system, and went
to work to prove how little use it was in
detecting felons. He was weighed at the time of
his measurement, and tipped the scales close to
one hundred and ninety pounds ; his height was
recorded at five feet two inches. Now his weight
is one hundred and fifty pounds, and he is five feet
four inches in height. The change was brought
about by training he undertook. The ability to
increase his height was the one thing that broke
down the certainty of the Beitillon system. It
was known that a stooping thief might learn to
walk erect; one with cross eyes have his optics
made straight; or a man with birthmarks be freed
from these signs of identity ; but when it was dis-
covered that several inches could be added to the
height of a suspected man, the detectives who
relied upon the Bertillon system were thrown into
confusion.”
Mr. Minges has written a remarkable book,
which fully explains the secrets and the science of
increasing the height. It tells how he himself
grew tall; and how many of his friends have done
so. He sold the copyright of his book to the
Cartilage Company, of Brighton, and this com-
pany proposes to give away 5000 copies simply
for advertising purposes. But there is little
doubt but that the returns which will eventually
come from this substantial kind of advertising
will in the end prove very profitable, as nearly
every short person who sees the book will want
a copy, and there will be a perpetual demand for
it. Until the 5000 copies are exhausted, any
person who wishes to increase his or her height
may get a copy absolutely free, simply by
addressing the Cartilage Company, Dept. 52a,
Brighton, N.Y., U.S.A. Please do not write
through mere idle curiosity, as the book is an
expensive production, and is offered free only to
those who wish to make practical application of
the information contained therein.

Morse’s Indian Root Rills was that of Mr. A. J.

from indigestion and liver complaint, causing his

hands. His letter, telling of the wonderful effects
of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Rills, is as follows :
“ I have just arrived from the above station after
a stay of several years, and during that time I was
always troubled with indigestion and liver com-
plaint ; also, my hands broke out in very bad sores,
and would not heal up. I was advised by one of
the men to try Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills, as

My hands healed up very rapidly, and stopped
itching ; also the indigestion left me entirely, and

fixed me up all round. I strongly recommend Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills to anyone troubled with
the above complaints.” Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills go straight to the liver and kidneys, cleansing
the stomach, and eradicating all kinds of poisonous
humors, making the blood rich and red, and the
skin clear and soft. There is nothing more
humiliating to either men or w 4 jmen than to have

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The “turn-up” for the G.N. Hurdle Race at
Flemington was silently suggested by The Bulletin last
week when it had nothing to say for or against the
chances of the favorites. Twenty-three horses went out
for Saturday’s race, but only two of them, York and
Oblivion, gave their backers a hopeful five minutes. For
a while the nag from Sydney made tlje bolder show. At
the end of two miles the race was beginning to look
a gift for York, and as soon as the leader’s head was
turned for home, the backer of everything else
knew that “nothing else ought to have been backed.”
On his best performance in S. Australia and his recent
win at a Melbourne suburban meeting, York was a rather
promising 33 to 1 chance. A 5-year-okl chestnut gelding
by Tostig-Conclusion, he was one of the best-looking of
the crowd, and about the best-conditioned, and as for
the mild objection that he was not properly “ seasoned ”
—well, he was the same age as Sarasati, also from S. Aus-
tralia, which won the same race last year with 111 b. less to
carry. The time (smin. 50sec.) was only 2sec. above the
record that old Rawdon put up in 1902. Rawdon also was
a lightweight. The big hurdle race, as they run it nowa-
days, on a course which is getting to be more and more
favorable to fast times, looks a bad spec, for the heavy
weights. Fourteen times within 25 years the winner has
carried less than 10 stone. Another winner had 10 stone
exactly. Within the past 10 years only two of them have
carried more than 10 stone, and the top burden was not
more than 11 stone. The scheme of handicap in these
flying hurdle races needs amendment, that the good per-
formers may be given a more reasonable chance of scor-
ing.
*.* * *
The big Steeplechase next Saturday should
attract a field sufficiently numerous to make the book-
makers happy. The most dangerous animal is Accident,
who runs for*the “ books ’’ every year, and may clear the
way for a rank outsider this time. Leaving that veteran
competitor out of the question, the most taking chances
are Beilis, Syringa (a doubtful starter at time of writing),
Airly, The General, Error, Cardinal, Orlando, Forty
Winks, Player, and Blueskin. Airly or Beilis may abso-
lutely win, or come utterly to grief in the- forthcoming
obstacle race. In a field of ’chasers, Beilis, the son of
Lochiel, looks handsome enough to win easily under a
reasonable burden, and 10.12 will not stop him if lie jumps
up to expectations. Airly has already won a long-dis-
tance steeple at Caulfield, and is said to be equal to the
stiffer course at Flemington. He will be all the better for
his meritorious go in the Hurdle Race last Saturday, and,
at any rate, he is bound to be fairly fit to undertake the
long journey, which is more than one can say for the
lately-arrived Maoriland champion, Up-to-Date.
This and last year’s Y.R.O. G.N. winners —
Sarasati and York —started among very outside division
in their races, were bred by same man (Mr. J. V. Smith),
are by the same sire, Tostig, and came from the Wheat
State. Also, each was ridden by jockey Whelan. At
card call previous evening £lBOO to £OO was taken about
York’s chance, and yet he started at 100 to 3. Trainer
C. Quin bought York as a yearling for 50gs., but the nag
did nothing till recently, when he won hurdle races at
Port Adelaide, Morphettville, and Mentone. Second horse
(Old Chappie) is a cast-off of trainer Barney Donovan,
who prepared the favorite, Oblivion. Said favorite ran
seventh.
* * * *
NoN.S.W. horse has ever won V.R.C. G.N.
Hurdles, and only once has one been placed—Beilis
(second) last vear.
* * * *
South Brisbane J.O. races, at Woollongabba,
last week, supplied something that possibly has never
been paralleled the world over. Sowerby, Gamester, and
Diadem finished almost abreast in Club Handicap, but
judge posted Mystery’s number. As Mystery was some-
where at the other end of the field, the crowd ho wled its
wrath, and looked like sweeping the responsible official
out of existence, till one of its number rushed out and
saved slaughter by hoisting Sowerby’s numeral. The
judge accepted the situation calmly, and the bulk of the
punters went round and collected, but several still kicked
hard and insisted Gamester had won.
Maoriland racing authorities are considering the
advisableness of making it compulsory that all owners and
trainers, or whoever is responsible, should, pay a jockey’s
losing fee to a person appointed by the stewards, before
the mannikin weighs out. In case of a win the club is to
deduct the jockey’s fee from the stake, so that he can’t be
“let in” either way. A good idea. It will obviate the
necessity of the unpaid jockey falling back on Ikey Mo.
for the loan of a liver now and again. Once a rider
starts borrowing from Nose, his descent is easy and his
final drop is great.
Next Maoriland racing conference is to be
ijnvited to deal with the mannikin who gets a license to
ride, but doesn’t do so, preferring to punt, or spiel, cr
act as Ikey’s agent in assisting to “ square ” races. Pro-
posed that any jock who hasn’t ridden in 25 events during
the preceding year be refused a license, unless he is
regularly employed by an owner or licensed trainer.
* * * «
There is gloom and disappointment over the
whole continent. The renowned mare Wakeful, which
was recently retired from the turf for domestic purposes,
has declined to dabble in'the birth-rate question. Pro-
bably the wonderful little mare will re-appear on the turf
when it becomes quite certain that she declines family
responsibilities.
“Gun”: Though the horse often badly beats
his owner and backers generally, he doesn’t usually do it
this way : M.L. racing nag Bill Perkins was joyfully sold
for 12gs. two weeks before beginning of recent Dunedin
J.C. meeting, whereat he easily appropriated a race on
the first day, and tote paid £24 14s. Second day the few
who “ chanced it ” because Bill P. was the greatest out-
sider of the field, secured £2O 16s. for every pound ven-
tured ; same afternoon the nag went out again, very
much unfancied, and ran second, beaten in the last few
yards, and paid £3 12s. Doubtful whether any people
immediately concerned in the horse benefited a cent
beyond the prize-money. It was the sort of thing that
only the fatuous punter, always in quest of big dividends,
would be likely to profit by.
* * *
The show at Fitzgeralds’ circus-building last
Saturday night provided an interesting study in human
disappointment. Buttan Singh was to wrestle Clarence
Weber, catch-as-catch-can, for the championship of
Australia, and a large audience including all the
earnest young physical culturists in Melbourne —had
rolled up co witness the humiliation of the colored alien.
The appointed hour of triumph for Australia was
9 o’clock, and the gladiators kept the appointment punc-
tually. A model of big, blonde, manly beauty in the
pride* of youth versus a lithe, copper-colored son of Asia,
aged 41. * Time. The men put out their hands warily for
a minute and 28 seconds. Then came a sudden confusion
of limbs and bodies, and in three seconds the brownie
lay overthrown, cross-buttocked, whilst Weber stood
monarch of the tableau. Amidst the thunders of
joyful comment, the roars of applause, this scribe
could hardly hear the still small voice of conscience
protesting that poor old Buttan, the hero of a
hundred wrestles, deserved some little sympathy under
the circumstances. It seemed hard for the man of 41 to
be chucked about so violently by a lusty Clarence for the
entertainment of an antagonistic multitude. All the
same, Buttan looked none the worse for wear as he
retired to his dressing-room for a “ breather.” In another
10 minutes the joyful crowd was watching for a repetition
of the cross-buttock throw or a “ giant swing,” or any
other striking device whereby Weber would put the
colored person to shame. Alas 1 the second aggressive
movement on Weber’s part was a non-success. He, not
Buttan, fell because he couldn’t help it, and the wily
foreigner, crouching over him as he lay upon the mat,
had choice of moves. The rest of the story is' sad.
Weber undoubtedly is strong enough to resist for
hours the attempts of a smaller man to turn
him over upon his two shoulders, but he had to
attempt to out-general his adversary if he was to
win the match outright. The crowd, now growing eager
for more enthusiasm, exhorted “ Clarry ” to do something,
aiid “ Clarry,” in trying to do it, got done himself. The
fall scored against him was not satisfactory in a pictorial
sense. He seemed to be getting the best of a roll over,
whereas there was a sixteenth portion of a second during
which his shoulders had simultaneously touched the mat.
The conditions of the match were really in the Hindu’s
favor, although they left him at Weber’s mercy as regards
the chuck. Had Weber stipulated for definite “pin”
falls instead of “ flying ” falls his masterful chuck might
not have availed, even though he had supplanted it with
a personal flop upon the body of his foe. But he had
seemingly lost his power of aggression, anyhow. He was
suffering from partial paralysis of confidence when the
third act opened. Pretty soon the men were on the
ground, kneeling, and looking at one another. The
countenance of Weber expressed the subjective character
of the voung; giant. He has no devil, no ginger in his
composition. And on this occasion the thought of two or
three thousand people having assembled to see him assert
his superior strength, and wipe the carpet with the
Pathan, was troubling the young novice, and driving all
other thoughts out of his head. Eventually, the Pathan
won the rubber by a “ fall ” that lasted half as long as a
flash of lightning. Or he bluffed the referee—as some
people said—by leaping high into the air before a decision
was given in his favor. An absurdly brief “ fall” was
awarded to the dancing dervish, and a great silence fell
upon 99 per cent, of the crowd, whilst a brother Hindu
hoisted the frolicsome winner and bore him away.
The Australian was not convincingly beaten, but he
had fallen short of expectation. Incidentally, he had ex-
posed the humbug of all those exhibition wrestling
matches at Rickards’, in which the underneath man used
to suddenly change places with the other fellow. A
match for money means that the man who seeks safety
by lying on his stummick is not at all likely to, recover
from his degradation.
* * * *
The most notable difference between white
wrestler Weber and black Buttan Singh is in their chest
expansions. Weber expands from 45 to 51£in., whereas the
Hindu goes only 39 to 42. Buttan’s forearm compares
favorably with that of the taller and heavier man, whilst
his waist is actually thicker, but his poor, brown .calf goes
4in. less than Weber’s.
A Melb. Argus report of the Weber-Buttan
Singh wrestling match libelled the assembled multitude by
saying “the spectators rushed the ring, hooting.” A
dozen or two.young men may have sauntered towards the
platform, and half-a-dozen of them may have groaned
over the loss of their little wagers, but the “spectators,”
to give the word its full meaning, merely gathered up
their hats and walked silently away, just as they walk
from an Australia v. England cricket match, when the
hoped-for has not happened.
Said that a considerable sum of money, which
changed hands over the Weber-Buttan Singh wrestling
match, went into the pocket of “ Jack” Wren, who runs
a tote shop, by the grace of Melbourne’s police authori-
ties. Wren had been informed that Weber, as the
stronger, but less experienced, man, had made a mistake
in agreeing to wrestle for “ flying ” falls instead of “ pin ”
falls.
* * * •»
Altogether Saturday was N.S.W.’s day out.
At Epping (Syd.) N.S.W. downed Maoriland Soccer team
by two to nil ; and at Brisbane the Cornstalks beat the
Bananalanders very thoroughly by 15 to 3 at the Rugby
game.
The return N.S.W.-Queensland match was won
by aggressive play. The Cornstalks took the offensive
from the first, and the Bananalanders failed to make
counter attacks of any particular brilliancy or sustained
effort, and contented themselves with a stubborn defen-
sive game. So the Cornstalks kept coming again, carrying
the ball down the field at the toe till within striking distance
and then letting the backs loose. Over and over again the
Queenslanders blocked them, but the few occasions when
they didn’t, together with a penalty goal, accounted for
the Cornstalk win.
The N.S.W.-Maoriland “ Soccer ” match was a
great struggle, and one of the fastest games writer ever
saw, in spite of the fact that the ground was so slippery
as to make dribbling difficult and kicking erratic. Both
sides played up well, and the goal-keeping on either side
was like St. Peter keeping the gate of Paradise. The
Cornstalks had slightly better luck, or just a wee bit
better combination, or something, and won by 2 to nil.
Under Australian rules the Y.M.O. A. probably
touched high water line when, on Saturday, it obliterated
West Sydney by 135 to 9 points. The curious thing about
this match was that, just a year ag-o, Y.M.O. A. held the
painful position now occupied by YVest Sydney. As a
match it was a dreary exhibition of one well-organised,
perfectly-disciplined team running over a straggling-
crowd; and there was no joy in it. Newtown’s victory
over Paddington was also a one-sided business, but
Paddington did manage to keep its tail slightly towards
the horizontal, and snatched 23 points while the vic-
torious Newtown accumulated 75. At Rushcutters Bay
Sydney could only get IS points ahead of North Sydney,
the fig-ures being 53 and 40. The real struggle of the
day was between" East Sydney and Balmain, George Reid’s
electorate winning by one point—26 to 25. There is some
sort of weird connection between this event and the
majority that George lost the run of last week, 'out it s
too hard to follow.
* f- ir *
Anything may happen at football, and even at
horse-racing the beast that carries the money wins some-
times. Saturday week South Sydney fell upon Glebe
and rolled it in the dirt. Last Saturday South Sydney
itself went down before the University, which Glebe had
previously defeated in a wholesale fashion. It was not a
crushing defeat, but 8 to 3 was decisive enough to prove
that the victory was more than an accident. The defeat
of North Sydney by Western Suburbs (eight points to nil)
was very startling. Sydney downed Balmain by 5 to nil,
and Glebe had the bad luck to only effect a draw with
Newtown. Had Glebe had its usual luck and beaten
Newtown it would have regained its position at the head
of the competition list. It now ranks with the beaten
South Sydney (14 points each). Western Suburbs is just
behind with 13 ; Newtown follows with 12 ; and University
and Sydney are far down with 11.
The wiser of Melbourne’s amusement seekers—
including Waxcellency Madden, gaud Mayor Plesaunce
(new spelling), and little Sammy Gillotte (ditto) —shied
off the follies of Flemington on Saturday, and toddled
down to Oollingwood football ground, where the Magpies
were expected to put through the hitherto undefeated
Fitzroy in style. A close-packed 20,000 heaved and yelled
while the expected transpired, but a strong wind spoiled
all chance of good play. Both teams seemed to be afraid
of giving the other a chance to get its system
into work, and crowded on the ball, and mulled
much, spoiling the game as a spectacle. ’Wood started
early and steadily forged ahead. The finish saw ’Wood
5 goals 11 behinds to ’Roy’s 3 goals 1 behind. These two
clubs are now level with one defeat each, Carlton being
third with three defeats, the rest nowheie. In the
Association the hitherto undefeated West Melbourne —
the babies of the Association—went down to their old
Mentors, North Melbourne, by 22 points to 26, but they
are still a game ahead of North, Ports and Williamstown,
so they feel fairly comfortable. No umpires have been
assaulted of late, nor has any Ikey been alleged to have
half the players in his bag.
* » * »
Auckland and Hawkes Bay Rugby Unions are
going to put up a stern fight against the Maoriland
Union action in blindly following the English ruling that
an injured player cannot be replaced. And quite right
too. To refuse to allow replacements is a direct bribe to
the team that is losing to lay the other fellows right out,
and by breaking one or two of the other side’s be- t men
to equalise matters.
* * * *
The fact that the Maoriland Rugby footballers
will not arrive till Friday, or perhaps not till early on the
Saturday on which they have to don the guernsey against
N.S.W., shows shockingly bad management. The Aus-
tralian tour seems to be only a grasp for boodle, irrespec-
tive of players or public. Under the conditions, the
’bailers can hardly be expected to show respectable form,
and apparently the people who sent them don’t care
whether they do or not.
* * * «
Possible that the injury received by N.S.W.
Rugby captain Wickham, in the inter-State match at
Brisbane (Q.), last week, will cause his definite retirement
from football. Prior to getting maimed, Wickham had
captained the N.S.W. team at Brisbane in eight successive
matches, and, by kicking alone, had piled up heaps of
points. His compulsory retirement will be a great loss.
The Australian team’s score of 527 against
Gloucestershire, declared at nine wickets, fell short
of the performance of the ’O2 team against VV. G. Grace’s
men, when the Australians heaped up 545 for five wickets.
A coincidence was that Trumper and Duff, who put up 134
for the first wicket three years ago, nearly repeated the
feat this time, the score being 132 when Duff was caught
at the wicket. Trumper’s first hundred of the tour was
made this match.
* * * »
Noble reached his 1000 runs in the Warwick-
shire match, easily beating Armstrong, who had started
off at a great rate in the first half-dozen matches of the
tour. Still, Armstrong, with four innings less and aided
by a couple of not-outs, is 10 runs ahead in averages. The
batting figures of Gregory, Gehrs, M’Leod and a couple of
others are more despairful than ever.
* * * *
Frank Iredale throws some light on the disabili-
ties an Australian XT. 1 ibors under. On many days it
was almost impossible to see in the streets up to 11 in the
morning ; factories and shops were illuminated by gas and
electricity all day, and the air was filled with smoke and
dust and soot. The match against Yorkshire commenced
ip cold, gloomy weather, with the haze so thick that the
stumps were only occasionally visible from the press-box,
and during the frequent adjournments the Australians
huddled and crouched over the fire in the dressing-room.
And they call it sport!
A *
The approach of midsummer in England may
bring about an improvement in the form of the Australian
cricketers by giving them a slight opportunity to play
under conditions more akin to what they have been
accustomed to. The totals of 380 against Warwickshire
and 527 against Gloucestershire in one week look as
though the kangaroo’s tail was beginning to point again
towards the Pole Star. The score against Gloucester was
very evenly distributed.
Swimmer Kieran’s defeat by Billington, in
English mile supremacy, was nob surprising. With only
10 days to prepare for so stiff a struggle, under adverse
climatic conditions, and in fresh instead of the salt water
he was accustomed to, he had little hope. ■ That Ivieran
lacked thorough training' is evidenced by his leading ov r
half the distance. Dick Cavill had an almost similar ex-
perience, but later on made amends by doing remarkable
things. Billingt in’s time, 24min. 42see., is the best ever
done in England, but it is lmin. 25 l-ssee. slower than
Kieran’s world’s record.
* * * *
London Sportsman states that Towns and Stan-
bury have issued a challenge to all comers for the Double-
Sculling championships of the world; and from another
source it is alleged the Barry Brothers, of England, are
ready to take up the challenge. If terms are signed, tin
event will be rowed on the Frazer River, Canada, when
also a race between Towns or Stanbury and Canadian
Durnan is possible.
* * * *
After all, the Towns-Stanbury sculling cham-
pionship will be rowed over the old course —Rvde Bridge
to Searle’s monument. In the original conditions, the
course was altered—the race to finish at Gladesville ; but
the owners of water frontages near Searle’s monument
have backed down and decided to hand over a share of
the receipts, to the scullers. Both scullers are giving
their supporters much satisfaction in their training opera-
tions ; but the Towns supporters are the more sanguine,
believing that the champion will breakup Stanbury in
the first mile, just as he did Tressider. But Stanbury is
different from Tressider.
* « * *
Sydney City Bowling Club has declared that it
will.not pay the £lO4 per annum demanded by the City
Council as rent for the green in Cook Park. It has also
decided that it will not be ejected by the council, and the
council is making fearsome threats. It is a difficult ques-
tion to decide which view to take in this matter. Cook
Park is a dreadful, ill-lit place, and the grass has been
smothered for years by a dreary waste of impenetrable
clay. By day it is unpleasant; by night it is a horror ; the
sandbag-ger and the plain, ordinary thief who cleans out
the drunk’s pockets have it for their own, and ghastly
caricatures of femininity frowse under the trees. Abso-
lutely the only bright spot in the “park” is the
one that the Bowling Club has spent its money in
beautifying. The grounds are open to the publ.c. Seats
are provided, and all that the club asks (and insists on) is
that the Woolloomooloo pet shall not play up on the green.
The Bulletin doesn’t like the grabbing of the parks ; but
Cook Park, where it is not grabbed, is an unlovely spot,
and if the Council hunts the club out, it certainly ought
to take steps to make the alleged city lung a place where
people can-go in the evenings and breathe twice before
they are murdered.
* * * *
Tempe-Leiehhardt hurling club, which mopped
the floor with Redfern a week ago, was apparently suffer-
ing from a slight attack of swelled head on Saturday, and
held the (pity (Sydney) crowd rather cheaply. Result
was defeat by 7to 5. Both teams played a good defensive
game, and the Tempe-Leichhardt men, when on the
offensive, ; rushed thing's very vigorously. Had they
played with the same caution as they did when they
downed Redfern, they would not have been beaten by
City, which Redfern had previously thwacked quite
soundly.
* * * *
Fact that that arrant deceiver, T.M.S., won
Farewell Handicap at Boulder (W.A.) meeting, Saturday
week, after repeated failures in the same section, was
taken by many betting men as the “ office” that T.M.S.’s
previous owner, “fiduciary agent” Slattery, would
triumph also, and they wagered accordingly, laying 2 to
1 “ on ” in several instances.
* * * *
“ 11. G.”: The most noticeable fact about the
Vic. amateur boxing championship competition, opened
on Saturday evening- in Melbourne, is the lack of con-
dition on the part of the amateurs as compared with the
pros. While even the tenth-raters who fight prelimin-
aries at the halls show no distress, apart from that
caused by punishment, over six or eight rounds, the
average amateur is dead to the world at the end of the
first three minutes. And the most surprising part of it is
that the amateurs are well-fed, well-clothed, decent-
living youngsters fresh from school, and followers of
athletics all the year round, while the member of the
Rats Push who gets into a ring for the prospect of a
“ shower ” generally does no work, lives on a diet of beer
and cigarettes, and doesn’t even take as much exercise in
boxing itself as the amateur. Perhaps the explanation is
that one man is really exerting himself in the ring and the
other only looks as if he were.
« * * *
Frost’s defeat of “Spud” Green at Sydney
Gaiety Athletic Hall on Saturday last was a triumph of
condition. Green threw out some ugly insinuations with
his fist during the first few rounds, and Frost looked
several times as though he was likely to go the way of all
meat. But apparently someone knew something, and the
betting was 2tol in his favor. Then “ Spud ” seemed to
evaporate. He lost his sting, and the devil departed o t
of him, and in the ninth round he waved the pale, anxioi s
towel of surrender.
* * * *
Fennessy, who put up such a whirling, meteoric
scrap with Farley in Melbourne recently, has “ taken on ”
Mr. William Squires, the steam-hammer from Newcastle
way. The upheaval will take place at Melbourne on the
njght of July 10th.
Now that they have been working under
minimum wage legislation for a few years, the
Vic. sweaters, who once wept so loudly about
“restrictive legislation” and the “ hampering of
private enterprise,” are very quiet and appar-
ently quite content. Vie. Factories Inspector
Ord reports that in ’O4 there were 443(5 registered
factories in the State, employing 61,977 persons.
Of these 38,000 were working under Wages Board
rates and regulations, and “there is little trouble
in enforcing the rates fixed.” Only the Mongol
in the laundry and furniture trades defies the law,
and even he would have been brought to heel
by this time but for the House of Landlords,
which so loves the alien that it is willing to
handicap its own fellow white Australians in hi-;
favor.
THE CRICKET CRAZE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(The portion of the daily papers recently devoted to the
prowess of the Monkey is now occupied with accounts of
the score in Hingland.)
There’s a slump in Monkey stocks now, for the cricket
craze is raging—
Aye, the bank-clerk howls in frenzy as he scans the won-
drous score ;
And the barber’s understudy doth descant in terms
engaging—
Aye, he rubs the news and lather in at every suffering
pore !
And the man behind the counter simply reeks with tales
of cricket—
He discourses on Maclaren as he serves the shirts and
socks;
The head boarder at my hash-house moans about the
moistened wicket
Till I wish some fiend would bash him with ten thousand
heavy rocks. •
One gets cricket on the tramcar, in the train, and at the
table—
In the bar and on the boulevard, and across the billiard-
baize ;
Yea, Australia’s been converted to a flannelled-foolish
Babel,
And I’m sick and tired and weary of this stupid cricket
craze !
Was the telegraph invented to relate flow one “ Bo-
sankey ”
Biffed a harmless sphere of compo. to the boundary for
four ?
The inventor’s ghost informs me, with aloud, disgraceful
blanky,
That his wires are desecrated by the silly Hinglish score !
Bne the cables keep on jerking, and they prate of Jones
and Lilley—
They descant upon the prowess of de Trafford and of Fry
Yea, they speak of Gehrs and Trumper till the clamor
drives one silly,
And I seem to see “ SYD. GREGORY ” in placards on the
sky !
A disturbance known as Tyldesley looms dark on our
horizon,
So the daily paper tells me as upon my chop I graze ;
But Ido not care a match-head if he be a bear or bison -
For I’m not infatuated with this precious cricket craze !
I have talked with men of cricket, and they didn’t much
impress me ;
They knew nothing of de Balzac, but were connoisseurs
or beer —
They could tell each brand of whisky by its smell, and
heaven bless me !
They had mouths like huge portmanteaux, stretching
round from ear to ear !
I have always hated people who wear mouths like railway
tunnels —
I believe a three-inch orifice sufficed John Stuart Mill,
But the faces of some cricket fiends look like enormous
funnels.
So I do not worship Noble, and I don’t adore Clem Hill.
One can always gauge a pastime by the folk who
barrack for it,
And upon the cricket hoodlum may no man with plea-
sure gaze,
With his cigarette-stained fingers, and his hat as seer
ne’er wore it—
He’s a splendid sign and symbol of the stupid cricket
craze.
But the Monkey’s stocks are slumping, and the cricket
plague is raging—
Aye, the bank clerk howls in frenzy as he scans the
dreary score,
And the barber’s understudy doth relate in terms
engaging
How the blessed cricket ashes all depend on one match
more; ■ - 1
And the dude behind the counter simply reeks with yarns
of cricket;
He discourses on Bilowell as he serves the shirts and
socks.
The chief donkey at my hash-house brays about the damp
old wicket
Till I wish some fiend would bash him with ten million
heavy rocks!
One gets cricket on the tram-car, in the train, and at the
table —
In the bar, and on the boulevard, and across the billiard-
baize.
Yea, Australia’s been converted to a flannelled-foolish
Babel—
Bah ! I’m sick and tired and weary of this useless cricket
craze 1
Vio.

PURE, WHOLESOME and INVIGORATING. There is Nothing Better.
“ This stupid Arbitration business ” being
temporarily “off,” N.S. Wales has the blessings
of the strike again. And instead of being glad,
the Sydney papers mention quite severely that
they are using revolvers and stones up at the
Pelaw Main colliery, and that one of the people
who was helping to settle a dispute in the proper
way, and without any beastly interference from
the Arbitration Court, has a fractured skull, and
that trains are running under police escort.
A scrap from the censure debate in the
Federal Parliament: —
Mr. Reid : He, as head of the Government, received from
the Chief Justice a peremptory telegram, demanding three
thinsrs, and those three things had since been done, but
he strongly took the view that under no circumstances
should anyone, not even judges, send peremptory
telegrams to the head of the Government.
Mr. Deakin : Does not that depend on what preceded
the peremptory telegram ?
Mr. Reid: A tele ram in the nature of a demand on the
head of the Government was utterly unwarrantable.
Mr. Deakin: Unless something absolutely unprecedented
preceded.
Mr. Reid did not want to go into details.
No, certainly, Mr. Reid did not want to go into
details for, if what is whispered about is true,
there is enough in those details to give a fresh
blot to the political character of even Gr. H. Reid.
Owing to his ‘ ‘ name as well as his fortune
getting involved in matters over which he had no
control... owing to giving his time to public
business from early morning till past midnight,”
Premier Carrutliers sobbed to the Lands Commis-
sion. “lied retire from active private business
for two years, or longer if he had the misfortune
to remain in office.” Now, everyone knows that
Carrutliers rushed “the misfortune of office” with
full steam on, that he hugs the “misfortune”
with both hands and what legs he can spare for
the purpose, and that he can drop the “misfor-
tune” any moment he wants to. If he elects to
continue hugging the “misfortune” till somebody
stronger drags it from him, that’s his business,
but to bug the “misfortune” and make himself
out a public martyr over a personal advantage he
can forego at any time is snuffle.

Mr. W. H. Wood has been elected Chair-
man of Committees in the N.S.W. Assembly--
another illustration of the value in politics of
making yourself objectionable. Wood, a smart
political youngster, was raised to Cabinet rank
most unexpectedly by Bill Lyne. When the
Lyne Cabinet was reconstructed under John See,
Wood was dropped out. Thereupon Wood
dripped boiling oil on his enemies. When he rose
to speak little blue flames darted about the
Chamber, and the sergeant-at-arms stood by
with a bucket of water in case the old building
should combust. Now they have put Wood where
he will have to keep quiet.
Among those who whoop loudest in denun-
ciation of State Socialism at present are many
whose sole cure for land-agency ills is the bringing
of land-agents under State control. Make land-
agents register themselves, and fix their fees, and
hold them tight by State machinery, cry these
enemies of State interference with private enter-
prise. The N.S.W. land-agency system has been
private enterprise pure and simple, and it reached
its present gigantic evil dimensions because the
State did not interfere with it.

also a landscape gardener and painter. I can do
tailoring, and am. a good hand at darning socks. ’

A very important hygienic, event was the dis-
covery of a method of converting the essential principle
of milk into a substance which will not ferment or turn
sour. The preparation, under the name of Plasmon,
consists of the milk-a-lbumen, separted from the
fat and the sugar, but retaining the organic
salts, and dried in carbonic acid gas; thus be-
coming capable of practically everlasting preservation.
Plasmon—lor wnich it is claimed that loz. contains as
jpuch nutriment as* lib. of best beef—is sold as a fine,
white, tasteless powder, which may be added with advan-
tage to almost any article of diet.*

Legal Notice.
PURSUANT to an Order of the High Court of Justice
* Chancery Division dated (he 7th day of November IUO4
made in an Action in the matter of the Estate of Hans
Campbell late of Legananny in the District of Loughbrick-
land in the County of Down in Ireland Farmer deceased
CAMPBELL v. ROSCO 1904 C. 2033 Whereby the follow-
ing inquiry was directed An inquiry who were the per-
sons entitled by virtue of or according to the Statue of
Distribution or otherwise to the personal Estate of the
said Intestate Hans Campbell living at the time of his death
and whether any of them are since dead and if so who are
their respective legal personal representatives JOHN
CAMPBELL the brother of the Intestate the said Hans
Campbell if living or if the said John Campbell survived
the said Intestate Hans Campbell (who died on the 17th
day of February 1903) and has since died the legal personal
representatives of the said John Campbell or if the said
John Campbell pre-deceased the said Intestate Hans
Campbell the Children of the said John Campbell
if living or the legal personal representatives of
such of them as survived the said Intestate Hans
Campbell and have since died are on or before the 25th
day of October 1905 to come in and prove their respective
claims in the Chambers of Mr. Justice Swinfen-Eady and
Mr. Justice Farwell Room 710 at the Royal Courts of
Justice Strand London England or in default thereof they
will be peremptorily excluded from the benefit of the
said Order. Wednesday the Bth day of November 1905 at
12 o’clock at noon at the said Chambers is appointed
for hearing and adjudicating upon the claims.
Dated this 25th dav of May 1905.
SPENCER WHITEHEAD,
Master.
NOTE.—The said John Campbell left Ireland and went
to Auckland, in New Zealand, in 1805. In 1870 he was at
132 Queen-street, Melbourne, in Australia, having pre-
viously spent six months in Sydney, in Australia. He
subsequently returned to Sydney, where he was last heard
of in 1877.
SHAEN ROSCOE MASSEY & CO.,
8 Bedford Row, London, England,
Solicitors for the Plaintiff.

Last half-year of Ruby P.C., Charters Towers,
compares poorly with the half that went before ; 623 tons
gave 9030 z., an average of loz. 9dwt. Residues brought
the value to £6 19s. a ton. Previous term 1897 tons
averaged loz. 14dwt, worth £7 15s. sd. Only one div. of
3d. (41250) was paid, against £6250 for preceding term.
Co. commenced to sink a new shaft with £3OOO in hand,
and winds up with £2083. Considering £5OOO less in
divs. has been paid, position is not strong. When the
new straight was started, 190 ft. was the estimated
depth to the reef; but 240 ft. has not disclosed anything
payable, so an underlie has been started at the' 100 ft.
formation on a few inches of stone. No. 1 shaft has been
sunk 395 ft. and bottomed on formation. The report
mentions in various places, “The stopes are now ex-
hausted.” You can’t say worse of a stope than that.
* * * *
The Commonwealth, Charters Towers, one of
the “ pups ” that were sooled on to Brisbane, exhausted
its capital in sinking a hole 30ft. ; but it has been re-
floated. Prospectus calls it the Mystery; 100,000 shares,
, all contributing, of ss. ; situated oh the famous Mystery
Flat, adjoining the Columbia and the Extended, on the
sensational Brilliant line of reef, &e. Co. has 45 acres,
and proposes to sink 250 ft. for a start. The flat is a net-
work of pig-rootings, abandoned for years on account of
heavy water. Wardens’ returns (10,496 tons for 10,5150 z.)
do not shine, when one remembers that some crushings
went 4oz. a ton. The Columbia (shut down), formerly
the Queen Block Extended, has spent £BB,OOO and got
nothing. Brilliant Extended is working at a depth of
2366 ft., and the Mystery is 1J miles further out. What
show have they of getting the Brilliant reef with a capital
of £25,000? In order to rush things along, the office has
reserved 30,000 shares.
* » * *
An interesting show is the Queen Deep, Charters
Towers, better known as the Union Jack, the Sunburst,
the Queen Consols, and by sundry other names. Present
co. is in 200,000 shares, of ss. ; 100,000 paid-ups went to
Bull, mostly for his heap of mullock. Contribs. are held
by a select few ; calls are light (id. per month). Mine is
situated in the northern part of the field, in diorite coun-
try, remarkable for the number of cross-courses it con-
tains. Previous co.’s in that locality made ado of things
for a while, but failed hopelessly when deep ground was
reached. Queen Deep will possibly be a 14dwt. show,
and should be on stone this month. With careful man-
agement, there should be something in this. Recent
operations in the Union Jack (or whatever its name was)
missed, it is said, 4ft. of crushing stuff in some old work-
ings that other co.’s had left as unpayable, with the
crushing appliances in use some years ago. A shrewd
crowd is running the business.
* * * *
Mills’ United, Charters Towers, for a fortnight,
505 tons for 281 oz. ; concentrates and residues bring total
to £2332. This leads some squibs on ; even the School
Reserve, after about 11 years’ exemption, is on the move.
Why not let dead cats lie ? Freeholds have unwatered
their mine, and a start to drive their bottom level west,
towards Mills’ (under three-quarters of a mile), may be
made any day. Ground, so far, has a nasty habit of turn-
ing over just when a bit of stone comes in. As Mills’
United and the Freeholds are managed by the same firm,
why not continue one of the former’s drives, at the
latter’s expense, and prove beyond a doubt that payable
ore lives in the Freeholds’ ground ? It seems ridiculous
to ask a co. to spend thousands of pounds in learning if
the reef lives, when the same information could be
obtained with the expenditure of as few hundreds. About
here is where the gamble in Towers mines comes in.
Mexican is after the same make, and should it locate it,
the field will have to be shifted round a bit to fall in
line. Freehold Co. is all contributing.
* * * *
Expert Rolker’s report on Great Fingall (W.A.)
has been made ; he thinks B. M. & Co. over-estimated ore
reserves 7 per cent., which, in the circumstances, isn’t
much, and apparently reduces their value from £2,500,000
to £2,325,000. Estimates here must be largely guesswork,
anyway. The levels are vertically 200 ft. apart, and on
the underlay fully 250 ft., so data for gauging either
quantity or quality are meagre. Mine continues to give
poor results at two lowest levels ; and that’s hard fact and
not guesswork, though it hasn’t much bearing on the
question of actual reserves.

* * * *
Rumors are afloat that Associated (W. A.) is
running short of ore, and will have to close down treat-
ment plant. This is nonsense. Since G. M. Roberts took
control three months ago reserves have gone up by leaps.
At 1400 ft., Australia main lode is 17ft. wide, and all ore
cpming from it is going to the mill. Workings on this
lode are connected with Oroya’s south block at 200, 300
and 500 ft. ; but, for most part, lode is quite unexplored.
There is nearly 1000 ft. of virgin ground south from
Oroya’s boundary. In addition to Tetley’s lode and the
cross lode, Australia east lode is providing ore for mill.
Latter lode is entirely misnamed, as, at 900 ft., it is 150 ft.
;west of shaft, and, at 1200 ft., about 200 ft. west. Above
900 ft. thisslode has not been tapped at all, and west side
of shaft is unexplored in upper levels. Management of
this mine has in the past been very faulty at times.
Treatment plant is being remodelled, and nearly half of
it thrown out. When overhauled it is expected to treat
10,000 tons, instead of 8000 monthly as hitherto. Plant
will be shut down for a few odd shifts to make some
alterations, but not because of any shortage of ore. It
looks as if Associated is to be one of the field's best mines.
Oroya shoot is still proving a will o’ the wisp, but if it is
found to continue and maintain its richness, Associated
will certainly be in the top flight.
* * * *
Cumberland, Norseman (W.A.), is being boomed,
but booming is wholly unjustified. Reef is a mere leader,
and, according to official reports, only worth 7 to 8 dwt.,
and a lot of sorting must be done tq maintain output.
Battery is an out-of-date light plant.' Costs are high,
though last month they were only 553;,'. as against 665. for
April, and 83s. for March. Profit for last three months
has been £6OO, £9OQ, and £750, and value of ore 100 s., 965.
2d., and 80s. Bd. per ton. Grade of ore. has declined very
seriously during past three month's, and last month
would have shown a loss at the March costs rate. Total
working exes, and upkeep tot up to 325. 4d., while
development costs 225. Bd., or nearly £7OO monthly.
This is more than double the amount Golden Pole spends
on development, although treating nearly four times as
much ore. Co. may continue paying divs. a little longer,
but position, as set out above, can’t be called promising.

Burbank's Birthday Gift (W.A.), which has
been under a cloud for some time, has rejoined producers,
after a long spell. At 800 ft. a shoot of ore 200 ft. long by
12ft. wide, worth 12dwt., has been opened up, and shaft is
down to 900 ft., w’here a fresh level will be started shortly.
Half the mill is now crushing, and possibly whole 30 head
will be going before long. Should No. 9 level equal No. 8,
prospects will be greatly improved, and co. may again
pay divs.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Billy Barlow (the “Blue-tailed Ply") had a birthday
up Gympie way t’other week. He is now on the shady
side of 85, and has had his golden wedding.
“A. 8.”: Whilst passing through Colombo last year, 1
visited a native theatre and saw a dusky George Lauri,
with a chalked face, create shrieks of mirth by his
impersonation of a white man. Here is a tip for some of
our colored comedians. Pope as a black man disguised
as a white man should be a matter of interest. I have
wondered at times why one of the ebony tribe did not
study tile aboriginal character a little, and give a sketch
of King Billy in his ’possum rug and copper plat..
’All Caine has not lost the knack of booming his pro-
ductions. Somehow it has got into the papers that
George Alexander receives £250 per week as lea ling man
in “ The Prodigal Son ”at Drury Lane. Probably, also,
the supers engaged, lying flat, would extend from Loudon
to Port Darwin ; placed perpendicularly they would reach
the planet Mars ; and the paint used for the scenery
was as many gallons as a year’s beer supply for tl e
Britisli Empire.
“ Tet. P.”: S.M, Herald, an organ that abhors the alleged
tendency of a few Australians to pronounce “ a ” as “ i,”
instead of adapting the “ naice ” English practice of
reversing the blunder, dogmatised thus (26/6/’O5 ) on the
recent production of “ The Yeoman of the Guard”: “In
the ‘Spinning Song’ Miss Aggie Thorne’s ‘Heigh-ho’
was repeatedly mispronounced ‘ Hy-ho,’ an individual
peculiarity, which had apparently been overlooked at
rehearsal.” All the same, Miss Aggie Thorne had Web-
ster on her side. He says the pronunciation is Hi-ho !
And Gilbert himself at its dainty best in “ Princess Ida,"
has this idea of the word —
Oh dainty triolet!
Oh fragrant violet !
Oh gentle heigh-ho-let!
(Or little sigh).
The Herald’s craving’ to have the word called “ hay-ho ”
or “ hee-haw ” should be discouraged.
“S.S.”: Why was not Premier Bent moved to wrath
and autocratic action by M'Kee Rankin’s irreverent cari-
cature of George Reid in “ Magda ” ? Rankin, as the mili-
tant pa, is an exact replica of Reid, excepting that
the moustache is grey instead of sandy, and the monocle
is missing. The fall of the corporation and the warp in
the legs display an astonishing fidelity. The infuriated fat
father dashes on a small belltopper in one excited moment,
and the laug’h that results springs from the people’s recog-
nition of the fact that the caricature is now complete.
So general is the appreciation of the resemblance that
George is asked at his meetings : “ Have you brought
Magda with you, old man?”
“M.A.G.”: Had the idea that Australians who have
raved over every action of Togo’s did so on account of
John Bull’s repulsive alliance with Japan ; but a visit to a
certain show lately went a good way towards demolishing
that idea. Part of the entertainment consisted of a series
of pictures depicting the alleged treachery and cruelty of
Russian soldiers to wounded Japanese. Each picture
was announced by the manager in this style : “ Here we
have the dear little brave Jap,” &c., or “ Here we see our
dear little brown brother in the east ” (seems to me the
bump of locality needs developing here), “ who is a pattern
to the whole world,” &c. The hall was crowded with a
Japanalian audience, who clapped and hoorayed at the
limelight reflection of the Monkey’s flag. They evidently
thought the pictures were real—in fact, one man nearly
bit me when I called them a “ fake.” I asked him if he
thought those brutal Russians allowed a strange photo-
grapher to stand beside them with impunity and take
views of their crimes, and he told me they had to do so
because the photographer was protected by the British
flag.
When the Modern Milo, whose bounteous form and
economical costumes are booming Rickards’ Melbourne
show, was a noticeable member of. a comic opera chorus j
she was the only girl on the stage who dispensed abso-
lutely with What the late Henry' Parkes used to :call
“ hadventitious haids.” Not that all are padded, but
stage nymphs often wear—as their admirers do not alwavs I
know—a pair of thick, woollen tights under the silken
hose which the audience breathes hard over. “Milo’s”
measurements were so well-proportioned that the woollens
were regarded as unnecessary, is a fine girl, and her
unadulterated figure is a standing reproach to those who
assert that woman has deteriorated since the days of
Phidias and other persons whose descendants sell oysters.

“ Gr.H.” complains :
Sydney Worker of June 24 contained a cartoon depict-
ing Australia’s political alternatives. Reidism was shown
on one side, with the furious walrus of Monopoly howling
for its prey behind the electoral door. Behind the other
door sat the feminine embodiment of Australian progress,
with a large cog-wheel gracefully depending from her left
hand, and with 68 foundries in full blast on the right.
And the leading article on the opposite page held Chris.
Watson up in silhouette against the bellowing dawn of
destiny, with a golden pavement of noble conceptions be-
neath his feet, five glorious ideals in his right hand, four
slightly-frayed ditto in his left, but nary a sign of a sen-
sible, foundry-maintaining, Protective fiscal policy about
him.

Some N.S.W. wayback people who want a State
railway foreninst their door offer to give a pledge
to crop so many acres within three years ; and the
Sydney dailies say that it is a system which ought
to be encouraged, and that these people ought to
have a preference over the people who also want
railway lines but don’t offer any pledge. Yet,
when certain workmen give a pledge, backed up
by actual money, that they will keep steadily at
work, and won’t strike and disturb the business
of the country, the same papers say it is monstrous
to give them any preference over other workmen
who won’t offer any pledge. Also, they ask con-
temptuously how you can compel men to work if
they won’t; but they don’t ask how you can com-
pel men to put in crops if they won’t.
Writes Beaumont Smith :
I am sorry to see it, but at present it can’t be averted.
I refer to the jealousy between our artists, or many of
them, in Australia. There is bitter jealousy, and the
cause is the very limited market for pictures here. It is
a fight against each other, and friendship cannot be
maintained when men are starving, and there is a crust
on the horizon. I was talking to a rather well-known
artist the other day, just after he had come out of a big
exhibition then being held in Adelaide. I asked his
opinion of it. He puckered up his brow. “ Tripe ! awful
stuff!” Attended the exhibition, and talked with the
promoter ; mentioned this artist’s work. He turned up his
nose, “ Didn’t like it! Tripe ! Awful stuff 1.” It seems
the same everywhere in Australia. In nine cases out of
ten an artist has got little to say in a favorable strain
about his brother’s work, if their markets are the same.
If I went by Australian artists’ opinions of Australian
art, I’d begin to think everything was “ tripe.” Every
artist agrees that Longstaff, and one or two more men in
England, are real painters; but then these men don’t
affect their market.
The notion that Germany spends its nights
pouring beer down its throat doesn’t pan out too
well when you come down to figures. Certainly,
Germany gets rid of almost exactly half-a-gallon
per throat per week; but Westralia is nearly as
thirsty—its tally is only a spoonful or two short
of the half-gallon, and Britain beats both of them
hollow. S. Australia is the worst customer of the
brewer in these parts; it doesn’t even get rid of
so much as Maoriland, in spite of the latter State’s
big Prohibition patches. ButS.A. partly makes
up for its beer-famine by taking in just three
times more wine than the Australian average; and
even then it only drinks an annual gallons per
throat, while France keeps itself merry on 30.
Belgium is the real beer-drinker among the
nations; its score is 47| gallons a year, with
Britain second at 31, and Germany a bad third
with 26|.

THE FOOD PASSAGES
and ORGANS.
The body is a working machine, and like all other
machines wears away in use. Our bones and muscles, so firm
and strong, need constant repair to keep them so. For this
purpose food is necessary, and a series of instruments or
organs by which it may be changed into flesh and bone. We
are, therefore, furnished with teeth to chew, palates to taste,
stomachs to digest, livers to aid digestion, and bowels to carry
off the refuse matter. The Liver is the very gateway of
health, the sewage farm, where the poisons are neutralised by
the action of the bile ; it exerts an all-powerful influence on
the mind, temperament, and disposition. If your Liver is
active you are happy and light-hearted, if sluggish you are
crabby and miserable. Kutnow’s Powder will keep the
liver in perfect order.

Uric Acid.—The presence of this acid in the blood is the cause of people suffering from Rheu-
matism, Gout, Neuralgia, Bachaehe, Lumbago, Sciatica, Gravel, and Bladder Troubles. The accumu-
lation of uric acid in the blood is a certain indication that the kidneys are acting inefficiently.
Warner’s Safe Cure acts specifically upon the kidneys, and invariably restores those vital organs to
health and activity, when all suffering due to uric poisoning ceases. So potent is Warner’s Safe Cure
that it will even cure Bright's Disease of the kidneys.
A simple test to make as to the condition of the kidneys is to put some of the urine, passed the
first thing in the morning, into a bottle or covered glass, and let it stand until next morning. If it is
then cloudy, or if it contains a sediment like brickdust, or if particles float about in it, or it is of an
unnatural color, the kidneys are not healthy, and no time should be lost in commencing to take
Warner’s Safe Cure.

Suburbs, and Ballarat.
At the recent Sydney Licensing Court
three renewals of publicans’ licenses on the Rocks
resumed areas were refused. One of these houses
had a venerable piece of timber over the door.
John Rochester, a North Shields man and mate of
a whaling ship, and Eleanor, his wife, a Meath
woman, established, prior to the year 1830, a
public-house at the corner of Harrington and
Gloucester streets —at least, so Bays an advertise-
ment of the time, though the searcher to-day will
have some difficulty in finding the junction of the
streets named. In the late 30’s the Rochesters
removed to a new house in Cambridge-street, and
carried the sign there. Later the faded intima-
tion about demented and furious liquors
was shifted along with the license—a license
was then a very portable thing—to a house
in Cumberland-street, which was named “ The
Erin-Go-Bragh.” This is the place that has just
lost its license through old age, and a relic that
has announced that beer was available almost
since the beginnings of Sydney will now presum-
ably serve as firewood.
About Mummers.
“ O’Fipp ”: Notice that a postcard-pictured foreign
actress rejoices in the name of “ Meaty.” Probably it is
not pronounced in the English way, but it ought to be.
There is much meat on the lady.
Nance O’Neil, when she returns to America, intends
going in for stock raising on her 200-acre farm, which she
has named O’Neil Manor. Nance spent her girlhood in
the Californian valleys, and is said to know a cow from a
horse.
Barney Bernard, who played some German Jew parts
here with Kolb and Dill, but has now quitted burlesque to
devote himself to the serious Jew, tells a ’Frisco
Examiner interviewer that when he opened at Sydney,
four liabbis attended “in full rig-—hats on and ail that
kind of thing.” Bernard evidently means “ rabbies ”
wild rabbies. And in addition to keeping their hats on
they most likely took their coats off. Also they had a
donah whose name was Sal.
Vesta Tilley, of the London music-halls, goes to the
United States on a six weeks engagement at £2 a minute.
The contract was probably made in that precise form for
advertising purposes, but the lady assured a London
Express interviewer that it is quite genuine. From the
moment of her going on, three stop-watches (including
her husband’s) will be on her. “ Any time spent in bow-
ing after the last song not to be allowed.” Beckoning on
a 20-minutes’ turn twice a day, the pay works out at
about £5OO a week.
One incident in “ Sporting Life,” played by Bland Holt
at Melbourne Boyal, leaves confusing financial impressions.
The hero’s best girl’s little brother has signed a P.N. for
£6OOO, and has forged the hero’s name on the back. The
bill gets into the hands of the Jew moneylender, from
whom the hero has borrowed £6OOO on the security of
“ the old ’ome.” Not being able to pay £12,000 this tall
and noble youth lets the mortgage be foreclosed and
acknowledges the forged signature as his own (loud cheers
and whistling). Then, to show how complete is his mag-
nanimity, he tears up the bill, thus destroying the only
security the Jew has got. And the Jew, instead of leap-
ing at him like a clawful vulture, takes it quietly, while
the gallery cheers again, and nearly whistles its head off.
At Melbourne City Court, the other day, a citizen
named Thomas Bergin was charged with stealing a watch
(on a very doubtful question of identity by a Chinaman),
and the Age described him as “once well-known as a
vocalist.” Whilst the accused was flatly contradicting
the Chow a brass band outside the Court struck up “ The
Dead March in Saul,” whereupon Bergin fell to the floor
in an epileptic fit. This was not what might have been ex-
pected of the old original Tom Bergin who used to sing
the part of Private Willis in “lolanthe,” and, as a matter
of fact, Tom had nothing to do with the case of watch-
stealing. He wrote to the Age stating that he was in no
way connected with the Bergin who took a fit when the
“ Dead March ” came to pass. But possibly he would have
joined his namesake in the fit if he had been present on
that melancholy occasion.
Munsey’s Magazine suggests that Ibsen is, among other
things, a bit of a plagiarist:—“New York has recently
enjoyed—we use this last word as did the lady who ‘ en-
joyed very poor health ’—an opportunity to hear ‘ When
The Dead Awake.’ This is described as the ‘ swan song’
of the septuagenarian Norwegian dramatist. No self-
respecting swan would approve such an application of the
term. It is a dreary and talky piece with marked resem-
blance to ‘ Amoureuse,’ which Bejane inflicted on us
during her American tour. Both plays portray a husband
and wife who have grown tired of each other. Neither is
dramatic, pleasant, or interesting, neither teaches any-
thing that has not been much more effectively taught
before.” Incidentally the Munsey critic remarks that the
hero of Ibsen’s latest is introduced to the audience as a
genius, but from the things he says and does upon the
stage one would take him for a drivelling idiot. The un-
enlightened have often made the same complaint. In
Ibsen’s plays the alleged brainy or brilliant people never
by any chance talk in a bright or impressive way.
“ Melb.” : “ Arrah-na-Pogue ” was first acted here with
Barry Sullivan, as Shaun the Post, at the Boyal. Mike
Feeney, Harwood ; Arrah, Mrs. Heir. Sullivan sang the
“ Wearing of the Green,” and it took well enough, but
the Pros were very sarcastic on him. He also sang the
“ Wonderful Crocodile,” and Long Tom Coffin, in “The
Pilot.” Jokes were cut on this, after he had gone, in
Whitworth’s farce of “ Catching a Conspirator,” wherein
Harwood acted Barry Sullivan. Bogers acted Mike
Feeney in Sydney. Williamson and Maggie Moore, after
their great London hit in “ Struck Oil,” were announced
for “ Arrah-na-Pogue,” but it was baulked through a dis-
pute between Manager Webster and Boucicault. Wil-
liamson gave up the engagement rather than spite Bouci-
cault by acting this piece. Countess Vacaresco, in her
newly-published book on “ Kings and Queens I Have
Met,” says she spent an evening at Windsor Castle with
Queen Victoria, Queen Alexandra, and Princess Beatrice.
Victoria then made a young Irish lady sing “Wearing of
the Green,” greatly to the disgust of Alexandra.
Mr. Caldwell, a calico-printer, and member of the House
of Commons, has been talking to his Mid-Lothian con-
stituents about the supposition that musical compositions
and trash of that sort deserve a protective copyright law.
“ Copyright,” says Caldwell “ is not a right of property
such as a man has to his watch. ... A great deal of fuss is
made about copyright music, as if it were some superior
article. The fact is, a boy in the street may string to-
gether a few bars of music, and register his manuscript
at Stationers’ Hall,” &c., &c. Caldwell’s sympathy with
the pirates of property in music doesn’t extend to pirates
of calico printing designs, of course. He holds any num-
ber of valuable patents and trades-marks, and a portion of
his bloated wealth is devoted to a fund for the protection
of his precious Caldwellian interests. The difference
between a new music copyright and a Caldwell calico
copyright is that the one is intended to protect the work
of a producer, whilst the other stringently protects the
purchase of a capitalist.
“ The Creole,” a little play produced at the Haymarket
(London) Theatre, the other week, gives Miss Alice Craw-
ford, late of Australia, a chance to score a big success
with her eloquent speaking tones. She plays the heroine
—Josephine—pleading, unseen, for reconciliation with her
ruffled Buonaparte. One critic wrote of Alice Crawford’s
effort: “ Although she acted with the door between her
and Napoleon until the last moment of the piece, she
spoke her lines always with feeling, and there was the
ring of sincerity in her voice. It was the one touch of
nature in the acting of the little piece.” The Bendigo
girl graduated for the stage as a recitress, and in the art
of elocuting unaffectedly she can give points to the ave-
rage clever actress, whose talk would sound purely theatri-
cal if she were required to speak a piece on a cold plat-
form. It was on the bare boards of Melbourne Town Hall
that Alice Crawford started her professional career.
“ Melb.” : The drop curtain once more appears at the
Bijou as the background for variety items. It is a finely
painted scene at Arden Forest, by Spong, with little
figures of Touchstone and Audrey. The furled-up drop
of the Princess’s is Venice by G. Gordon. He went there
to sketch for the scenery in the Bancroft production of
the “Merchant of Venice.” The long disused Theatre
Boyal drop is by Brunton. That of Her Majesty’s is a
view of the Melbourne Exhibition. There were four
beautiful drops by Hennings, in succession, at the Boyal:
“ Shakspeare’s House,” “ Bacchus and Ariadne,” “ Lake
Maggiore,” and “Thespians Leaving Deserted Greece."
The Opera House had Habbe’s copy of his drop at the
Sydney Victoria—a Gondola and Palace scene.

Bafferdy’s bub, an’ find de tarn Tagoes vos alretty dere
mit de harp an’ feedle. Dey vos stan’ ad de bar-door, an’
blay de ‘Serenata.’ Dey vos make me some mad, so I
go to de side-door, an’ I plow de 1 Sweede Leedle Sham-
rog ’ down mit de bassage. Dem hoogly, sly Tagoes den
blay de ‘ Vearin’ mid de Green.’ Dey vos make me
some more madder, so I go inside te tarn passage, an’
blay mit spiridoso de ‘ Boy-in-de-Vater.’ Ach, mein
Gott 1 Meesder Bafferdy he roosh oud, an’ trow de
sbiddoon ad me. ‘ Vot mit de ’ell you mean,’ he shout,
‘ makin’ all de tam noise.’ He roosh me mit de broom,
an’ I run —vot you gall it—leather-mit-’ell long de
streed. Dem tam Tagoes dey blay de ‘ Serenata ’ on de
nex’ gorner, an’ laff ad me. Yah, I vos an’ old Sherman
soldier, mein friendt, an’ I lofe your loovely goontrie
some. Dake mit mein advice, an’ alvays vote some vor
de snow-vite Hoostralia. Yah t ”
In America, “Mfr.” is short for “manu-
facturer ; in Australia, for “ malefactor.” Aus-
tralia adopted the abbreviation because it has
been taught by its importing press that the two
are the same person.

CHILBLAINS, which are a modified form of frost-bite, causing an itching,
irritable, inflamed condition of the hands and feet, are found to yield to
thorough applications of Zam-Buk. Persons weakened by disease and
those who have a rheumatic constitution are more liable to have chil-
blains ; and for such the use of Zam-Buk at
the outset of the season is recommended as a
precautionary step. The efficacy of Zam-Buk
in such cases is illustrated in the following :
Mr. William Bacon, of Yerner-street, Goulburn,
N.S. W., says:—“During the winter
we experience very cold, frosty
weather in Goulburn, and nurse the
fire very closely, and the inevitable
result is chilblains. I suffer con-
siderably in this respect, having
experienced chilblains on the feet,
hands, and ears. I tried many
ointments and salves in the hope
of driving out the irritation, but
with scant success. Last winter I
applied Zam-Buk, and with won-
derful results. The first applica-
tion drove out the irritation and soothed the chilblains considerably.”
