Treasure Island.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MOON islands out in the shimmering splendor
Of the Queen moon’s pathway of white
sphere fire,
Moon shadows touching a dream world tender !
O, Treasure Island ! O heart’s desire !
Were you but a dream with a dream’s surrender
Of light and laughter that must expire ?
A fate-flung phantom out in the distance,
A rose in the grayness of life’s gray chart; ?
Still I wrestle with fiends in a fool’s resistance.
O Treasure Island ! my restless heart
Craves thee again with a fierce insistence !
And we are drifting apart—apart!
Lord of the heights, which thy suns illumine !
Rifted with light that has blessed and banned,
Thou hast chained our spirits in pathways human,
And hedged our lives with unseen hand ;
Shall that which has fashioned man and woman
Hear us, and pity and understand ?
O Treasure Island ! shall Time’s dividing
Quench the white lustre of moons gone by ?
Is there no land of a great abiding
For beacons that beckoned from lip and eye ?
No love-stars still in the heavens riding
When the hills are dust and the seas are dry?
Yet who shall say ! while the dead are sleeping
And fettered souls in the distance fret,
If this be the end of our night of weeping !
We dream it may be, and yet! and yet !
If one should waken with pulses leaping
For glad remembrance—and one forget!
Nay, it is written, the pale years fleeing
Like a train of ghosts to the far away,
Bear all our treasures beyond our seeing,
And the earth-heart yawns for the senseless clay
That has loved and suffered and spent its being
Like the swift, sweet breath of a summer day !
But Treasure Island, my heart is yearning,
For life’s full measure, and it is o’er ;
And I am hungered and sick with learning
The weary wisdom of world-old lore.
This is the end ! God ! the slow sands turning,
And then the darkness—for evermore !
tas.
SPHINE.
Brown Brethren.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f HE money that I had intended to leave
to the South Sea Mission Fund will go
towards the Home for Decayed Australian
Editors. I have seen my Brown Brethren.
w * * *
For 20 years past on the island of Aneityum, in
the New Hebrides, a missionary and his wife have
been working strenuously to convert the natives.
At one time, before these apostles arrived,
Aneityum supported a population of over 8000.
That was in the wicked old days of tribal wars
and cannibalism. The latest census shows a
population of 490, or thereabouts. They have a
sullen, obsolete Presbyterian look, and they wear
short trousers that do not fit them. The joy of
living has gone out of their bones. I draw no
inference. I merely give the facts as they were
given to me by the wife of the missionary. She
is a Scotchwoman of the type that is firmly con-
vinced—at odd times when it thinks of things
profane—that Robert Burns wrote Shakspeare.
“ But do you never take a holiday, Madam ?”
“ I was in Scotland a year or two ago, but my
old friends were dead, or had gone away, or had
forgotten me. And so I was glad to get Home
again. ”
Home was this forlorn southernmost Island of
the New Hebrides, with its dwindling population
of black Presbyterians in short pants.
“ And are you never dissatisfied ? ”
“ Sometimes. But we have our work to do.”
I thought this a fair example of cheerful uncon-
scious martyrdom until I saw the sallow-faced,
lank-haired, quinine-loaded Catholic missionaries
in Port Vila and in Rica Sura. Mere wisps of
men, but sublime enthusiasts.
I talked with missionaries of every denomina-
tion —Roman Catholics, Presbyterians, Wes-
leyans. They all agreed in the statement that
the adult native has the kind of soul that cannot
be saved to any profitable extent. A promise that
he will not eat his brother too frequently, and will
attend Mass regularly, is all that the R.C. mis-
sionaries expect from him. The Anglicans teach
him to sing the hymns of Bishop Heber and other
sacred writers. To hear him under the sweltering
Solomon Island sun chanting, in his plaintive
voice, “ Greenland’s Icy Mountains ” is a pleasure
that would be highly appreciated by Mr. W. S.
Gilbert. The Presbyterians and Wesleyans per-
suade him to add a few inches to the length of his
lava-lava, and to keep the Church well supplied
with arrowroot, to be sold in England at pious
bazaars.
* * * •
“We have to catch them young,” is what all the
missionaries say. The grown-up generation will
not give up its secret faith in little Jew-faced
wooden gods that make fine weather and good
fishing when earnestly appealed to, or assaulted.
It is also satisfied with its own kind of devils—
beings of grim humor, who fall upon you from the
tops of trees, and bite pieces out of your shoulder
if you have been a sinner. The Christian Deity
who “lives alonga hell” does not seem to it to be an
improvement. And somehow there appears to be
(to me at any rate) a show of reason on the part of
these benighted Eeathens. If I lived on a small
strip of beach, with a dark, mysterious, sodden
jungle behind me, I think it quite likely that I
would find some religious solace in worshipping
these little crazy grotesque timber gods. They
look so natural and so suitable to the surround-
ings.
* * * •
In most of the islands the natives believe in a
Good Spirit and an Evil Spirit. The former
means well in a vague, general way, but is lazy
and does not trouble himself much about human
affairs; therefore he is neither feared nor loved.
The idea of loving a god is, indeed, inconceivable
by the untutored native mind. The Evil Spirit is
worshipped, because he is übiquitous and takes
an active, practical interest in the everyday lives
and occupations of men. He must be propitiated,
or he will blight the plantations or spoil the fish-
ing. The offering most acceptable to him is
something that was alive and is dead. Ur. Wilson,
the Anglican Bishop of Melanesia, informed me
that he once saw a hundred heads in a canoe.
They were brought by a war-party, and were to
be placed in the Canoe House as votive offerings
to the Fish God, to induce that sullen deity to be
liberal in the matter of fish.
* * * *
But the real basis of indigenous Island Religion
is Ghost-worship. All native ghosts are malevo-
lent. A man while in the flesh may be a cheerful
creature, full of the milk of human kindness, but
when he turns into a ghost his nature changes for
the worse, not by degrees, but immediately. He
is then at war with the living, and capable of any
crime. Jealousy seems to be the real cause of this
sudden change of character. Ghosts have a dim,
gloomy existence, which is merely a shadow of
their former life. They fish and fight and culti-
vate plantations, wherein yams and taro grow;
but the fighting is unreal and ineffective, the fish
they catch and the yams and taro they grow are
shadows without substance. Hence their envy of
the living and desire to injure them. It is only
by ceaseless vigilance and regular propitiation
that the living are able to hold their own at all.
This will perhaps explain why the first white men
who landed on the Islands were attacked by the
natives with so much ferocity. The natives
believed them to be ghosts.
Here and there, scattered amongst the Solo-
mons and the Floridas, are little islands upon
which the natives will not live, or even set foot.
These islands are the peculiar property of the
ghosts. On them they have their plantations and
their villages. If a native lands upon one of these
sacred spots he dies soon afterwards, or something
evil happens to him.
The Anglican missionaries made a brave attempt
to knock the bottom out of this superstition by
establishing their headquarters on Scioto, one of
the most celebrated of these ghost islands. I was
there, and saw a pretty little church, but no con-
gregation. In the cemetery adjoining, I counted
thirty-three graves. Here are buried five white
women, two wives of missionaries amongst them.
The other graves contain the remains of native
teachers and converts. In the opinion of the
neighboring heathen this place is now a more
terrible ghost island than it was before. Possibly
the existence of an evil-smelling swamp behind
the little hill upon which the mission houses are
built may have something to do with the sinister
reputation of Scioto. But then there are swamps
behind almost every little hill in every little island
in the Solomons and the Floridas. Some ad-
ditional explanation seems necessary. If I had to
live in those parts I would not make my camp
upon a ghost island.
* * * »
There are secret societies—called Tamate in the
Solomons, Matabalo in the Floridas. The mem-
bers of these societies are chiefs and priests. On
certain occasions they dress themselves up as
ghosts—according to the native idea of what
ghosts look like. They wear hats twelve feet
Eigh and made of grass, and rush through the
village shaking bags filled with human bones.
When this occurs the village is said to be closed.
The inhabitants keep within their houses, and
close their eyes and tremble. If one of them
should, by unlucky chance, behold this weird,
tall-hatted procession, he will die within the
year—unless he can show good cause to the con-
trary in pigs or other currency.
* * * *
I asked a leading missionary what, in his
opinion, the result would be if, after being con-
verted, the natives of the islands were left
entirely to themselves for a generation or two.
He replied that he believed they would evolve a
sort of mongrel Christianity of their own, as the
Abyssinians did, but on a lower scale. And every
island would have its own home-made religion,
which would be the only true and genuine creed—
and would see that it was adopted by every other
island, smaller than itself, or know the reason
why. That is the native idea of conversion.
The same missionary, who has lived for years in
the Islands, informed me that in his talks with
the natives he heard, at times, vague references
made to spirits that were neither devils nor ghosts,
but independent beings who interfere little, if at
all, with the affairs of men. He seemed to think
that in these vague references he had discovered
traces of a higher and more ancient, but almost
obsolete, religion. But he could never get clear
replies upon the subject, probably because the
natives have no longer any clear ideas themselves
with regard to it.
The chiefs are the last to come into the fold
when the inhabitants of an island, or a village,
are converted. There is no such thing as heredi-
tary rank in the Solomons, but the Richest Men
are chiefs by courtesy. The following diagram
will, when explained, show the reason why the
Rich do not give a warm welcome to the mission-
aries :
The above may be imagined to be the Village Club
House divided into compartments. All males
who have attained a certain age are entitled to sit
and eat in the first compartment. They must pay
before being allowed to eat in the next compart-
ment, and pay again—the fee increasing as they
ascend —when they wish to enter the one next to
that, and so on. The fees are paid in pigs. It
costs over 100 pigs to get into the last
and most select compartment. A man seldom
arrives there until he is old, and has
amassed wealth, and the Wisdom which con-
sists of keeping Things As They Are. The
top compartment is, in fact, the native House of
Lords, or Legislative Council, with this additional
advantage —it collects the admission fees from the
compartments below it. When the missionaries
got a firm grip on a village, they started to level
downwards, and insisted on the pig-proud mem-
bers of the Upper Chamber eating their way
down from compartment to compartment until
they found themselves messing with the common,
pigless proletariat. This is why the chiefs are
not warm admirers of the new religion. At pre-
sent, in some mission villages, even the women
are allowed to eat with the men—and the sky does
not fall, or the ghosts or devils devour them, both
of which results would most assuredly have fol-
lowed such a sacrilege a few years ago.
These children of Nature have no idea of Love
as we understand it. No real children of Nature
have, for that matter. One of the hardest tasks
the missionaries had with their male converts was
to make them believe that women should be loved
for their own sake, and courted as equals. They—
the male natives—considered this a sort of white
man’a madness. There is no romance among
them. When a young man wants a wife he says
to her father, “ How much?” If the price is not
too high he pays it, and takes away the goods.
That ends the matter. In the island of Ngela
women were, for some reason or other—l think
because the inhabitants used to believe in a Female
Creator—so exceptionally well thought of, and so
highly valued, that only the most prosperous young
men could afford to buy wives. The price of a chief’s
daughter was, I think, fifty strings of shell money,
the price of the daughter of a commoner thirty
strings. The young men complained to Bishop
Wilson and the Resident Commissioner, and a
meeting of leading elders was called. The result
was that the prices were reduced about one-half.
When the bishop was leaving the island not one
of the unmarried women came down to the beach
to bid him good-bye. They resented bitterly the
lowering of their value.
I asked little Captain Burns, who used to con-
vey labor to Queensland, what he thought of his
brown brethren. “There used to be £2O a head
in the brutes,” he replied, “but there’s nothing
in them now.”
I believe Captain Burns to h© a good judge of
human nature.
Viotob

vain.

A Gassy Tale.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
A CHEMIST once, in times long past,
By bottles quite surrounded,
With thought and labor hard and
vast,
And calculations deep, at last
A temp’ranee drink compounded.
It was a most delicious brew,
It fizzed and madly spluttered.
The gases from it rushed and flew.
The foam leaped up and rolled and blew.
“ Ah-ha ! ” the chemist muttered.
“ At last—at last, my fortune’s made ! ”
Triumphant, grave, and placid
A smile upon his features played,
As round his den he slowly strayed
And corked up all his acid.
“ The Methodists of Sabbath schools
Who gaze at things bucolic—
Who sit and giggle on long stools,
And play round games with lots of rules,
And shun drinks alcoholic,
“ In summer to the beach they’ll fly
With this good tipple laden ;
And arm-in-arm they’ll wander by,
And sip and kiss, and sip and sigh—
Each youthlet with his maiden.”
Six months elapse. A joyous band
In vans, with horses snorting,
Comes rolling by thro’ sun and sand
(“ Will you behave !” “Let go my hand !”)
For all are busy courting.
Beneath the seats —all laid in rows
The cordials are reclining ;
There, too, the sandwiches repose.
Delicious smells salute the nose
And make one think of dining.
At last upon the sunlit strand
They-come, and, after eating,
They grab each other by the hand,
And screech. (’Tis hard to understand
The joy they take in bleating.)
Fiercer and fiercer grows the glare,
The sea has scarce a ripple.
Out in the sun —no shade is there —
The picnickers—too hot to care —
Lap up the gassy tipple.
All silent are the simple ones
Out in the sunshine lying.
Hark ! How the echo rolls and runs !
Is it the boom of distant gun 3
The nation’s foes defying ?
But what is this ? The people race
And scream—they seem excited.
Terror is writ on every face.
What are these bangs that shake the place ?
And why are all affrighted ?
“ Look out t” they yell—the flying crowd
As from a bomb that’s loaded,
Rushing they leave a deacon proud,
A fizz—a flash —and clear and loud—
A bang I—he has exploded!
High in the blue a top-hat soars,
A hymn-book follows after;
And all around are screams and roars.
A bang !—a splash—a bang ! —a pause—
And dreadful madman’s laughter.
Louder and louder grows the noise,
Like forts in action roaring ;
And, from the heavens, bits of boys
Burnt clothes and sandwiches and toys
In cataracts come pouring.
'Tis done. Of all those happy souls
Among the wreckage busy
One delves among the sausage rolls.
(They’re dangerous—those glowing co ils.
Look —bang I Alas, where is he?)
His saintly coat-tails lash the sun,
(O ! poor inflated mortal!)
His toil down here on earth is done,
For, like a shell from out a gun,
He flies through Heaven’s portal.

KODAK.
State Kids.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
LOOKING back over long years (I am
28), the main thing I seem to remember
about the places I have Worked are State
kids. Somehow, they seem to have been with me
wherever I have sojourned.
The first place I remember seeing them was at
Picton. I was serving my time to the building
trade, and we were doing a Government job there.
Hard by was an undersized dairy farm, run by a
hungry couple and four hungrier State kids.
When the woman wasn’t hunting them about the
house, the man would be belting them in the pad-
dock, Swede turnips were cheap that year; it
was there I learnt to hate the smell of them. Pour
times a day the big copper set up outside foamed
over with them. We thought it was for the pigs,
till we saw the nippers lined up round it with
plates. After a week we moved our camp to the
other side of the job. It took a very strong wind
to put us off our feed there. On the leeward side,
even on calm days, the smell used to follow us
about like a dog. After a month of it, the fore-
man reckoned he’d write to the Government; said
the kids used to wake him up jabbering Scandin-
avian in their and he couldn’t stand it.
When the man wasi/t chasing the kids about, he
would be loafing around in our way, and explain-
ing “Dairy farmin’ don’t pay ; an’ as fer the
kids, there’s nothin’ in ’em at five bob er week.”
The next lot I remember were at Guildford,
near GraViville. Everyone round there had them
as a sort of spec. They used to take them round
to each other’s places to compare. The top-
notcher, I remember, was tongue-tied. My sister
was district visitor, and used to give two days’
notice ahead, so we were pretty popular. The
people had little go-carts for them, and it was
wonderful what loads they would haul from the
station. A neighbor of ours held the record ; his
team of three State kids brought a full-sized
spring-mattress two miles over soft roads in an
hour. Some new arrivals burst the show up.
Another neighbor, whose yard joined theirs, used
to attend the hen sales at Parramatta every week.
As usual, he had chained his State kid up to the
kennel to keep him out of mischief, after letting
the dog loose to mind the yard. The new-comer’s
wife happened to see him there (it was the top-
notcher, by the way), and, when her husband c*- >e
home, made him report to the police. They . d
an inquiry, and nearly all of us lost our nippers
over it. The new-comers had to leave the district
after that.
The next place I remember them I was fixing
up a public school near Campbelltown. The mis-
tress had four, to keep the attendance up.
One was fair, one was red-headed ; the next was a
brindle (quarter Chow, quarter Cingalese), and the
fourth a nigger. He was the best of the lot; the
others were born liars and thieves. About once a
month they used to bolt for Sydney, with some
neighbor’s horse and sulky. The mistress would
wire down and generally pick them up in Liver-
pool lock-up. They ruined her buggy horse one
day when she left them at home by themselves.
They invented a rope bridle and bag saddle, and
double-banked it round the paddock in turns till
it fell down. It was never any good afterwards.
I saw another with a little Welshman on the
Bellinger. Any one who has ever worked for a
Welsh cocky will know the sort he was. The kid
was just big enough to hit back when I was there.
The Welshman was only game to belt him when
he had him in a corner, or stun him with a stone
on the ear when the boy wasn’t looking. At
last the boy fired the humpy when his boss was
away ploughing in the corn-paddock, and hit out
for the Macleay.
• * * *
Where I am Working now there are three
beauties. Two are brothers, and have an unre-
liable pedigree, whilst the third, who has not long
come, has been on the State all his life. Appa-
rently no one owns him or knows anything about
him; he doesn’t even know his own age. He
had been with some storekeepers on the South
Coast before we got him, and was rather superior
when he came. The other two soon knocked that
out of him. His name, he told them haughtily,
was Bertie Norman Cowper Salt. Cowper took
their fancy ; so he was promptly branded Cowpup.
I don’t mind confessing that I was deceived in
Cowpup. On first acquaintance I pitied him, he
seemed so miserable. He is squat and thick set,
and fat with an oily flabbiness. The large, watery
grey eyes are always upturned, as if in mute pro-
test, to match his little buttony nose; his mouth
resembles a gash in a pound of steak. No one has
ever seen him walk or run. He has two paces—a
slow, crab-crawl when at work, and a peculiar
three-cornered sidle when approaching anything
he is doubtful of, or when he is being called up for
an explanation. Obviously he hates the latter
process; it always ends the same way.
“ Jim-mee, come here ! ” screams his missus.
“Yes, ma’am,” squeals Cowpup sidling up at
an angle of 45 degrees, which leaves him a line of
retreat through the kitchen door.
“ What d’ye mean by not scrubbing the kitchen
when I told you ? ” she demands wrathfully.
“I did scrub it,” Cowpup bleats, sidling off to
60 degrees as she advances towards him.
“You did not! ” she screams excitedly. “ Here,
don’t run away like that. Come here ” —with her
right hand behind her back.
Cowpup breathes hard, and sidles in a wide
circle, aiming at the mangle in the corner, behind
which he just fit 3. His dull eyes light up with a
steely gleam ; he keeps them fixed on his assailant
after the manner of a skilful boxer.
She closes, waving a piece of old cart trace.
Cowpup, with a shrill yelp, dives under her arm
to his haven. The trace rises and falls whilst he
moans loudly. After many strokes, she discovers
she has been belting the leg of the mangle.
Savage as a meat-axe, she grabs him by one arm,
and drags at him strongly to get him in the open.
Cowpup, snorting and yelping alternately, clings
to the other leg of the mangle.
“ Come out where I can get a fair hit at you,”
she shouts. “You’d better come out. ”
Cowpup thinks otherwise; when, his strength
failing, he is detached and brought out, the missus
has only breath left for a couple of hits. He
sidles away into the yard, yelling. I noticed him
smiling greasily to himself ten minutes after,
though, by the wood-heap.
At first, Cowpup took a great interest in me,
and used to bring me all the bent nails and rusty
screws he could find. I foolishly gave him three-
pence at the end of the week, after which he
followed me about with doglike devotion—for more
threepences. He used to whine hymns to me as I
worked, and mournfully told me his troubles.
One of them is about a girl. The other boys, he
tells me, are utterly bad ; “they swear, ’n’ won’t
sing hymns, ner nuthin.”
One day last week he confided to me that if
he had sixpence more he could buy a dog, the
“champeen” of the district. I took the hint,
being curious, and he brought it home.
“ Don’t look much,” I told him. “ Looks more
like a meat hound. What’s he good for ? ”
“’E’s the best dorg roun’ ’ere,” objected
Cowpup, tearfully. “’E’ll sneak roun’ the end
of a lorg when ’e sees a ’are asleep, ’n’ shake th’
inside out uv it in less’n three minutes.”
A few days later I heard a terrible combat
being waged down in the bush. A little later
Cowpup sidled up to me in great distress.
“I wuz jes tryin’ me new dorg on ole
Tiger,” he sobbed, “’n’ Tiger’s gorn V killed
him.”
He left weeping, to get a spade for the burial,
and a few stones when Tiger should return.
The missus’s girl insulted Cowpup the other
day, and out him deeply.
“ D’ye know what that boy’ll be w’en ’e grows
up ? ’E’ll be a Wesleen parson.”
I tried Cowpup for a week with me; thought he
would come in handy for running errands. The
first day I only missed nails; the second he had
advanced to bits and moulding planes, so there
wasn’t any third. Instead I borrowed the old
man’s stockwhip, and just as he was sidling away
with my hammer I got him on the rear with the
old Burragorang cut that chops three initials in
one flick.
For the first and, I believe, the only time in his
life Cowpup ran. I ran after him. We were
both about done when we struck the scrub-edge.
He kept wide of me after that, but has been
throwing out hints from a safe distance (40 yards)
that he only wants a shilling to buy a mouth-
organ. The missus has stopped him jumping on
the skillion-iron in the early morning, so he is
getting desperate. He is in disgrace at present,
having been caught boiling settings of high-class
Minorca eggs in the kettle for private consump-
tion. I must knock off writing now; I can hear
Cowpup poking about near my room, and I have
left 2s. 6d. on the mantelpiece.
